# ENDOCRINE SOCIETY

##### Hormone Science to Health

The Endocrine Society is the world's largest, oldest, and most active organization working to advance the clinical practice of endocrinology and hormone research. Founded in 1916, the Society now has more than 18,000 global members across a range of disciplines. The Society has earned an international reputation for excellence in the quality of its peer-reviewed journals, educational resources, meetings, and programs that improve public health through the practice and science of endocrinology.

##### OVERVIEW

The Endocrine Self-Assessment Program (ESAP™) is a self-study curriculum aimed at physicians seeking initial certification or recertification in endocrinology, program directors interested in a testing and training instrument, and clinicians simply wanting a self-assessment and a broad review of endocrinology. ESAP 2015 is available in both print and online formats. It consists of 120 brand-new multiple-choice questions in all areas of endocrinology, diabetes, and metabolism. There is extensive discussion of each correct answer, a comprehensive syllabus, and references. ESAP is updated annually with new questions and new syllabus materials.

ESAP is composed of two key components: the initial online interactive module and the printed book. Upon purchase, learners will initially receive access to the online module. To use ESAP as a true self-assessment tool, learners are strongly encouraged to complete the online interactive self-assessment module first before continuing self-study with the printed book; the online module may be accessed at education.endocrine.org.

##### LEARNING OBJECTIVES

ESAP 2015 will allow learners to assess their knowledge of all aspects of endocrinology, diabetes, and metabolism.

Upon completion of this educational activity, learners will be able to:

• Recognize clinical manifestations of endocrine and metabolic disorders and select among current options for diagnosis, management, and therapy.

• Identify risk factors for endocrine and metabolic disorders and develop strategies for prevention.

- Evaluate endocrine and metabolic manifestations of systemic disorders.

- Use existing resources pertaining to clinical guidelines and treatment recommendations for endocrine and related metabolic disorders to guide diagnosis and treatment.

##### TARGET AUDIENCE

ESAP is a self-study curriculum aimed at physicians seeking initial certification or recertification in endocrinology, program directors interested in a testing and training instrument, and clinicians simply wanting a self-assessment and a broad review of endocrinology.

##### STATEMENT OF INDEPENDENCE

As a provider of CME accredited by the Accreditation Council for Continuing Medical Education, the Endocrine Society has a policy of ensuring that the content and quality of this educational activity are balanced, independent,

objective, and scientifically rigorous. The scientific content of this activity was developed under the supervision of the Endocrine Society's Self-Assessment Committee.

##### DISCLOSURE POLICY

The faculty, committee members, and staff who are in position to control the content of this activity are required to disclose to the Endocrine Society and to learners any relevant financial relationship(s) of the individual or spouse/partner that have occurred within the last 12 months with any commercial interest(s) whose products or services are related to the CME content. Financial relationships are defined by remuneration in any amount from the commercial interest(s) in the form of grants; research support; consulting fees; salary; ownership interest (eg, stocks, stock options, or ownership interest excluding diversified mutual funds); honoraria or other payments for participation in speakers' bureaus, advisory boards, or boards of directors; or other financial benefits. The intent of this disclosure is not to prevent CME planners with relevant financial relationships from planning or delivering content, but rather to provide learners with information that allows them to make their own judgments of whether these financial relationships may have influenced the educational activity with regard to exposition or conclusion. The Endocrine Society has reviewed all disclosures and resolved or managed all identified conflicts of interest, as applicable.

The faculty reported the following relevant financial relationship(s) during the content development process for this activity: Shehzad S. Basaria, MD, is a consultant for Eli Lilly. Kathryn McCrystal Dahir, MD, is on the advisory board for Alexion. Thomas W. Donner, MD, is a study site principle investigator for Novo Nordisk. James W. Findling, MD, is an investigator/consultant for Concept Therapeutics and Novartis. Pamela U. Freda, MD, is an investigator for Novartis and Ipsen, sits on the advisory board for Pfizer, and is a content reviewer for UpToDate. Duke University receives funds from Merck and Amylin (now BMS/AstraZeneca) for research performed by Jennifer Green, MD. These funds support a portion of her salary. Dr. Green has also received honoraria from Merck for serving as a consultant, honoraria from BioScientifica for editorial activities, and has received publication support services from Boehringer Ingelheim. Suzanne M. Jan de Beur, MD, is on the data safety monitoring board for KKP Pharma, Inc, and TissueGene, Inc. She is a consultant for KKP Pharma and sanofi-aventis. She is also an investigator for Eli Lilly. Adrian V. Vella, MD, is a consultant for sanofi-aventis and Genentech and receives grant support from Novartis and BioKier. Corrine Welt, M

is a writer for UpToDate and a consultant for Astra Zeneca and Takeda.

# Laboratory Reference Ranges

Reference ranges vary among laboratories. The listed reference ranges should be used when interpreting laboratory values presented in ESAP™. Conventional units are listed first with SI units in parentheses.

## ENDOCRINE SELF-ASSESSMENT PROGRAM 2015

Part I

A nephrologist colleague asks you for an opinion regarding deterioration of renal function in a 42-year-old woman with primary hyperaldosteronism diagnosed 2 months earlier. Her baseline estimated glomerular filtration rate of 98 mL/min per 1.73 m² decreased to 69 mL/min per 1.73 m² after initiating spironolactone, 50 mg twice daily. A repeated measurement 1 month later showed an estimated glomerular filtration rate of 58 mL/min per 1.73 m². Her blood pressure has been well controlled on spironolactone alone, and her other antihypertensive medications have been discontinued. She has a normal potassium concentration without supplementation.

She had initially presented with a 6-year history of intermittent hypokalemia and hypertension requiring 3 to 4 antihypertensive medications. Initial studies showed a morning serum aldosterone concentration of 19 ng/dL (527.1 pmol/L) and plasma renin activity of 0.2 ng/mL per h while taking lisinopril, hydrochlorothiazide, and amlodipine. Measurements of urinary aldosterone and sodium excretion were 18 µg/24 h (49.9 nmol/d) and 202 mEq/24 h (202 nmol/d), respectively. Abdominal CT showed normal adrenal glands. Bilateral adrenal venous sampling without ACTH stimulation demonstrated the following:

Which of the following should you recommend?

A. Substitution of eplerenone for spironolactone at the same dosage

B. Laparoscopic right adrenalectomy

C. Another adrenal venous sampling procedure with ACTH stimulation

D. Magnetic resonance angiography of the renal arteries

E. No change in current treatment

2 A 52-year-old man is referred to the endocrine clinic for management of hypogonadism. Idiopathic hypogonadism was diagnosed 4 years ago when he had presented with fatigue, sleepiness, and reduced libido. He recalls having “low” testosterone levels at that time. He was started on transdermal testosterone gel, 7.5 g daily, which has improved his symptoms.

At the time of his diagnosis, the patient was morbidly obese with a BMI of 42.0 kg/m². He also had difficult-to-control hypertension and impaired fasting glucose. Approximately 18 months ago, he underwent gastric bypass surgery that resulted in clinically significant weight loss. His fasting glucose is now normal and he takes only 1 antihypertensive medication. He continues to apply testosterone gel at the same dosage. The patient has no history of pituitary or testicular disease. He is married with 2 children and does not desire to extend his family. He would like to know if his weight loss has any impact on the management of his hypogonadism.

On physical examination, his pulse rate is 78 beats/min and blood pressure is 128/72 mm Hg. Weight is 219 lb (99.5 kg), and height is 69 in (175.3 cm) (BMI = 32.3 kg/m²). There is no gynecomastia. Findings on cardiac examination are normal. He has white stretch marks on his abdomen. Findings on genital examination are normal with both testes measuring 20 mL.

Laboratory test results (ordered by his primary care physician):

Total testosterone = 591 ng/dL (20.5 nmol/L) (on testosterone gel) Prostate-specific antigen = 1.4 ng/mL (1.4  $ \mu $g/L)

Which of the following is the best course of action in the care of this patient?

A. Decrease the dosage of testosterone gel to 5 g daily and check testosterone levels in 3 months

B. Continue current treatment

C. Discontinue testosterone therapy and check testosterone levels in 3 months

D. Replace testosterone with tamoxifen, 10 mg twice daily

E. Replace testosterone with intramuscular hCG, 1500 units 3 times a week

3 A 67-year-old man with no notable medical history is referred for evaluation of symptoms suspicious for hypoglycemia. These symptoms occur when the patient avoids calories for a period exceeding 2 to 3 hours and are characterized by sweating, tremor, and tachycardia. He has been waking up 3 times each night to consume a snack, and he carries orange juice with him constantly. These symptoms have been present to some degree for about 4 years, but have worsened in the past 6 months. Over the 4-year period, he has gained approximately 100 lb (45.5 kg). His current BMI is 43 kg/m².

On the basis of this history, his primary care physician ordered CT of the abdomen, which suggested the presence of a discrete mass in the head of the pancreas. At the time of his initial evaluation, the patient was asymptomatic after a 2-hour fast. However, the following laboratory values were obtained:

 $$ \mathrm{Glucose}=50\mathrm{mg}/\mathrm{dL}(2.78\mathrm{mmol/L}) $$ 

 $$ \mathrm{Proinsulin}=1587\mathrm{pg}/\mathrm{mL}(180\mathrm{pmol/L}) $$ 

 $$  C-peptide=14.3\ ng/mL(4.7nmol/L) $$ 

Insulin antibodies, undetectable

 $$  Insulin=112\ \mu IU/mL\ (777.8\ pmol/L) $$ 

Sulfonylurea screen (including repaglinide and glimepiride), negative

Review of the abdominal CT performed at an outside institution documents the presence of a 2.4-cm enhancing mass in the head of the pancreas with central inhomogeneity and areas of low attenuation. The appearance is consistent with an islet-cell tumor of the pancreas. No other masses are seen, but the mass is encasing the pancreatic duct. The pancreas and the liver are also characterized by extensive fatty infiltration.

Which of the following describes appropriate management?

A. Initiate chemotherapy

B. Recommend a weight-loss program before considering surgery

C. Refer the patient for ethanol ablation under the guidance of endoscopic ultrasonography

D. Ask an experienced endocrine surgeon to enucleate the tumor

E. Initiate verapamil

4 A 19-year-old man presents to establish care. Type 1 diabetes mellitus was diagnosed at age 7 years. He reports a history of poor diabetes control, with hemoglobin  $ A_{1c} $ levels in the 8% to 10% (0.080 to 0.100) range for the past several years, but he has recently refocused his efforts with therapy adherence and has noted some improvement in his glycemic control. He currently uses an insulin pump. He has retinopathy, but no other diabetes complications. He is the only member of his family with type 1 diabetes, but several maternal relatives have type 2 diabetes. His family history is also notable for heart disease in his paternal grandparents who are in their 60s, as well as hypertension and obesity in both parents. He does not consume alcohol, but smokes 1 pack of cigarettes per day.

On physical examination, height is 68 in (172.7 cm) and weight is 266 lb (120.9 kg) (BMI = 40.4 kg/m $ ^{2} $). Pulse rate is 99 beats/min, and blood pressure is 125/80 mm Hg. Physical examination findings are normal except for obesity and acanthosis nigricans.

Laboratory test results (fasting):

Hemoglobin  $ A_{lc}=7.8\% $ (0.078)

Aspartate aminotransferase = 43 U/L (0.7 μkat/L)

Total cholesterol = 224 mg/dL (5.80 mmol/L)

Alanine aminotransferase = 95 U/L (1.6 μkat/L)

HDL cholesterol = 26 mg/dL (0.67 mmol/L)

LDL cholesterol = 186 mg/dL (4.82 mmol/L)

Creatinine = 0.77 mg/dL (68.1 μmol/L)

Triglycerides = 229 mg/dL (2.59 mmol/L)

TSH = 2.83 mIU/L

In addition to smoking cessation, initiation of which of the following would lead to the greatest reduction in his lifetime cardiovascular risk?

A. Intensified glucose control

B. Fibrate

C. Statin

D. Niacin

E. Low-fat diet

A 38-year-old woman presents with amenorrhea. She experienced menarche at age 15 years and had regular menstrual cycles. Five years ago, she underwent treatment for infertility using in vitro fertilization, but was unsuccessful. At that time, her husband had a normal semen analysis. She began to miss occasional menses 1 year ago and developed amenorrhea 9 months ago. She has not flashed several times per week, along with vaginal dryness. She does not exercise excessively and she has no galactorrhea, hyperandrogenism, headaches, or peripheral vision loss. Her medical history is remarkable for hypothyroidism treated with levothyroxine. Family history is notable for her mother undergoing menopause at age 45 to 50 years. Her mother's height is 64 in (162.6 cm), and her father's height is 69 in (175.3 cm).

On physical examination, blood pressure is 108/68 mm Hg. Height is 59 in (149.9 cm), and weight is 118 lb (53.6 kg) (BMI = 23.8 kg/m²). She has no acne, hirsutism, or vitiligo. Findings on head and neck examination are normal. Her thyroid gland is small at approximately 10 g. Pelvic examination reveals no masses.

Laboratory test results:

FSH = 23.6 mIU/mL (23.6 IU/L)

Estradiol = <20 pg/mL (<73.4 pmol/L)

β-hCG = <6 mIU/mL (<6 IU/L)

Adrenal antibodies, undetectable

Prolactin = 5.7 ng/mL (0.25 nmol/L)

FMR1 repeat length, normal

TSH = 0.99 mIU/L

A. Thyroid antibody measurement

B. Pelvic ultrasonography

C. Pituitary-directed MRI

D. Karyotype analysis

E. IGF-1 measurement

6 A 35-year-old man with a history of end-stage renal disease secondary to hemolytic uremic syndrome is referred to you for evaluation of hypercalcemia. He required hemodialysis by the age of 27 years, and by age 31 years, he had undergone a successful renal transplant from a living, unrelated donor. Despite treatment with appropriate phosphorus binders and vitamin D analogues before transplant, his PTH concentrations were generally greater than 500 pg/mL (>500 ng/L).

In recent months, laboratory studies have documented progressive hypercalcemia and a persistently elevated PTH level (most recent results displayed below). Although not typically recommended in transplant recipients who develop hypercalcemia, the nephrologist caring for him ordered a parathyroid scan (see image). All 4 parathyroid glands were identified (arrows), with greater uptake in both superior glands.

7 A 49-year-old man with poorly controlled type 2 diabetes mellitus of 18 years' duration, hyperlipidemia, gastroesophageal reflux disease, and obesity presents for steadily worsening nausea and abdominal pain over the last 72 hours without any obvious exacerbating factors. The pain is primarily epigastric in location and seems worse with eating, although it has become more constant in the past day. He has started to run a low-grade fever (101°F [38.3°C]) and has had 2 episodes of nonbloody bilious emesis in the past 24 hours.

He currently follows an insulin therapy regimen with insulin detemir, 60 units twice daily, and insulin aspart, 10 units before each meal. He takes simvastatin for elevated LDL cholesterol diagnosed 2 years ago and lansoprazole to manage gastroesophageal reflux disease. He typically consumes 2 to 3 alcoholic beverages per day but has not done so since the onset of abdominal pain. In recent weeks, he has been frequently consuming fast-food meals and, as in the past, has occasionally been nonadherent with his insulin therapy. His blood glucose values over the past 72 hours ranged from 220 to 350 mg/dL (12.21 to 19.43 mmol/L).

On physical examination, he has central obesity, but no evidence of abdominal striae. Weight is 190 lb (86.4 kg), and height is 65 in (165.1 cm) (BMI = 31.6 kg/m²). Blood pressure is 135/95 mm Hg, and pulse rate is 76 beats/min. He has acanthosis nigricans. The thyroid gland is normal without nodularity. Abdominal examination reveals marked epigastric and right upper quadrant tenderness without rebound but with some guarding. Bowel sounds are diminished. No masses are identified. He has a rash consisting of reddish-yellow lesions on the backs of both legs, on the buttocks, and on his knees. The retinal blood vessels show a creamy appearance on fundoscopic examination.

Laboratory test results:

Hemoglobin  $ A_{lc} = 13.5\% $ (0.135)

Creatinine = 0.8 mg/dL (70.7 μmol/L)

Sodium = 128 mEq/L (128 mmol/L)

Potassium = 4.5 mEq/L (4.5 mmol/L)

White blood cell count = 28,500/μL (25.5 × 10⁹/L) with a left shift TSH = 1.5 mIU/L

Which of the following is the most likely cause of his current symptoms?

A. Gastroparesis

B. Pancreatitis

C. Ulcerative colitis

D. Cholecystitis

E. Mesenteric ischemia

8 You are asked to see a 45-year-old man with a history of type 2 diabetes mellitus for routine follow-up. He feels well and has no acute concerns. You have been encouraging his efforts at lifestyle changes with the hope of achieving some weight loss. His weight of 244 lb (110.9 kg) has been stable over the past year. He is otherwise healthy. He comments that he was relieved when he recently heard on the news that lifestyle changes do not really help people with type 2 diabetes, and he asks whether he must continue his activity program of walking 30 minutes to 5 times per week.

He currently takes metformin, 2000 mg daily; simvastatin, 20 mg daily; and lisinopril, 20 mg daily.

On physical examination, he appears well. Height is 70 in (177.8 cm), and weight is 244 lb (110.9 kg) (BMI = 35 kg/m $ ^{2} $). Blood pressure is 138/80 mm Hg.

Laboratory test results:

Hemoglobin  $ A_{lc} = 6.8\% $ (0.068)

Serum creatinine = 1.0 mg/dL (88.4 μmol/L)

In this patient, intensive lifestyle changes (goals of weight loss of &gt;7% and 175 minutes of moderate-intensity activity per week) would be expected to decrease which of the following?

A. Risk of nonfatal stroke

B. Risk of nonfatal cardiac events

C. Risk of fatal cardiac events

D. Risk of developing diabetic microvascular disease

E. Need for insulin therapy

A 65-year-old man with a 30-year history of type 1 diabetes mellitus returns to see you for a routine follow-up appointment. He was referred to you for management 2 years ago after recurrent episodes of hypoglycemia, some of which had been severe and required the assistance of others. At that time, his insulin therapy was changed to a regimen including once-daily dosing of insulin glargine and multiple daily doses of insulin aspart with meals.

The patient keeps a careful blood glucose log in which he records 8 to 10 fingerstick blood glucose readings daily. His glycemic control stabilized following the change in his insulin regimen. Although he periodically experiences blood glucose levels between 60 and 70 mg/dL (3.33-3.89 mmol/L) in the very early morning, his ability to sense hypoglycemia has improved. The patient's most recent episode of severe hypoglycemia occurred 18 months ago during the nighttime while he was asleep. His diabetes is complicated by microalbuminuria, peripheral neuropathy, and retinopathy that has required photocoagulation but not resulted in clinically significant vision impairment.

Although he has never experienced a driving mishap related to hypoglycemia, he voluntarily stopped driving when he was referred to you for care. He now asks whether it would be advisable for him to resume operation of a motor vehicle for personal use.

On physical examination, the patient is alert and appears well. Blood pressure is 127/64 mm Hg, and pulse rate is 62 beats/min. Height is 67.5 in (171.5 cm), and weight is 162 lb (73.6 kg) (BMI = 25.0 kg/m²). He has evidence of previous photocoagulation on undilated fundoscopic examination, but visual fields are grossly intact. He has no foot deformities, foot lesions, or gait abnormalities. He has a few areas of sensory loss on monofilament testing of the plantar surfaces of both feet, but proprioception is preserved.

Laboratory test results:

Hemoglobin  $ A_{1c}=7.6\% $ (0.076)

Sodium = 141 mEq/L (141 mmol/L)

Glomerular filtration rate = 70.2 mL/min per 1.73 m²

Creatinine = 1.3 mg/dL (114.9 μmol/L)

TSH = 1.85 mIU/L

In addition to reducing the patient's dosage of insulin glargine, which of the following should you recommend now?

A. Continued avoidance of driving given his high risk of hypoglycemia-related mishaps

B. Continued avoidance of driving given his diabetes-related complications

C. Resumption of driving after education to minimize risk of hypoglycemia while driving

D. Transition to insulin pump therapy before resumption of driving

E. Use of a continuous glucose-monitoring device during all periods of driving

10 During an appointment with her primary care physician, a 28-year-old woman reports that she is pregnant. Your assistance in interpreting her thyroid hormone levels is requested. She has regular menstrual cycles every 28 to 30 days, and her last menstrual period was 6 weeks ago. A home urine test kit for pregnancy has confirmed a positive result. She delivered a healthy baby boy 30 months ago following an uncomplicated pregnancy. Thirteen months ago, she had a miscarriage at 7 weeks' gestation. The patient reports tiredness that has been present for 3 months, but has worsened over the past 2 weeks. She has also developed mild nausea, mainly in the morning over the last week. She reports a family history of rheumatoid arthritis and pernicious anemia in her maternal grandmother and Hashimoto thyroiditis in her mother.

11 A 60-year-old woman seeks an evaluation for low bone density. She has a history of rheumatoid arthritis and has been treated with prednisone, 10 mg daily, for the last 6 months. She has no personal history of fractures, has no family history of fractures or osteoporosis, and has not experienced height loss. She takes calcium supplements, 1200 mg daily (in divided doses), and vitamin D, 2000 IU daily. She walks 30 minutes a day, engages in resistance training 3 times per week, and remains active.

On physical examination, weight is 131 lb (59.5 kg) and height is 65 in (165.1 cm) (BMI = 21.8 kg/m $ ^2 $). Blood pressure is 110/72 mm Hg, and heart rate is 72 beats/min. Her examination findings are normal.

Results from a complete blood cell count and comprehensive metabolic panel are within the reference range. Her vitamin D concentration is 32 ng/mL (79.9 nmol/L).

DXA scan reveals osteopenia with a femoral neck T score of -2.2 and a lumbar spine T score of -2.3.

Which of the following is the best next treatment step?

A. Increase daily calcium supplementation to 2400 mg

B. Increase daily vitamin D supplementation to 4000 IU

C. Start raloxifene

D. Start denosumab

E. Start risedronate

12 A 72-year-old man with no history of diabetes mellitus is confused after being involved in a car crash and is brought to the emergency department. A fingerstick glucose measurement is 45 mg/dL (2.50 mmol/L).

Venous blood is drawn, which confirms the presence of a low venous glucose concentration (39 mg/dL [2.16 mmol/L]). The hypoglycemia is treated with intravenous glucose and the patient's confusion resolves. Abdominal CT is subsequently performed and is remarkable for the presence of multiple vascular hepatic metastases, the largest of which is 2.3 cm in diameter. His pancreas is well visualized and normal in appearance. The right kidney is remarkable for the presence of a 4-cm, complex cystic mass whose appearance is strongly suggestive of renal cell carcinoma, occupying the upper pole. As far as can be ascertained, the patient was asymptomatic before this presentation, and his weight has been stable.

27 A 23-year-old woman comes to see you for evaluation of a 7-month history of amenorrhea. She stopped hormonal contraception 7 months ago because she thought taking hormones for many years was a bad idea. Her history is notable for irregular menses, every 3 months, starting at menarche at age 12 years. She states that she has never had an eating disorder. In high school, she participated in 3 sports, including track and cross-country. She continued running in college and began training for a half-marathon 8 months ago. She currently runs 30 to 40 miles per week. Acne appeared at age 13 years. Hormonal contraception was prescribed at age 14 years, which alleviated the acne and caused regular withdrawal bleeding, but her acne has returned since she stopped the medication. She has no symptoms of hirsutism, alopecia, galactorrhea, hypothyroidism, or hyperthyroidism. She is not sexually active.

On physical examination, blood pressure is 100/58 mm Hg. Height is 64 in (162.6 cm), and weight is 122 lb (54.5 kg) (BMI = 20.9 kg/m²). Acne lesions are present on her face and back. Her Ferriman-Gallwey score is 2, and there is no acanthosis nigricans or violaceous striae. Her thyroid gland is approximately 20 g and has no nodules. There is no galactorrhea. Pelvic examination reveals a normal uterus and ovaries.

Laboratory test results:

TSH = 1.01 mIU/L

Prolactin = 5.3 ng/mL (0.23 nmol/L)

Estradiol = 43 pg/mL (157.9 pmol/L)

Testosterone = 76 ng/dL (2.6 nmol/L)

FSH = 5.4 mIU/mL (5.4 IU/L)

DHEA-S = 150 µg/dL (4.1 µmol/L)

LH = 12.8 mIU/mL (12.8 IU/L)

17-Hydroxyprogesterone (8 AM) = 57 ng/dL (1.7 nmol/L)

Which of the following is the most likely cause of the amenorrhea and acne?

A. Polycystic ovary syndrome

B. Postpill amenorrhea

C. Hypothalamic amenorrhea

D. Pregnancy

E. Primary ovarian insufficiency

28 A cardiologist has referred a 25-year-old man with a 1-month history of worsening palpitation,

development of tremor, heat intolerance, anxiety, and 11-lb (5-kg) weight loss. In the last 2 years, he has developed paroxysmal atrial fibrillation that has been successfully

treated with electrical cardioversion. A 54-year-old woman is referred to you for assistance with type 1 diabetes management. Diabetes was diagnosed at age 44 years. Her current insulin regimen includes insulin glargine, 10 units in the morning and 5 units in the evening. She boluses insulin aspart before meals using an insulin-to-carbohydrate ratio of 1 unit for each 15 g of carbohydrate and a sensitivity factor of 1 unit to lower her glucose 100 mg/dL (5.55 mmol/L) to a target level of 100 mg/dL (5.55 mmol/L). She participates in aerobic exercises for 60 to 90 minutes daily and weight training for 30 minutes 3 times per week. Her diet is rich in fruits, vegetables, lean meats, and mostly high-fiber carbohydrates. She feels comfortable with carbohydrate counting when eating at home, but has more difficulty accurately assessing the carbohydrate content of foods eaten at restaurants. She measures her glucose level 4 to 8 times daily. Her downloaded glucose meter shows glucose concentrations that range from 42 to 493 mg/dL (2.33 to 27.36 mmol/L) with a mean of 168 mg/dL (9.32 mmol/L) and a standard deviation of 76 mg/dL (4.22 mmol/L). Glucose levels are least variable after eating a similar daily breakfast and are quite variable throughout the rest of the day. She senses that her glucose levels are low when they fall below 60 mg/dL (3.33 mmol/L) because she feels “different.” She has not had any episodes of severe hypoglycemia requiring assistance. She takes atorvastatin, 10 mg daily, for hyperlipidemia and ramipril, 2.5 mg daily, for hypertension.

On physical examination, her blood pressure is 114/69 mm Hg and pulse rate is 72 beats/min. Height is 69 in (175.3 cm), and weight is 133 lb (60.5 kg) (BMI = 19.6 kg/m $ ^{2} $). Findings on eye, cardiovascular, abdominal, and peripheral nerve examinations are normal.

Laboratory test results:

Hemoglobin  $ A_{1c}=7.6\% $ (0.076)

LDL cholesterol = 52 mg/dL (1.35 mmol/L)

Urinary albumin = 7 mg/g creatinine

The patient asks whether insulin pump therapy would assist with her diabetes control.

Which of the following outcomes would be most likely if this patient's insulin regimen were changed to insulin pump therapy?

A. Reduced hemoglobin  $ A_{1c} $

B. Reduced flexibility in the timing of her meals

C. Reduced risk of diabetic ketoacidosis

D. Increased total daily insulin dose requirement

E. Increased frequency of severe hypoglycemia

34 A 64-year-old man with a 15-year history of type 2 diabetes mellitus returns to see you for an appointment to reassess management of his peripheral neuropathy. He has managed his diabetes with the use of an insulin pump for the past 8 years and has had no notable problems related to the use of the device. He monitors his fingerstick blood glucose levels 6 to 8 times daily and keeping a carefully documented log of the results. Review of his log reveals that he has occasional nonsevere hypoglycemia during periods of prolonged exertion. He also has blood glucose readings above 200 mg/dL (11.10 mmol/L) 2 to 3 hours after his evening meal several times each week.

The patient's diabetes is complicated by painful peripheral neuropathy. He describes stinging and burning sensations in both lower legs and feet. The discomfort is interfering with his sleep because it is more noticeable at night and is aggravated by touch from sheets or blankets. Three months ago, he began pregabalin at a dosage of 50 mg orally 3 times daily, which he increased to 100 mg 3 times daily after 1 week. He has tolerated the medication well and reports good adherence to therapy; however, he has had no noticeable improvement in his neuropathic symptoms.

On physical examination, blood pressure is 122/80 mm Hg, and pulse rate is 80 beats/min. Height is 74 in (188 cm), and weight is 208 lb (94.5 kg) (BMI = 26.7 kg/m $ ^{2} $). He has no obvious foot deformities or foot ulcerations, and his ability to sense a monofilament in his feet is well preserved. His Achilles reflexes are intact bilaterally.

Laboratory test results:

Hemoglobin  $ A_{1c}=7.2\% $ (0.072)

Glomerular filtration rate = 78.6 mL/min per 1.73 m²

TSH = 1.40 mIU/L

You review with the patient appropriate strategies to reduce the risk of exercise-induced hypoglycemia, including use of temporarily lower basal rates or suspension of insulin infusion via the pump.

Which of the following is the best therapeutic intervention for this patient's diabetic neuropathic pain?

A. Adjust pump settings to intensify glycemic control

B. Add duloxetine to his pregabalin therapy

C. Add amitriptyline to his pregabalin therapy

D. Change from pregabalin to duloxetine therapy

E. Change from pregabalin to gabapentin therapy

A hand x-ray shows fused epiphyses. Findings on cardiac echocardiogram are normal.

36 A 44-year-old man presents to your office for a second opinion regarding low bone density and possible hypogonadism. He recently sustained a T12 spinal compression fracture while stepping off a curb, and low bone density has been confirmed on DXA (both lumbar spine and femoral neck T scores are less than -2.5). He has a history of hypothyroidism treated with levothyroxine. He notes increasing fatigue over the past several years, along with decreased libido and decreased early morning erections.

On physical examination, weight is 185 lb (84.1 kg) and height is 70 in (177.8 cm) (BMI = 26.5 kg/m²). Blood pressure is 134/78 mm Hg, and heart rate is 82 beats/min. He has no kyphosis, but he does have tenderness to spinal palpation near the T12 vertebra. His testicular size is within normal limits but the testes are soft. There is no synovitis or joint swelling. Reflexes are normal, as are the rest of the examination findings.

Laboratory test results (sample drawn while fasting):

Hemoglobin = 14 g/dL (140 g/L)

TSH = 3.2 mIU/L

Free  $ T_4 = 0.9 $ ng/dL (11.6 pmol/L)

FSH = 3.2 mIU/mL (3.2 IU/L)

LH = 2.1 mIU/mL (2.1 IU/L)

25-Hydroxyvitamin D = 34 ng/mL (84.9 nmol/L)

Intact PTH = 42 pg/mL (42 ng/L)

Calcium = 9.4 mg/dL (2.4 mmol/L)

Serum glucose = 176 mg/dL (9.8 mmol/L)

Total testosterone (8 AM) = 180 ng/dL (6.1 nmol/L)

Which of the following is the best next step?

A. Measure free $T_{3}$

B. Measure hemoglobin $A_{1c}$

C. Measure transferrin saturation and ferritin concentration

D. Measure free testosterone

E. Perform pituitary-directed MRI

38 A 19-year-old man is transitioning from the pediatric endocrinology clinic. In infancy, he was admitted to the hospital for new-onset hyperglycemia. The patient's father has a history of diabetes mellitus that is well controlled with oral hypoglycemic therapy.

40 A 73-year-old man is referred by an endocrine surgeon before a scheduled adrenalectomy. He has a 10-year history of hypertension that had initially been well controlled with hydrochlorothiazide. of 75% insulin lispro protamine suspension/25% insulin lispro injection (75/25 insulin) with his morning and evening meals. 56 You are asked to evaluate a 43-year-old woman after thyroidectomy to determine the best next step in her care. Her TSH level at the time was normal. She was followed up with annual examinations and had no further intervention.

Repeated ultrasonography verifies that nodules on both lobes are larger. She undergoes elective total thyroidectomy. The pathologic findings for the larger nodules are benign, documenting nodular hyperplasia. There are no abnormal lymph nodes noted on inspection, and findings on histologic examination of 2 nodes included in the surgical specimen are negative. 67 A 38-year-old biologic male patient presents to discuss transgender hormone therapy. The patient reports a lifelong feeling of having been born in the wrong body, and she struggled with anxiety and depression in her teenage years and 20s. She states that she drank alcohol heavily in the past, but quit completely after she had an episode of pancreatitis in her late 20s. She struggles with her weight. She recently sought psychological evaluation, and her psychologist concluded that she has gender dysphoria. At today's appointment, the patient requests estrogen therapy, and she presents a letter from her psychologist confirming the diagnosis of gender dysphoria and stating that the patient is ready to transition.

On physical examination, height is 71 in (180.3 cm) and weight is 215 lb (97.7 kg) (BMI = 30.0 kg/m $ ^{2} $). Blood pressure is 136/86 mm Hg, and pulse rate is 72 beats/min. Physical examination findings are otherwise normal.

68 A 59-year-old woman presents for assistance with diabetes control. She was first noted to have diabetes-range fasting hyperglycemia on screening laboratory tests at age 19 years. This followed a 15-lb (6.8 kg) weight gain during her freshman year at college. She was placed on a low-concentrated sweets diet, and she successfully lost weight and was not followed up closely thereafter. At age 29 years, hyperglycemia was again not on routine blood work. Her BMI was 20.8 kg/m². Referral to an endocrinologist led to a presumptive diagnosis of type 1 diabetes, and she was prescribed a multiple daily injection insulin regimen.

Over the past 10 years, her hemoglobin  $ A_{1c} $ levels have ranged from 6.3% to 7.2% (0.063 to 0.072). There are no known diabetes complications. She eats a low-carbohydrate, low-fat diet and exercises for 90 minutes daily. Stakes bupropion for depression and alendronate for osteoporosis.

Her current insulin regimen consists of insulin glargine, 13 units at bedtime, and insulin aspart, 4 to 6 units before meals. She has been measuring glucose levels 0 to 2 times daily since she started insulin therapy, mostly fasting and at bedtime. Her 3-month glucose average is 132 mg/dL (7.33 mmol/L) with a range of 28 to 250 mg/d (1.55 to 13.88 mmol/L). Hypoglycemia occurs mostly overnight. She has a history of hypoglycemia unawareness and has had 3 instances of severe hypoglycemia since her diagnosis.

Her family history is notable for insulin-requiring diabetes mellitus diagnosed in her father at age 20 years and diabetes mellitus diagnosed in her paternal grandmother in her 20s.

On physical examination, height is 65 in (165.1 cm) and weight is 118 lb (53.6 kg) (BMI = 19.6 kg/m $ ^{2} $). Her blood pressure is 117/55 mm Hg, and pulse rate is 66 beats/min. She has normal findings on funduscopic examination, normal peripheral pulses, normal findings on peripheral nerve examination, and no acanthosis nigricans.

Hemoglobin  $ A_{lc} = 7.0\% $ (0.070)

Creatinine = 0.78 mg/dL (70.0 μmol/L)

LDL cholesterol = 135 mg/dL (3.49 mmol/L)

Urinary albumin = 6.8 mg/g creatinine

Triglycerides = 82 mg/dL (0.93 mmol/L)

C-peptide (fasting) = 0.63 ng/mL (0.21 nmol/L)

Total cholesterol = 276 mg/dL (7.15 mmol/L)

HDL cholesterol = 125 mg/dL (3.24 mmol/L)

Glucose (fasting) = 154 mg/dL (8.55 mmol/L)

A. Instructing her in carbohydrate counting to reduce glucose vanity.

B. Increasing the frequency of glucose monitoring to 4 times daily and when she has any symptoms of hypoglycemia.

C. Reducing her insulin aspart dose at the meal before she exercises.

D. Referring her for genetic testing.

E. Switching to insulin pump therapy.

A 39-year-old woman with Hashimoto hypothyroidism returns for follow-up after adjustment of the levothyroxine dosage. Hypothyroidism was diagnosed at age 31 years in the postpartum period. She had a high titer of TPO antibodies and did not recover normal thyroid function. She ultimately developed a serum TSH concentration of 70 mIU/L before levothyroxine was prescribed at a dosage of 112 mcg daily. Her levothyroxine dosage was gradually increased to 150 mcg daily. She had been clinically and biochemically euthyroid while taking this dosage for several years, with her serum TSH concentration being between 1.0 and 3.0 mIU/L.

Over the past 6 months, she has unintentionally lost 8.8 lb (4 kg). She describes being more irritable, having modest heat intolerance, and having a diminished exercise capacity. You have had her return 3 times in the last 6 months for thyroid hormone testing and on each occasion her TSH was suppressed. Thus, her levothyroxine dosage was reduced to 50 mcg daily (her current dosage).

On physical examination, height is 65.5 in (166.4 cm) and weight is 153 lb (69.5 kg) (BMI = 25.1 kg/m $ ^{2} $). Blood pressure is 124/74 mm Hg, and heart rate is 88 beats/min. Her thyroid gland is slightly enlarged and has an irregular texture. She has brisk patellar reflexes.

Thyroid function test results:

Serum TSH = 0.01 mIU/L

Free  $ T_{4} $ = 2.1 ng/dL (27.0 pmol/L)

She confirms that levothyroxine is her only prescription medication. She never takes extra tablets. You inspect the tablets in her levothyroxine pill container and find no error in the prescription. She takes no over-the-counter supplements. Your review of her history does not uncover any reason accounting for her decreased levothyroxine requirement.

Over several subsequent visits, and after further levothyroxine dosage adjustments, you discontinue her levothyroxine. Thyroid function testing 6 weeks later shows a TSH concentration of 2.1 mIU/L and a free T₄ concentration of 1.4 ng/dL (18.0 pmol/L). With this last blood test, you also measure a serum total T₃ level, a serum thyroglobulin concentration, and a spot urinary iodine concentration. Her total T₃ level is 120 ng/dL (1.8 nmol/L), her thyroglobulin concentration is 43 ng/mL (43 μg/L), and her random urinary iodine excretion is 177 μg/L (reference range, 28-544 μg/L).

#### Which of the following best explains euthyroidism after the discontinuation of levothyroxine in this patient?

A. Surreptitious ingestion of liothyronine

B. Ingestion of an over-the-counter supplement containing tiratricol

C. Ingestion of large amounts of nutritional supplements containing iodine

D. Autoimmune thyroid disease with current predominance of thyrotropin receptor-stimulating antibodies

E. Surreptitious ingestion of desiccated porcine thyroid extract

91 A 55-year-old woman seeks your opinion for apparent episodes of hypoglycemia. She underwent Rouxen-Y gastric bypass 8 years ago for the treatment of medically complicated obesity. At that time, she had hypertension and type 2 diabetes mellitus treated with 2 oral medications. The patient experienced diabetes remission within weeks of the procedure. Her preoperative weight was 300 lb (136.4 kg). The procedure and subsequent weight loss were uncomplicated, with nadir weight being reached approximately 14 months after surgery. She has regained some weight, and at today's visit she weighs 240 lb (109.1 kg).

For the past 2 years, she has been troubled by symptoms of tachycardia, tremor, and diaphoresis that typically occur within an hour or two of food ingestion. These symptoms have progressively worsened. The patient reports that on one occasion, she required assistance after an episode of presyncope and altered mentation. A home reflectance glucose meter regularly reports glucose readings between 30 and 50 mg/dL (1.66 and 2.78 mmol/L) at the time symptoms occur. 103 for follow-up. The patient was first seen 7 months ago when he had presented with reduced libido and fatigue. His morning total testosterone concentration was 240 ng/dL (8.33 nmol/L). ## ENDOCRINE SELF-ASSESSMENT PROGRAM 2015

Part II

#### ANSWER: E) No change in current treatment

The use of a mineralocorticoid receptor antagonist such as spironolactone or eplerenone improves blood pressure and potassium homeostasis in patients with primary hyperaldosteronism. It is less well appreciated that there is a decline in glomerular filtration rate in the early phase after treatment initiation. This observation has been explained by aldosterone-induced hyperfiltration, which is abolished with either surgery or mineralocorticoid receptor blockade. The observed glomerular hyperfiltration in patients with primary hyperaldosteronism is associated with decreased intrarenal vascular resistance. The decline in glomerular filtration rate after treatment is significantly lower in patients with low pretreatment plasma aldosterone levels. Mineralocorticoid receptor antagonists can also decrease serum sodium levels indicating the reversal of aldosterone-induced sodium-retinic effects and hypervolemia. Urinary albumin excretion decreases in patients who are treated with mineralocorticoid receptor antagonists.

Despite this initial decrease in glomerular filtration rate in patients treated for primary aldosteronism, the decline in renal function appears to be reversible and long-term renal function stabilizes. Therefore, no change in the patient's current treatment is indicated (Answer E). Substitution of eplerenone for spironolactone (Answer A) would not be expected to provide any additional benefit. In fact, on a milligram-for-milligram basis, higher dose of eplerenone are usually needed to adequately treat primary aldosteronism.

of eplerenone are usually needed to adequately treat primary. The adrenal venous sampling procedure in this patient provided good sampling as reflected by the elevated cortisol and epinephrine levels. Repeating the procedure with ACTH stimulation (Answer C) would not provide any new information. Although ACTH infusion is generally recommended during adrenal venous sampling, there is no compelling evidence that it greatly improves the diagnostic accuracy of the procedure. The findings here are clearly consistent with bilateral adrenal hyperplasia. The aldosterone-to-cortisol ratio in each adrenal vein is similar and both ratios are considerably greater than that of the inferior vena cava. Since there is less venous admixture in the right adrenal vein (directly off the inferior vena cava), it is not surprising to find higher steroid levels here. The epinephrine levels serve as another useful means to verify the integrity of adrenal venous effluent; however, the absolute measurements have no diagnostic utility. The epinephrine level in the inferior vena cava is usually very low. Consequently, a laparoscopic right adrenalectomy (Answer B) would not be indicated.

Since this woman had no evidence of secondary hyperaldosteronism that would be expected with renal arterial stenosis, magnetic resonance angiography of the renal arteries (Answer D) is not warranted. Moreover, the use of an ACE inhibitor such as lisinopril in a patient with renal artery stenosis would have caused very significant increases in plasma renin activity, as well as probable deterioration in renal function before the introduction of a mineralocorticoid receptor antagonist.

##### Educational Objective:

Identify the early and transient decrease in renal function that accompanies the treatment of primary aldosteronism

2 ANSWER: C) Discontinue testosterone therapy and check testosterone levels in 3 months and case series show that serum testosterone levels are lower in obese men. Data from epidemiological studies testicular disease) than in nonobese control patients. Although the exact mechanism behind this “functional”

androgen deficiency remains unclear, the posited mechanisms depend on the degree of obesity. For example, men with mild-to-moderate obesity (BMI <40 kg/m²) have low total testosterone levels (due to low serum sex hormone-binding globulin levels); however, their free testosterone levels are normal. To the contrary, in men who are morbidly obese, even free testosterone levels are low (presumably due to elevated circulating estradiol levels, although frankly elevated estradiol levels are rarely seen). Weight loss after gastric bypass surgery is associated with a significant increase in both total and free testosterone levels (Figure 1). Similarly, weight loss resulting from calorie restriction is also associated with increased total and free testosterone levels (Figure 2). Serum sex hormone-binding globulin levels also increase (due to a decrease in insulin resistance and resumption in its hepatic synthesis). This patient has experienced clinically significant weight loss, and his gonadal axis will most likely recover once exogenous testosterone therapy is discontinued. Hence, discontinuing testosterone therapy is the best next step in his management rather than subjecting him to lifelong testosterone therapy (thus, Answer C is correct and Answer B is incorrect).

3 ANSWER: C) Refer the patient for ethanol ablation under the guidance of endoscopic ultrasonography. A typical approach to insulinoma would be to consider enucleation and, in normal circumstances, Answer D would be correct. However, there are several unusual features of this patient's presentation. Typically, insulinoma does not cause an elevation in β-cell polypeptides that is as marked as that observed in this vignette and elevations of such magnitude should raise the possibility that hypoglycemia is caused by insulin antibodies. However, this possibility is excluded by undetectable insulin antibodies. There is a strong possibility that this insulinoma is malignant given its size, behavior, and inhomogeneous appearance. Their relatively slow growth often makes chemotherapy ineffective and in the absence of a tissue diagnosis, initiating chemotherapy (Answer A) is incorrect. The proximity to the main pancreatic duct and the location in the head strongly suggests that enucleation (Answer D) is not technically possible. Moreover, a Whipple procedure or any pancreatic procedure in the presence of fat infiltration places the patient at high risk of complications such as pancreatic fistula or anastomotic breakdown.

A staged approach was taken in this case, using intervention to decrease unregulated insulin secretion from the insulinoma and to permit weight loss before definitive surgery. Diazoxide and somatostatin were not viable options in this patient, primarily because of cost and, in the case of somatostatin, the patient was unwilling to sell inject 3 times daily. Given some previous success with ethanol ablation (Answer C), this approach was undertaken with subsequent short-term benefit in that the patient was able to fast for periods of 4 to 6 hours without clinically significant symptoms. He was also able to avoid nocturnal snacking and this led to some weight loss (~20 lb [9.1 kg]). Ethanol injection is performed under the guidance of endoscopic ultrasonography. Typically between 0.5 and 1.0 mL are injected and, especially for large tumors, more than one treatment session is required.

Weight loss (Answer B) may improve the ease with which pancreaticoduodenectomy proceeds and may decrease the risk of operative complications. However, impairing this patient's ability to eat and using caloric intake to treat or prevent hypoglycemia is not the best way forward.

Several case reports suggest that verapamil (Answer E) may help prevent hypoglycemia in patients with insulinoma. Many patients referred to tertiary care centers for the treatment of insulinoma have been prescribed this medication with poor results necessitating surgical treatment.

##### Educational Objective:

Evaluate management options for a large insulinoma in the head of the pancreas.

UpToDate Topic Review(s):

Insulinoma

ANSWER: C) Statin

Although he is young, this patient has several cardiovascular disease risk factors including type 1 diabetes, cigarette smoking, positive family history, and dyslipidemia (elevated LDL cholesterol and triglycerides, low HDL cholesterol). Obviously, cessation of smoking is a major modifiable risk factor (quitting before age 30 years is estimated to add approximately 10 years of life) and should be addressed, but this was not provided as an answer option. Weight control and increased physical activity should also be recommended; however, they are not sufficient by themselves to reduce cardiovascular risk, so additional therapy should be considered.

In the Diabetes Control and Complications Trial, intensified glucose control resulted in prolonged reductions of cardiovascular disease events (the so-called legacy effect). A total of 1441 participants with type 1 diabetes aged 13 to 39 years were randomly assigned to intensive glycemic control vs standard diabetes control for an average duration of 6.5 years. After completion of the intervention, all participants were offered intensive control and the clinical course of 93% of participants was followed for up to 12 more years. During the active study, the intensive group had a mean hemoglobin  $ A_{1c} $ level of 7.4% (0.074) compared with a mean hemoglobin  $ A_{1c} $ level of 9.1% (0.091) in the standard group. However, during the follow-up period, both groups achieved similar mean hemoglobin  $ A_{1c} $ levels of 8.0% (0.080) and 8.2% (0.082), respectively. Previous intensive treatment reduced the risk of cardiovascular disease events by 42% compared with risk in the control group. Thus, intensive glycemic control reduces cardiovascular disease. However, in this vignette, the patient's hemoglobin  $ A_{1c} $ level of 7.8% (0.078) is close to that attained by the intensive control group, and thus improving his glycemic control now (Answer A) is not likely to substantially reduce his cardiovascular disease risk.

Given his elevated LDL cholesterol, low HDL cholesterol, and elevated triglycerides, use of a lipid-modifying medication may be appropriate. Dyslipidemia in young adults tends to persist throughout life and is associated with increased risk of cardiovascular disease with aging. Statins have been well studied in a wide range of patients, and they reduce unfavorable cardiovascular outcomes in nearly all. Almost all guidelines recommend statin therapy for persons with LDL-cholesterol levels of 190 mg/dL or greater ( $ \geq $4.92 mmol/L), although there is some controversy as to the age at which treatment should start. However, numerous studies evaluating lifetime risk for cardiovascular disease demonstrate associations between elevated LDL cholesterol and lifetime risk. Although this patient has a relatively low 10-year risk, his lifetime risk is high. Recent guidelines suggest that individuals aged 21 years or older who have an LDL-cholesterol concentration of 190 mg/dL or greater ( $ \geq $4.92 mmol/L) should be treated with high-intensity statin therapy to reduce risk, and calculation of 10-year risk is not required. Although this patient is younger than 21 years and his LDL-cholesterol concentration is just under 190 mg/dL (4.92 mmol/L), it is reasonable to recommend statin therapy (Answer C), which, of the listed options, is the most likely to have the greatest influence on reducing his cardiovascular risk. According to a recent meta-analysis, even persons at low risk for cardiovascular disease benefit from statins, with a 38% reduction in risk of major vascular events even in patients with an estimated risk less than 5% over 5 years.

Fibrates (Answer B) would be expected to lower his triglycerides by up to 50%, raise his HDL cholesterol by up to 10%, but have minimal effects on his LDL-cholesterol level, and potentially even increase it. The effect of fibrates on reducing cardiovascular risk has been controversial, with some studies showing a benefit and others no longer showing a significant benefit. In general, patients with high triglycerides and low HDL-cholesterol levels are most likely to have reduced risk of cardiovascular events with fibrate therapy; however, fibrates have not been well studied in young populations and thus outcomes are unclear.

Niacin (Answer D) is a lipid-lowering agent that could be expected to reduce his triglyceride level by up to 50%, raise his HDL-cholesterol level by up to 30%, and lower his LDL-cholesterol level by up to 25%. However, niacin has not been well studied in primary prevention cohorts, and it has clinically significant adverse effects, including worsening of insulin resistance, which would limit enthusiasm for its use in this patient. Comparison of niacin monotherapy vs statin monotherapy suggests greater risk reduction with statin use.

Although initiation of a low-fat diet (Answer E) should definitely be recommended as part of lifestyle therapy and weight-reduction plans, no data demonstrate reduced risk of cardiovascular disease with diet alone. The

expected improvements in his dyslipidemia who has a long-term adherence. Thus, although he is young, he has a long-term adherence.

## 5 ANSWER: D) Karyotype analysis

In women with primary ovarian insufficiency (previously termed premature ovarian failure), it is important to determine the underlying cause. The most common causes, after excluding radiation exposure, chemotherapy or ovarian surgery, are autoimmune etiologies and abnormal karyotypes, with Turner syndrome being the most common cause of primary ovarian insufficiency. In addition, expansion of the CGG repeats in the 5' untranslated region of the FMR1 gene is common in this patient population. When the number of CGG repeats expands to greater than 200, the gene is methylated and silenced and Fragile X syndrome can result. Women who carry 5' 200 repeats are at risk for primary ovarian insufficiency. Therefore, all women with primary ovarian insufficiency should have assessment of FMR1 repeat length, measurement of adrenal antibodies (which are indicative of autoimmunity against steroid-secreting cells in the adrenal and ovary), and karyotype analysis (Answer D). The patient's karvotype was 45.X, which is diagnostic of Turner syndrome.

Patient's karyotype was 45,X, which is diagnosed as a tumor of the X chromosome.

Turner syndrome classically results from loss of an X chromosome, but it can be caused by loss of the shown of the X chromosome or by mosaic 45,X/46,XX; 45,X,i(Xq) consisting of 2 long arms of the X chromosome, 46,XY; or 46,X,del(Xp), among others. In women with Turner syndrome and a 45,X karyotype, the�otype can be quite variable as demonstrated by this patient. In the classic presentation, affected patients have a long arm of the X chromosome, but it can be caused by loss of the X chromosome or by mosaic 45,X/46,XX; 45,X,i(Xq) consisting of 2 long arms of the X chromosome, 46,XY; or 46,X,del(Xp), among others. In women with Turner syndrome and a 45,X karyotype, the

fetuses with Turner syndrome, the complement of primordial germ cells is normal up to 12 weeks' gesta

subsequently decreased. Therefore, it appears that follicle atresia occurs more rapidly than normal and can

complete loss of oocytes before the onset of puberty. Ovaries may become fibrotic and appear as streak

sonography (Answer B) would not be helpful in this patient who had normal menstrual function and m

w has small ovaries on the basis of her high FSH level.

nts with Turner syndrome should be followed up for common medical complications. A baseline program is required for diagnosis of congenital left-sided cardiac defects and follow-up every 5 years with program or MRI is needed to evaluate for aortic dilatation. Other assessments should include hearing loss for renal anomalies, along with close follow-up for obesity, hypertension, hyperlipidemia, type 2 mellitus, celiac disease, osteoporosis, and liver function abnormalities. Hypothyroidism is also common syndrome, as it is in women with other causes of primary ovarian insufficiency. However, adrenal not thyroid antibodies (Answer A), are indicative of concomitant ovarian autoimmunity, and assess the need for the treatment of the patients who has already presented with hypothyroidism.

A gonadotropin-secreting pituitary adenoma in a woman of reproductive age may secrete functional FSH. The presentation would consist of ovarian enlargement from multiple follicle development (as seen with FSH stimulation during ovarian hyperstimulation for fertility treatment), elevated estradiol from multiple follicle development, and, possibly, slightly elevated prolactin due to pituitary stalk compression. This patient has high FSH, low estradiol, and normal prolactin; therefore, a pituitary-directed MRI (Answer C) is not warranted.

Short stature in women with Turner syndrome is related to loss of the SHOX gene on the short arm of the X chromosome. There is no need to workup a central cause of short stature with an IGF-1 measurement (Answer E) in a patient with an elevated FSH level and low estradiol level because the cause of amenorrhea is primary to the ovary.

##### Educational Objective:

Order a complete workup that includes karyotype analysis in a patient with primary ovarian insufficiency.

UpToDate Topic Review(s):

##### ANSWER: D) Subtotal parathyroidectomy

More than 95% of patients with chronic kidney disease develop evidence of secondary hyperparathyroidism by the time hemodialysis is needed. A number of potential physiologic explanations account for the rise in PTH, including the diminution in phosphate excretion with resultant hyperphosphatemia, which directly increases PTH secretion; a reduction in renal 1α-hydroxylase activity resulting in decreased 1,25-dihydroxyvitamin D levels and increased PTH levels; and the indirect effects of uremia blunting the actions of PTH itself. These stimuli result in both polyclonal glandular hyperplasia, as well as monoclonal expansion with autonomous function and nonsuppressible PTH secretion. In some instances, all 4 glands are involved (as observed on this patient's parathyroid scan), whereas a single, solitary gland may be the source of hyperparathyroidism in other patients.

The rise in PTH levels in the setting of renal failure is referred to as secondary hyperparathyroidism. Because the elevated PTH results from dysregulation of mineral and vitamin D metabolism, the term secondary hyperparathyroidism is used to describe the scenario of elevated PTH, elevated (or upper-normal) phosphorus, and normal (or below normal) calcium levels. In contrast, tertiary hyperparathyroidism reflects autonomous PTH secretion with resultant hypercalcemia and hypophosphatemia, as is the case for this patient. Tertiary hyperparathyroidism is usually seen in patients with previous secondary hyperparathyroidism after renal transplant, but it can also occur in X-linked hypophosphatemic rickets, oncogenic osteomalacia and longstanding hypercalciuria.

In tertiary hyperparathyroidism, as in primary hyperparathyroidism, hypercalcemia can cause end-organ damage. Thus, protection of the renal transplant is of utmost importance. Although there are no universally accepted indications for intervention, general guidelines for management of patients with renal transplant and tertiary hyperparathyroidism have been proposed. Intervention should be considered in the presence of hypercalcemia (1.0 mg/dL above the reference range) persisting more than 3 to 12 months after transplant; hypercalciuria, nephrolithiasis, and/or nephrocalcinosis; renal phosphate wasting; low or declining bone mineral density; pruritus; and parathyroid glands estimated to weigh more than 500 mg on ultrasonography. Treatment of this patient's tertiary hyperparathyroidism is warranted because of the degree of hypercalcemia. Thus, simply continued observation (Answer A) is incorrect. Although parathyroid ultrasonography (Answer B) would be helpful to estimate gland size and extent of disease, it is not needed to determine the therapeutic course. Similarly, ultrasonography of the transplanted kidney (Answer C) to look for evidence of nephrocalcinosis in the transplant would be helpful, but is not needed to determine management.

Four-gland parathyroidectomy with autotransplant and subtotal parathyroidectomy (ie, resection of 3 to 3.5 glands) are both effective in correcting hypercalcemia associated with tertiary hyperparathyroidism after renal transplant with similar risk of recurrence requiring reoperation and hypoparathyroidism. No randomized controlled trials have compared these 2 procedures head to head. Four-gland parathyroidectomy (Answer E) renders patients

hypoparathyroid and is not recommended because this procedure is associated with the development of adyn bone disease, osteomalacia, impaired bone healing, and the need for long-term high-dosage calcium and vitamin D supplementation. Subtotal parathyroidectomy (Answer D), often done with measurement of intraoperative levels, is the best next step in this patient's care. A third option, which has been used in some patients with hyperparathyroidism, includes 4-gland parathyroidectomy with autotransplant of some parathyroid tissue, and the forearm or the neck. This approach is undertaken in other conditions such as primary hyperparathyroid associated with multiple endocrine neoplasia type 1, when all 4 glands are involved.

As an alternative to parathyroidectomy, some studies have evaluated the effect of cinacalcet on calcium regulation and allograft function in patients with tertiary hyperparathyroidism. Cinacalcet, although not appropriate for use in tertiary hyperparathyroidism, may reduce serum calcium levels and most reports show no deleterious effects on renal function. For patients with an unacceptably high surgical risk, use of a calcium receptor mine such as cinacalcet may be an option.

##### Educational Objective:

Evaluate persistent hyperparathyroidism after renal transplant and review indications for parathyroidectomy.

Management of primary hyperparathyroidism

Kidney Disease: Improving Global Outcomes (KDIGO) CKD-MBD Work Group. KDIGO clinical practice guideline for the diagnosis, evaluation, prevention and treatment of chronic kidney disease-mineral and bone disorder (CKD-MBD). Kidney Int. 2009;76(Suppl 113):S1-S130.

Uhlig K, Berns JS, Kestenbaum B, et al. KDOQI US commentary on the 2009 KDIGO Clinical Practice Guideline for the Diagnosis, Evaluation, and Treatment of CKD-Mineral and Bone Disorder (CKD-MBD). Am J Kidney Dis. 2010;55(5):773-799.

7 ANSWER: B) Pancreatitis

Acute pancreatitis (Answer B) is characterized by inflammation of the pancreas with extension of the inflammatory response to other abdominal sites. The severity of acute pancreatitis is variable—mild-to-moderate abdominal pain, pancreatic necrosis, and even catastrophic abdominal inflammation and death. The cause incidence of acute pancreatitis ranges from 4.9 to 35 per 100,000 persons, and it is a leading cause of hospitalization in the United States. Numerous factors predispose an individual to acute pancreatitis including any disease/obstruction, alcohol, hypercalcemia, and hypertriglyceridemia (as was the case in this vignette). Uncontrolled hyperglycemia in the setting of diabetes promotes the risk of hypertriglyceridemia-induced creatitis. Insulin, along with thyroid hormone, is an important regulator of lipoprotein lipase activity, a key one involved in the processing of triglyceride-rich particles including chylomicrons and VLDL. Either insurance or insulin deficiency reduces lipoprotein lipase activity and results in hypertriglyceridemia. Triglycerin�ations of 1000 mg/dL or higher (≥11.30 mmol/L) increase the risk of acute pancreatitis, although closely lower levels do not confer protection. Children with inherited disorders of lipoprotein metabolism increased risk of hypertriglyceridemia-induced pancreatitis. Patients with pancreatitis associated with glyceridemia usually present with abdominal pain, nausea, and vomiting. Typical findings on physical include eruptive xanthomas over the extensor surfaces of the extremities, buttocks, and back; lipemia and hepatosplenomegaly from fatty infiltration of the liver.

enteric ischemia (Answer E) results from decreased blood flow through the intestinal vasculature. Chronic ischemia may present with “intestinal angina” and is characterized by recurrent acute episodes of pain during periods of heightened intestinal demand, such as after oral intake. Affected patients can crampy, postprandial epigastric pain, usually within an hour of eating. Subsequently, the pain subsides.

Some affected patients develop a food aversion due to the association with pain and subsequently eight loss. Patients with foregut ischemia from celiac artery insufficiency may have nausea, vomiting.

Cholecystitis (Answer D) is an acute inflammation of the gall bladder. Symptoms usually include steady, worsening right upper quadrant pain with associated guarding, fever, and nausea. Affected patients usually have leukocytosis and a positive Murphy sign, which is associated with increased pain on palpation of the gall bladder, and patients catching their breath during inspiration. Such patients are usually ill appearing and lay still because the pain is exacerbated by movement. The episode of pain lasts for several hours and is often aggravated by consumption of a fatty meal.

Gastroparesis (Answer A) is an autonomic complication in patients with diabetes and it is associated with nausea and early satiety. Affected patients are unlikely to present with abdominal pain.

Inflammatory bowel disease, including ulcerative colitis (Answer C), occurs at a higher incidence in patients with other autoimmune conditions. Like pancreatitis, ulcerative colitis can present with acute abdominal pain and signs of inflammation or infection. Usually there is a history of bloody diarrhea, or at least very frequent stools with mucus, which would not be the case in acute pancreatitis. The classic rash associated with ulcerative colitis is pyoderma gangrenosa, an ulcerating rash that tends to spread slowly.

##### Educational Objective:

Diagnose hypertriglyceridemia-induced pancreatitis in a patient with uncontrolled diabetes mellitus.

UpToDate Topic Review(s):

Hypertriglyceridemia-induced acute pancreatitis

Fortson MR, Freedman SN, Webster PD 3rd. Clinical assessment of hyperlipidemic pancreatitis. Am J Gastroenterol. 1995;90(12):2134-2139.

Wang C, Harris WS, Chung M, et al. n-3 Fatty acids from fish or fish-oil supplements, but not alpha-linolenic acid, benefit cardiovascular disease outcomes in primary- and secondary-prevention studies: a systematic review. Am J Clin Nutr. 2006;84(1):5-17.

Singer AJ, McCracken G, Henry MC, Thode HC Jr, Cabahug CJ. Correlation among clinical, laboratory, and hepatobiliary scanning findings in patients with suspected acute cholecystitis. Ann Emerg Med. 1996;28(3):267-272.

##### ANSWER: E) Need for insulin therapy

The Look AHEAD trial recruited more than 5000 overweight or obese persons with type 2 diabetes and randomly assigned them to either intensive lifestyle changes (goal of achieving weight loss >7% by aiming for a calorie goal of 1200 to 1800 kcal per day and engaging in 175 minutes of moderate-intensity physical activity per week) or a control program of support and education about lifestyle changes. The study aim was to detect the impact of lifestyle intervention on cardiovascular events using a composite score. The composite score included death of cardiovascular causes, nonfatal myocardial infarction, nonfatal stroke, and hospitalization for angina. Both cohorts experienced weight loss; the average differential weight loss between the 2 cohorts was 4% over the course of the study and 2.5% at the end of the study. Yet, there was no significant reduction in cardiovascular events or mortality in the intervention group compared with outcomes in the control group (thus, Answers A, B, and C are incorrect). The intervention cohort did experience significant improvements in urinary incontinence, sleep apnea, depression, mobility, and physical functioning. A decreased need for glucose-lowering therapy was also observed in the intervention cohort (thus, Answers E is correct). Impact on diabetes-related complications was not studied (thus, Answers D is incorrect).

Despite the lack of a significant reduction in cardiovascular events in the intervention group, many reassuring findings in this study support continued encouragement of patients to pursue a healthful lifestyle. The control group that participated in educational sessions about lifestyle changes did achieve a weight loss of 3.5% by the end of the study. This and statin use have been considered factors in the overall low number of events observed in both cohorts. No details were provided regarding the weekly activity in which the study participants engaged.

##### Educational Objective:

Counsel patients with type 2 diabetes mellitus regarding the impact of intensive lifestyle interventions.

UpToDate Topic Review(s):

Effects of exercise in diabetes mellitus in adults

Reference(s):

9 ANSWER: C) Resumption of driving after education to minimize risk of hypoglycemia while driving. Although most patients with diabetes are able to safely operate a motor vehicle, care must be taken to ideal those patients at high risk for driving-related mishaps. Patients with a history of hypoglycemia, impaired vision, peripheral neuropathy may be at increased risk for such events; however, the presence of these conditions should not automatically disqualify a patient from driving. Personalized assessment of driving safety and appropriate counseling and care to minimize the risk of hypoglycemia are essential to reducing the risk of motor vehicle accidents. Patients with hypoglycemia that occur without explanation or warning and those with a history of severe hypoglycemia within the past year are considered to be at higher risk; however, hypoglycemia that has occurred only during sleep should not automatically result in driving restrictions. Notably, the most significant predictor of collisions appears to be a history of hypoglycemia and/or mishaps occurring while driving.

This patient with longstanding type 1 diabetes does have a history of severe hypoglycemia; however, this is not a problem since his insulin regimen was modified. He is measuring his fingerstick blood glucose level many times per day, and he has not documented frequent problems with hypoglycemia. His last episode of sev hypoglycemia occurred 18 months ago and was not during daytime hours. Most importantly, he has no history driving mishaps and has only voluntarily discontinued use of his motor vehicle. Continued avoidance of driving because of fear of hypoglycemia-related collision (Answer A) does not appear necessary. As previously noted, there are particular diabetes-related complications that may pose a contraindication to driving. However, as this patient does not have clinically significant vision impairment and has only mild sensory loss in his feet, he does need to avoid driving because of his complications (thus, Answer B is incorrect).

This patient should resume use of his vehicle after receiving appropriate education and counseling to minimize the likelihood of hypoglycemia while driving (Answer C). The American Diabetes Association recommends that the counseling include instructions to always check blood glucose before driving and regularly thereafter if an individual is driving for longer than 1 hour. Education regarding the impact of even modest hypoglycemia on driving and decision-making should be included in this counseling. Should hypoglycemia occur during operation of a vehicle, driving should immediately cease and not resume until 30 to 60 minutes after the low blood glucose is adequately treated to ensure full recovery of neurocognitive function. Drivers should always have access to a blood glucose and snacks, including a form of rapid-acting glucose, in the vehicle. This patient should be regularly reassessed at follow-up appointments regarding his ability to continue to drive safely. If hypoglycemia becomes a more frequent significant problem, or if his diabetes-related complications progress, he may need to relinquish his driving privilege.

Although this patient may be a candidate for insulin pump therapy for other reasons, this change in therapy would not be required before motor vehicle use. He is currently experiencing little to no hypoglycemia on his regimen of multiple daily insulin injections, and thus a change to insulin pump therapy (Answer D) would not enhance his driving safety. Use of a continuous glucose-monitoring device during periods of driving may be a reasonable consideration for some patients. This patient monitors his blood glucose levels very closely and is not currently experiencing frequent or unexplained hypoglycemia; thus, a requirement that he use continuous glucose monitoring while driving (Answer E) is unnecessary. Furthermore, use of a continuous glucose-monitoring device would not be an appropriate substitute for educational intervention or for fingerstick blood glucose testing.

##### Educational Objective:

Use current recommendations to counsel a patient with type 1 diabetes mellitus regarding driving safety.
