Choudhary P, Amiel SA. Hypoglycaemia: current management and controversies. Postgrad Med J. 2011;87(1026):298-306.

Cox DJ, Kovatchev B, Vandecar K, Gonder-Frederick L, Ritterband L, Clarke W. Hypoglycemia preceding fatal car collisions. Diabetes Care. 2006;29(2):467-4

##### ANSWER: A) Levothyroxine replacement

This patient has elevated TPO antibodies and a serum TSH level that is above the generally accepted reference range for the first trimester of pregnancy. Physiologic changes to thyroid function in pregnancy include a first-trimester decline in serum TSH and serum free  $ T_{4} $. Using a laboratory that has assays with pregnancy-specific reference ranges is recommended, but in the absence of such a resource, referring to the following TSH reference ranges is advised: 0.1 to 2.5 mIU/L (first trimester), 0.2 to 3.0 mIU/L (second trimester), and 0.3 to 3.0 mIU/L (third trimester). Serum TSH concentrations between 2.5 and 10.0 mIU/L with a normal free  $ T_{4} $ concentration are defined as subclinical hypothyroidism in the first trimester of pregnancy.

Although subclinical hypothyroidism is the most common form of thyroid dysfunction in pregnancy, its management remains controversial. Subclinical hypothyroidism can be indicative of early thyroid insufficiency that has come to light given the increased thyroid demand in pregnancy. Subclinical hypothyroidism has been associated with increased risks of adverse pregnancy complications including pregnancy loss, late-onset preeclampsia, and preterm delivery, which have been documented in some, but not all, studies. The detrimental effects of subclinical hypothyroidism on fetal neurocognitive development have also not been demonstrated consistently, although some studies have shown significant IQ deficits in the offspring of women with raised serum TSH concentrations in the early part of pregnancy. Evidence for levothyroxine's effectiveness in reducing these risks in pregnancies complicated by subclinical hypothyroidism is robust in some, but not all, studies, indicating reduced risks of miscarriage and preterm delivery. Moreover, the Controlled Antenatal Thyroid Screening study (CATS) showed no beneficial effect of levothyroxine treatment on offspring's mean IQ scores at age 3 years. Even though evidence of a definite beneficial effect of levothyroxine treatment in large-scale prospective studies is not available, many endocrine professional bodies have advocated levothyroxine treatment of subclinical hypothyroidism in pregnancy, ideally commencing preconception, given the highly favorable potential benefit-to-risk ratio. Recent American Thyroid Association guidelines support treatment of subclinical hypothyroidism only in TPO antibody-positive women, but remain in equipoise for treatment of TPO antibody-negative subclinical hypothyroidism. Guidelines from the Endocrine Society also recommend levothyroxine treatment of subclinical hypothyroidism. Taking the most current internationally accepted guidance into consideration, Answer A is the best choice.

Since the generation of biologically active  $ T_{3} $ by the peripheral conversion of  $ T_{4} $ to  $ T_{3} $ was documented in 1970, levothyroxine monotherapy has become the mainstay of treating hypothyroidism. Although a similar quality of life and circulating  $ T_{3} $ levels have been reported in patients treated with levothyroxine and in individuals without thyroid disease, other studies have not shown levels of satisfaction comparable to those of euthyroid controls. After a 1999 report citing the benefit of levothyroxine and liothyronine combination therapy, a number of studies have readdressed the benefits of synthetic levothyroxine and liothyronine combination therapy, but have largely failed to confirm an advantage of this approach to improve cognitive or mood outcomes in hypothyroid individuals treated with levothyroxine alone. Moreover there is no robust evidence that liothyronine monotherapy results in better outcomes. This approach is not recommended outside pregnancy and guidelines strongly recommend against doing this during pregnancy (thus, Answer B is incorrect).

The use of desiccated thyroid has rarely been systematically studied. Absorption studies indicate that the bioavailability of  $ T_{3} $ in desiccated thyroid is comparable to that of orally administered synthetic liothyronine. Therefore, the most commonly used form of desiccated thyroid, which is of porcine origin, may be viewed as a levothyroxine and liothyronine combination with a ratio of approximately 4:1 by weight. The content of thyroid hormone and the ratio of  $ T_{4} $ to  $ T_{3} $ may vary in desiccated thyroid preparations depending on the brand and whether it is of porcine origin. In the absence of controlled trials supporting the preferred use of desiccated thyroid hormone over synthetic levothyroxine in the treatment of hypothyroidism or any other thyroid disease, is not a recommended approach. Current guidelines concerning management of thyroid disease in pregnancy strongly recommend against the use of desiccated thyroid extract (thus, Answer D is incorrect).

There is clear evidence that the human fetus is exposed to physiologically relevant concentrations of maternal thyroid hormones as early as 5 weeks' gestation. In addition, studies that screen and diagnose subclinically hypothyroid women before conception report the highest pregnancy loss rates. It is therefore important to immediately start levothyroxine in this patient and simply repeating thyroid function tests in 4 weeks (Answer C) is incorrect.

Treatment with prednisone (Answer E) is not indicated in this patient and has no proven benefit in halting the progression of subclinical hypothyroidism to overt hypothyroidism, even in the presence of elevated TPO antibodies.

Educational Objective:

##### ANSWER: E) Start risedronate

The use of glucocorticoids is associated with increased bone loss, which is greatest in the first 3 to 6 more after therapy initiation. Moreover, fracture rates are greatly increased with glucocorticoids and can occur even patients with normal bone density. Treatment is warranted in patients with established osteoporosis, in patients with increased fracture risk on the basis of FRAX score, and in patients taking a daily prednisone dosage of 7.5 mg or more for 3 months or longer. Given her history of rheumatoid arthritis and glucocorticoid use, this patient has a FRAX calculated fracture risk of 4% for the hip and 20% for major osteoporotic fracture. She has been taking more than 7.5 mg of prednisone daily for 6 months and meets treatment thresholds established by the American College of Rheumatology. Treatment with a bisphosphonate, such as risedronate (Answer E), would be warranted in this patient. Both alendronate and risedronate have been proven effective in preventing and treating glucocorticoid-induced bone loss.

Although the dosage of supplemental vitamin D needed to provide adequate vitamin D stores varies among individuals, this patient is receiving enough to meet the target 25-hydroxyvitamin D level of 32 ng/mL (79.9 nmol/L) (thus, Answer B is incorrect). There may be little downside to an additional 2000 IU of vitamin D per but higher vitamin D levels are associated with an increased risk of nephrolithiasis and no further benefit has been established in terms of bone health. Calcium intake of 1200 mg daily, including supplements and dietary intake, considered adequate for most patients. In addition, glucocorticoids induce hypercalciuria, and high-dosage calcium supplementation could result in nephrolithiasis (thus, Answer A is incorrect). Raloxifene (Answer C) would not be the best treatment option because it does not offer fracture protection at the hip and is not approved by the Food and Drug Administration for treatment of glucocorticoid-induced osteoporosis. Denosumab (Answer D) has not been shown to prevent glucocorticoid-induced osteoporosis and is not approved by the US Food and Drug Administration for treatment of this condition.

##### Reference(s):

rossman JM, Gordon R, Ranganath VK, et al. American College of Rheumatology 2010 recommendations for the prevention and treatment of glucocorticoid induced osteoporosis [published correction in Arthritis Care Res (Hoboken). 2012;64(3):464]. Arthritis Care Res (Hoboken). 2010;62(11):1515-1526.

12 ANSWER: E) Insulin, <3 μIU/mL (<20.8 pmol/L)/C-Peptide, <2.0 ng/mL (<0.7 nmol/L)/Proinsulin, <5 pg/mL (<0.6 pmol/L)/Insulin Antibodies, Negative//Sulfonylurea Screen, Negative

Given the presence of multiple bulky hepatic metastases, it is unlikely that the patient's hypoglycemia is caused by inappropriate self-administration of sulfonylureas or other oral hypoglycemic agents (thus, Answer B is incorrect) or by administration of insulin (thus, Answer C is incorrect). Insulin antibody-mediated hypoglycemia would explain the laboratory data in Answer A, but this etiology would be unlikely in this patient given the presence of metastatic disease. The pattern in Answer D would be typical for insulinoma-mediated hypoglycemia. Conventional noninvasive imaging modalities can miss 30% to 40% of intrapancreatic insulinomas. However, it would be unlikely for a malignant insulinoma to present with metastatic disease yet not be visible on CT of the pancreas.

The pattern in Answer E is most compatible with tumor-mediated hypoglycemia where a circulating factor other than insulin is stimulating the insulin receptor. In such circumstances, endogenous production of β-cell polypeptides ceases, as would be expected when glucose concentrations fall below the 60 to 55 mg/dL threshold (3.33 to 3.05 mmol/L). This is certainly the case in this scenario. Indeed, the tumor mediating hypoglycemia in these circumstances is rarely occult and is easily detectable on imaging. Hypoglycemia can be caused by many tumors that are associated with an insulinlike factor. Most commonly, these include malignant fibrous tumors, renal cell carcinoma, hepatic adenocarcinoma, germ-cell tumors, and hematologic malignancies such as lymphoma.

##### Educational Objective:

Guide the diagnostic workup of a patient with large hepatic lesions and hypoglycemia.

##### Reference(s):

Placzkowski KA, Vella A, Thompson GB, et al. Secular trends in the presentation and management of functioning insulinoma at the Mayo Clinic, 1987-2007. J Clin Endocrinol Metab. 2009;94(4):1069-1073.

Le Roith D. Tumor-induced hypoglycemia. N Engl J Med. 1999;341(10):757-758.

##### ANSWER: A) Reduce the levothyroxine dosage to 112 mcg daily

This patient had stage III thyroid cancer because she was older than 45 years when she was found to have a 3-cm tumor with cervical lymph node involvement. She is now 15 years out from her initial diagnosis. No uptake in a TSH-stimulated radioiodine scan and an undetectable serum thyroglobulin concentration on 2 occasions are excellent predictors of disease-free status. In addition, the patient's subsequent surveillance has continued to indicate that there is no detectable disease present on the basis of her unremarkable cervical ultrasonography and undetectable basal serum thyroglobulin levels. Thus, although the patient has some recurrence risk, her risk is quite low.

Suppression of serum TSH, by essentially inducing iatrogenic subclinical hyperthyroidism, is an important tool for managing differentiated thyroid cancer. However, its benefit is most clearly demonstrated in patients who have high-risk thyroid cancer. Although continued TSH suppression (Answer C) would have been an appropriate approach to managing this patient immediately after diagnosis, such therapy is no longer merited for several reasons. First, following an approach in which this patient's risk is reassessed on the basis of results of follow-up monitoring, she now has a much lower recurrence risk than she did immediately after her diagnosis. Thus, there is now less benefit of TSH suppression. In addition, as with any other therapy, the risks vs benefits must be evaluated. At her age, the risks associated with this degree of TSH suppression (her current TSH concentration is 0.09 mIU/L) are considerable. The 2 main risks of TSH suppression that are particularly relevant for this patient are the detrimental effects of hyperthyroidism on bone density, with a resultant increase in fracture risk, and the risk of atrial fibrillation. Although both these risks are significant, it is especially important to consider risks to the skeleton in an individual who already has established osteoporosis. It is difficult to separate age-related bone loss from hyperthyroidism-induced bone loss in this age population. The risk of atrial fibrillation is approximately doubled in those with subclinical hyperthyroidism, whereas the fracture risk may be increased by 3- to 4-fold. All-cause mortality may also be increased in those with subclinical hyperthyroidism.

The best management strategy for this patient, which accounts for her low risk of thyroid cancer recurrence, would be to adjust her levothyroxine dosing to maintain her serum TSH concentration within the normal range. Thus, the levothyroxine dosage should be lowered and her dosage should be adjusted on the basis of her serum TSH levels. Given her body weight, a levothyroxine dosage of 100 mcg (94 mcg = 1.6 mcg/kg) might be predicted to result in a normal serum TSH. A reasonable strategy would be to reduce her dosage to 112 mcg daily (Answer A) and on the basis of her serum TSH values.

Although occasionally a repeated TSH-stimulated scan and thyroglobulin measurement may yield a positive result, this occurs in only a very small percentage of cases (thus, Answer E is incorrect). Reducing this patient's levothyroxine dosage and adding liothyronine (Answer B) is not likely to reduce her degree of TSH suppression this would not reduce her risk of worsening osteoporosis or developing atrial fibrillation. β-Blockade could reduce the tachycardia and potentially alleviate some of the tremor. However, it would have no bearing on her potential bone loss and is not indicated (thus, Answer D is incorrect).

##### Educational Objective:

Assess the risks vs benefits of levothyroxine therapy to suppress serum TSH in a patient with a history of thymocancer and determine the appropriate serum TSH goal.

14 ANSWER: A) Desogestrel (0.15 mg) and ethinyl estradiol (0.03 mg) combined oral contraceptive pill. The combination of oligomenorrhea, elevated BMI, and clinical and biochemical evidence of hyperandrogenism make polycystic ovary syndrome (PCOS) a possible diagnosis. However, the high concentration of 17α-hydroxyprogesterone suggests that nonclassic (also known as late-onset) congenital adrenal hyperplasia (CAH) is, in fact, the etiology of this patient's symptoms. This could be confirmed by performing an ACTH-stimulation test, which would demonstrate a further rise in 17α-hydroxyprogesterone concentrations.

Late-onset CAH usually presents in late childhood, adolescence, or early adulthood and can be clinically indistinguishable from PCOS. Therefore, late-onset CAH should be excluded in all women who present with appt PCOS by measuring plasma 17α-hydroxyprogesterone (normal in PCOS and elevated in nonclassic CAH because reduced 21α-hydroxylase activity). However, one must be careful when interpreting 17α-hydroxyprogesterone level in premenopausal women. During the luteal phase, levels can approach 300 ng/dL (9.1 nmol/L), but the concentral should never exceed 1000 ng/dL (30.3 nmol/L) as was observed in this vignette.

Nonclassic CAH is inherited in an autosomal recessive manner and its prevalence varies according to ethm. Overall, the prevalence is estimated to be 1 in 1000 in white populations, but as high as 1 in 27 in women of Eastern European Jewish descent and 1 in 40 among persons of Hispanic ancestry.

In nonclassic CAH, mutations in the CYP21A2 gene lead to reduced, but not absent, 21α-hydroxylase activity. Disease-causing mutations are characterized as severe or mild, depending on the amount of residual enzyme activity. Persons with late-onset CAH typically have either 2 mild mutations or 1 mild mutation and 1 severe mutation, resulting in 20% to 60% of normal enzymatic activity. Therefore, adrenal cortisol production is maintained at the expense of increased ACTH drive to the adrenal, which leads to androgen excess and increased levels of precursor steroid such as 17α-hydroxyprogesterone. In contrast, individuals with classic CAH present birth with ambiguous genitalia (in girls) and adrenal insufficiency due to absent 21α-hydroxylase activity.

The young woman in this vignette has menstrual disturbance and other clinical features of androgen excess (facial acne and hirsutism). Given that she does not currently desire pregnancy, treatment should be aimed at addressing these symptoms. It is clear in the vignette that menstrual disturbance is the predominant problem, so the combined oral contraceptive pill (Answer A) is the most appropriate treatment. The oral contraceptive pill effectively suppresses ovarian androgens and thus may also address her acne and hirsutism.

Because women with nonclassic CAH have normal cortisol production, routine corticosteroid treatment to ensure adequate glucocorticoid levels is not required. However, exogenous glucocorticoid inhibition of ACTH also reduces adrenal androgen production (and 17α-hydroxyprogesterone) and would thereby alleviate her symptoms of hyperandrogenism and restore a normal menstrual cycle. However, glucocorticoid therapy is less appropriate than oral contraceptives for this patient. She does not currently desire fertility and glucocorticoid therapy would make her potentially fertile, thereby necessitating birth control if she were to become sexually active. A number of glucocorticoid regimens have been used for patients with CAH who desire pregnancy, including dexamethasone at bedtime (Answer D). However, the dosage used in these circumstances is usually 250 mcg. Thus, 2 mg nightly represents gross overreplacement and would result in iatrogenic Cushing syndrome.

Metformin (Answer C) can be used in the management of PCOS, but it is not recommended for the management of oligomenorrhea or hirsutism in CAH because the hyperandrogenism is not related to insulin resistance. Treatment should be directed toward reduction of androgen production, not blockade of androgen action as a first approach to treatment. Thus, spironolactone (Answer E) is incorrect. A long-acting GnRH analogue (Answer B) will have no effect on adrenal androgen production.

##### Educational Objective:

Distinguish between the diagnoses of polycystic ovary syndrome and late-onset congenital adrenal hyperplasia and recommend appropriate treatment.

## 15 ANSWER: E) Improvement in diabetes mellitus

This patient has persistent acromegaly that was partially resistant to somatostatin analogue therapy after transsphenoidal surgery. Therefore, additional therapy is indicated and pegvisomant is a reasonable next therapeutic option for him. Given that this patient has diabetes, pegvisomant is especially appropriate medical therapy. Although the lowering of GH levels with somatostatin analogue therapy should improve insulin resistance in patients with acromegaly, these medications also suppress insulin levels. Thus, glucose intolerance and diabetes may not improve with somatostatin analogues. Pegvisomant, however, has been shown to improve insulin sensitivity and lower hemoglobin  $ A_{1c} $ levels in patients with acromegaly (thus, Answer E is correct).

Pegvisomant therapy is begun at a dosage of 10 mg daily and is titrated to achieve a normal IGF-1 concentration. In a small percentage of patients (2%-3%), liver enzymes rise on pegvisomant therapy. This tends to occur in the first 6 months of therapy and is usually transient. It is recommended that liver enzymes be monitored monthly after pegvisomant is initiated. If they rise to more than 3 times the upper normal limit of the reference range, pegvisomant therapy should be held. Therefore, monitoring liver enzymes regularly, especially in the first 6 months is necessary, but the development of abnormalities is unlikely (thus, Answer A is incorrect). If pegvisomant is held because of elevated liver enzymes, a re-challenge can be done if the liver enzymes normalize off therapy. Rarely do persistent enzyme elevations limit the use of pegvisomant.

Pituitary tumor size must also be monitored during pegvisomant therapy. However, the risk of pituitary tumor enlargement is small, roughly 3% over a 5-year period (thus, Answer B is incorrect). Pegvisomant does not

accelerate pituitary tumor growth. In patients taking pegvisomant, GH-secreting tumors seem to enlarge at the previous growth rate. Patients at higher risk for tumor enlargement are those whose tumors have been shown to enlarging before therapy, those who had significant shrinkage on somatostatin analogue therapy that is withdrawal or those who have not had radiotherapy. Although not necessary in this patient, if radiotherapy had been performed it would be expected to have reduced the chance of tumor enlargement on pegvisomant.

Tachyphylaxis to pegvisomant is not reported (thus, Answer D is incorrect).

Pegvisomant does not act on pituitary tumor GH secretion, but rather treats acromegaly by blocking peripheral receptors, thus lowering IGF-1 levels. On pegvisomant, GH levels remain the same or rise somewhat. Therefore, only IGF-1 levels, not GH levels, are monitored as a marker of pegvisomant's effectiveness (thus, Answer C is incorrect).

## 16 ANSWER: C) Once blood glucose falls below 200 mg/dL (11.10 mmol/L), add 5% dextrose to the intravenous fluids and continue intravenous insulin until the acidosis has resolved

intravenous fluid

Diabetic ketoacidosis is a life-threatening complication of severe insulin deficiency that is commonly seen in patients with newly diagnosed type 1 diabetes. However, it can also occur in patients with type 1 diabetes who interrupt basal and bolus insulin for more than 24 hours, or in those on insulin pump therapy whose rapid-acting insulin analogue infusion is interrupted for more than 4 to 6 hours. Diabetic ketoacidosis may also complicate severe stressful illness in patients with type 1 or type 2 diabetes.

The severity of ketoacidosis is determined on the basis of the arterial pH, serum bicarbonate, and alteration of sensorium:



<table border=1 style='margin: auto; word-wrap: break-word;'><tr><td rowspan="2">Measurement</td><td colspan="3">Severity of Ketoacidosis</td></tr><tr><td style='text-align: center; word-wrap: break-word;'>Mild</td><td style='text-align: center; word-wrap: break-word;'>Moderate</td><td style='text-align: center; word-wrap: break-word;'>Severe</td></tr><tr><td style='text-align: center; word-wrap: break-word;'>Arterial pH</td><td style='text-align: center; word-wrap: break-word;'>7.25-7.30</td><td style='text-align: center; word-wrap: break-word;'>7.00-7.24</td><td style='text-align: center; word-wrap: break-word;'>&lt;7.00</td></tr><tr><td style='text-align: center; word-wrap: break-word;'>Serum bicarbonate</td><td style='text-align: center; word-wrap: break-word;'>15-18 mEq/L\n(15-18 mmol/L)</td><td style='text-align: center; word-wrap: break-word;'>10-14 mEq/L\n(10-14 mmol/L)</td><td style='text-align: center; word-wrap: break-word;'>&lt;10 mEq/L\n(&lt;10 mmol/L)</td></tr><tr><td style='text-align: center; word-wrap: break-word;'>Alteration in sensorium</td><td style='text-align: center; word-wrap: break-word;'>Alert</td><td style='text-align: center; word-wrap: break-word;'>Alert/drowsy</td><td style='text-align: center; word-wrap: break-word;'>Stupor/coma</td></tr></table>

The standard of treatment of severe diabetic ketoacidosis involves aggressive fluid resuscitation; intravenous insulin; repletion of potassium; and close monitoring of hydration status, glucose, acidosis, and electrolytes. Following serum carbon dioxide or venous pH has mostly replaced following the arterial pH (Answer A) to assess adequacy of therapy. This avoids the pain and potential complications of serial arterial blood gas determination.

Venous pH is about 0.03 units lower than arterial pH, a nonclinically significant difference. Insulin therapy is the primary intervention to correct acidosis and it acts by inducing the metabolism of the ketoacids  $ \beta $-hydroxybutyrate and acetoacetate to bicarbonate. Insulin directly inhibits lipolysis and ketogenesis, promotes clearance of free fatty acids, and inhibits fatty acid oxidation to ketones in the liver. Administration of bicarbonate (Answer B) to patients with diabetic ketoacidosis who present with a pH as low as 6.9 has not been shown to speed correction of acidosis or reduce mortality. No randomized controlled studies of bicarbonate use have been performed in patients with a pH below 6.9. In those with marked hyperkalemia complicating ketoacidosis, bicarbonate can be used to accelerate the intracellular translocation of potassium from the intravascular space.

When treating ketoacidosis, intravenous insulin must be continued until the acidosis has cleared. To prevent hypoglycemia from occurring with continued intravenous insulin infusion, it is recommended that 5% dextrose be added to intravenous fluids when glucose levels fall below 200 mg/dL (<11.10 mmol/L) (thus, Answer C is correct). Normalization of acidosis, ketosis, and hyperglycemia is similar when intravenous regular insulin is compared with intravenous rapid-acting insulin (thus, Answer D is incorrect). In studies of patients with mild to moderate acidosis, equivalent rates of correction of acidosis, clearance of ketones, and glucose decline have been documented with intravenous regular insulin vs subcutaneous rapid-acting insulin administered every 1 to 2 hours. However, comparison of these 2 insulin regimens has not been well studied in patients with severe acidosis, in whom the rate of insulin administration can be more rapidly titrated with an intravenous insulin drip (thus, Answer E is incorrect).

Alterations in serum sodium are common in patients presenting with ketoacidosis. Hyperglycemia leads to the movement of intracellular water osmotically to the intravascular space, causing hyponatremia. Glucosuria leads to an osmotic diuresis of water in excess of sodium, increasing serum sodium. Hypernatremia is typically seen only with more severe hyperglycemia and dehydration. Marked hyperlipidemia can be seen in ketoacidosis, causing pseudohyponatremia as elevated lipids reduce the measured serum sodium concentration. Abnormalities of serum sodium in ketoacidosis correct after treatment with insulin and fluid resuscitation.

##### Educational Objective:

Treat advanced diabetic ketoacidosis.

UpToDate Topic Review(s):

## 47 ANSWER: E) Lumbar spine bone mineral density

Primary hyperparathyroidism is a common disorder. The incidence is reported to be as high as 1 in 1000 in some studies, although it may be becoming less frequent because of a decline in ionizing radiation exposure. Women are 2 to 3 times more likely than men to be affected, and the condition generally presents after age 45 years. There are additional risk factors for developing the condition including exposure to head and neck irradiation; a family history of hyperparathyroidism (there are multiple genetic syndromes in which the risk of hyperparathyroidism is increased); and the use of certain medications, most commonly lithium. Lithium has been associated with both glandular hyperplasia of parathyroid tissue, as well as a possible shift in the set point of the calcium-sensing receptor in parathyroid cells, leading to an excessive release of PTH. In patients with bipolar psychiatric disease, lithium use is relatively common. Even if the patient is not currently treated with lithium, one must query for historical use because the hyperparathyroidism can persist despite discontinuation of the medication. The therapeutic goal is to identify symptomatic patients with known complications or currently asymptomatic patients with a high likelihood of developing complications and to refer them for surgical intervention.

Current (2009) recommendations for surgery for asymptomatic primary hyperparathyroidism are outlined as follows:

1. Serum calcium concentration greater than 1.0 mg/dL (0.25 mmol/L) above the upper normal limit;

2. Creatinine clearance (calculated) reduced to less than 60 mL/min;

3. Bone mineral density T score less than -2.5 at any site and/or previous fragility fracture

4. Age younger than 50 years

The most clinically significant complications of untreated symptomatic primary hyperparathyroidism are renal up to 20% of this patient population experiences kidney stones, which are usually composed of calcium oxal. Other renal complications include chronic renal insufficiency and renal tubular dysfunction. Although this par has hypercalciuria, she has no history of kidney stones. Interestingly, urinary calcium excretion does not predict the development of stones. In the absence of stones, elevated urinary calcium is not considered an indication of parathyroid surgery. A 24-hour urinary calcium collection is still nonetheless an important part of the biochem evaluation to further evaluate for disorders of the calcium-sensing receptor. Glomerular filtration rate and ren function must be closely monitored in patients with untreated primary hyperparathyroidism. A decline in ren function and glomerular filtration rate, not otherwise accounted for by other medical issues, is an indication of surgical intervention. In this patient, the creatinine excretion and estimated glomerular filtration rate are normal a change following surgical cure is unlikely (thus, Answer B is incorrect).

Cardiovascular complications of primary hyperparathyroidism include left ventricular hypertrophy and hypertension. A survival benefit has been shown after parathyroid surgery in multiple European studies; how these patient populations had more severe disease than their US counterparts. Observational studies do not have a reversibility or improvement in hypertension after successful surgery for primary hyperparathyroidism, in particular if the hypertension antedates the development of hypercalcemia and hyperparathyroidism as was there (thus, Answer A is incorrect).

Only a small number of patients have neurocognitive symptoms related to hypercalcemia, although some patients report depression and a decline in neurocognition and quality-of-life parameters if the hypercalcemia is left untreated. Most studies that have examined these symptoms and shown improvement after surgery are observational and limited by selection bias. In a few small trials, there was little change in psychosocial function after parathyroid surgery compared with a significant progressive decline in the observational group. In this vignette, given that the patient's bipolar disorder preceded the development of her hyperparathyroidism, it is unlikely that her psychiatric disorder will improve after surgery (thus, Answer C is incorrect).

Bone mineral density in patients with hyperparathyroidism often shows a characteristic pattern of bone primarily in cortical bone, more so than in trabecular bone. The DXA result in this patient is consistent with trend. After parathyroidectomy, bone density at the spine increases rapidly and significantly (thus, Answer E correct), and, on average, it may remain higher for at least a decade. Responses at the femoral neck are small bone density does increase at that site as well. Despite the relatively greater loss at the distal radius, bone density is less than the total density.

little changed after successful parathyroid surgery, even in patients who experience an increase at the spine (thus, Answer D is incorrect).

##### ANSWER: A) hCG injections

Unlike patients with primary hypogonadism in whom sperm production is permanently affected due to damage to the seminiferous tubules, men with secondary hypogonadism (due to hypothalamic or pituitary disease) are fortunate because spermatogenesis can often be induced via treatment with GnRH or gonadotropins. When secondary hypogonadism is caused by diseases of the hypothalamus (genetic or acquired), treatment with GnRH can option. GnRH is administered in a pulsatile fashion via an infusion pump that delivers a bolus every 90 to 20 minutes via a subcutaneous needle residing in the abdomen. This in turn stimulates secretion of gonadotropins from the pituitary gonadotropes (LH stimulates the Leydig cells of the testes resulting in synthesis and secretion of testosterone, while FSH stimulates seminiferous tubules to promote spermatogenesis). Hence, intactness of the pituitary gland is a prerequisite for GnRH therapy. This patient's gonadotropes have been damaged as a consequence of hypophysectomy (thus, Answer C is incorrect).

An essential component of the negative feedback loop of the hypothalamic-pituitary-gonadal axis is aromatization of testosterone to estradiol (estradiol is a more potent inhibitor of GnRH and gonadotropins than testosterone itself). Blocking either the production or action of estradiol stimulates the hypothalamic-pituitary-gonadal axis. Clomiphene citrate (Answer D) is a weak estrogen receptor antagonist that stimulates GnRH, and turn, gonadotropin secretion by blocking estrogen receptors on the hypothalamus. Similarly, letrozole (Answer E) is an aromatase inhibitor that stimulates the hypothalamic-pituitary-gonadal axis by inhibiting aromatization of testosterone into estradiol. However, intactness of both the hypothalamus and the pituitary are a prerequisite for these therapies to be effective.

Gonadotropins are an effective treatment in patients with secondary hypogonadism irrespective of the site of the disease (hypothalamus or pituitary) because they bypass these organs and act directly on the testes. hCG possesses the biologic activity of LH (they have an identical α-subunit) and stimulates the Leydig cells to synthesize and secrete testosterone. As a result, the concentration of intratesticular testosterone increases (80- to 100-fold higher than in serum), which is crucial for the stimulation of spermatogenesis. FSH, however, stimulates spermatogenesis by acting on the Sertoli cells of the seminiferous tubules. Studies have shown that in postpubertal men who develop secondary hypogonadism (such as the man in this vignette), hCG alone is sufficient to stimulate spermatogenesis, even if the testes have undergone some atrophy. Interestingly, even prepubertal men with secondary hypogonadism who have larger testicular size at baseline (>8 mL) and do not have history of cryptorchidism also respond successfully to hCG alone. In this patient, the development of secondary hypogonadism was postpubertal and his testes are scrotal and larger than 8 mL, which suggests that monotherapy with hCG (Answer A) will be sufficient. Because preparations of hCG are less expensive than FSH, the sole use of hCG is also cost-effective. FSH in combination with hCG (Answer B) is generally necessary in those men who develop prepubertal hypogonadism (eg, Kallmann syndrome and its variants), have small testicular size (<8 mL), or have a history of cryptorchidism (although the response to dual therapy remains poor in the latter group).

##### Educational Objective:

Recommend the most appropriate therapy for fertility induction in a man with postpubertal secondary hypogonadism.

## 10 ANSWER: C) BMI, normal; cardiovascular risk, increased

BMI determination (weight [kg]/height [m²]) has been the accepted criterion for the diagnosis of obesity. BMI values can identify persons at risk for weight-related medical complications and increased mortality. However,

BMI has limitations in that it does not provide information regarding body adiposity, which is well recognized contribute to pathophysiologic processes affecting health and mortality. This is particularly true for persons in normal-weight category (18.5-24.9 kg/m²) or overweight category (25.0-29.9 kg/m²). This patient's BMI of 23 kg/m² is considered normal (thus, Answers A, B, and E are incorrect). For patients with a BMI in this range, both composition can provide additional information regarding health risks. In individuals with an elevated BMI, those who have a healthy body composition (defined as a body fat percentage of less than 23.1% in men and less than 33.3% in women) may not have an increased risk for metabolic complications. This patient has a high body percentage (37%). Recent studies suggest that despite a normal BMI, increased adiposity confers an increased risk for metabolic complications and increased mortality, particularly for women. The state of having a normal BMI increased body fat has been termed normal-weight obesity. Romero-Corral et al reported that women with normal weight obesity have a 2.2-fold increased risk for cardiovascular mortality when compared with the risk of women with normal body adiposity (thus, Answers C is correct and Answers D is incorrect).

weight obesity have a 2.2-fold increase with normal body adiposity (thus, Answer C is correct and). There are several methods for measuring body composition. The most accurate tend to be cumbersome (underwater weighing) and not applicable to routine clinical practice. Anthropometric measurements (height, weight, waist circumference, skin-fold thickness) are easily assessed and provide valuable information regarding a patient's health risks, but they have limitations. Waist circumference measurement requires a consistent measure of the level of the iliac crest. Waist circumference for accuracy: flexible measuring tape in the horizontal view at the level of the iliac crest. Waist circumference identifies individuals with abdominal adiposity, which confers the highest risk for metabolic complications. Measurement of skin-fold thickness is less accurate and is most often used as a research tool. DXA provides a 3-compartment model for body composition, but it can have substantial intraindividual variability and it is a Body plethysmography uses air displacement instead of water displacement to measure body fat. Measurement correlates well with underwater weighing and are accurate. Impedance measurements are widely available (weeks), but have limitations. Impedance determines body water content, and formulas have been developed to convert that measurement to body fat. Several studies suggest that these methods underestimate body fat (2% variability) when compared with fat measurement determined by DXA. Documenting how this patient had the percentage of body fat determined is important. If it was determined by body plethysmography, the measurement most likely accurate.

## 20 ANSWER: B) Dexamethasone, 0.5 mg daily

20 Androgen deprivation therapy is the standard of care for patients with advanced prostate cancer. How many patients eventually develop castration-resistant prostate cancer and less than 20% of these men survive beyond 3 years. The evidence now suggests that many of these men with castration-resistant prostate cancer have up-regulation of androgen biosynthesis enzymes resulting in an increase in intratumoral androgen concentration. Abiraterone is a selective inhibitor of CYP17A1, a key enzyme in cortisol synthesis, by inhibiting  $ 17\alpha $-hydroxyactivity. It also inhibits androgen biosynthesis by inhibiting 17,20-lyase activity, which catalyzes the conversion of 17-hydroxyprognenolone to the main androgen precursor DHEA. In men with castration-resistant prostate cancer, abiraterone prolongs overall survival.

Most patients treated with abiraterone develop ACTH-driven mineralocorticoid excess. There is an expect significant increase in serum deoxycorticosterone, corticosterone, and 11-deoxycortisol levels after treatment with abiraterone. ACTH levels increase by 200% to 1400% with abiraterone therapy. Despite subnormal cortisol levels, the risk of other diseases is higher than the risk of other conditions.

secretion, these patients have no signs or symptoms of glucocorticoid deficiency. The most likely explanation for this is the glucocorticoid activity of corticosterone whose levels are markedly elevated in patients treated with abiraterone. Most importantly, abiraterone effectively lowers adrenal, testicular, and intratumoral androgen production resulting in some attenuation of disease progression.

<div style="text-align: center;"><img src="imgs/img_in_image_box_163_242_934_821.jpg" alt="Image" width="64%" /></div>


Because aldosterone secretion is regulated by the renin-angiotensin system, abiraterone has no effect on aldosterone secretion per se. Although aldosterone increases acutely with an ACTH infusion, chronic ACTH elevations have no direct effect on aldosterone. In fact, the mineralocorticoid excess state that ensues (due to elevations of deoxycorticosterone) suppresses the plasma renin activity and keeps aldosterone levels in the low-normal range. The aldosterone-to-plasma renin activity ratio is meaningless in this patient (because the aldosterone is low). Therefore, he does not have primary hyperaldosteronism and the state of hypermineralocorticoidism would preclude the use of fludrocortisone (Answer D). However, the addition of exogenous nonmineralocorticoid steroids such as dexamethasone (Answer B) to abiraterone therapy promptly decreases ACTH and resolves the hypermineralocorticoid state. Either dexamethasone (0.25 to 0.50 mg daily) or prednisone (5 to 10 mg daily) is now recommended when abiraterone therapy is initiated.

Concomitant inhibition of 17,20-lyase by abiraterone also diverts 17-hydroxyprogesterone metabolites toward androgen synthesis via a backdoor pathway. Dexamethasone therapy further suppresses any rise in androgens from this unique mechanism.  $ 3\alpha,5\alpha $-17-Hydroxyprogesterone is a metabolite of 17-hydroxyprogesterone, which increases with abiraterone therapy. This metabolite can then be converted to dihydrotestosterone, probably in the adrenal gland or the prostate cancer itself.

Mifepristone (Answer C) is a potent progesterone and glucocorticoid receptor antagonist that is effective in patients with all forms of endogenous hypercortisolism. Although it is a weak androgen receptor antagonist, it has no role in the management of patients with prostate cancer. The administration of mifepristone causes further increases in ACTH and cortisol, which would only serve to further aggravate the hypermineralocorticoid state in this man. Adrenal androgen (DHEA) therapy (Answer A) is certainly not indicated in a patient with metastatic prostate cancer. Metyrapone (CYP11A—11β-hydroxylase inhibitor) (Answer E) would only serve to further increase the mineralocorticoid deoxycorticosterone and exacerbate his hypokalemia and hypertension.

## 21 ANSWER: D) Core biopsy of the largest thyroid nodule with repeated flow cytometry

21 ANSWER: D) Core biopsy of the largest thyroid nodule with repeated how� to present with goiter, but it is rarely, if ever, rapidly expanding. Painful thyroiditis can present with a diffuse goit or nodularity, but the physician should make every effort to eliminate the possibility that the growth represents an underlying malignancy. In the thyroid, anaplastic thyroid cancer, medullary carcinoma, and an intrathyroid lymphoma can present in this fashion. Rarely, the thyroid is the site of metastatic disease from a malignancy elsewhere.

Many different autoimmune diseases have an increased risk of lymphoma development. This has been well described in rheumatoid arthritis, Sjogren syndrome, systemic lupus erythematosus, celiac disease, and Hashimoto thyroiditis. Sjogren syndrome, Hashimoto thyroiditis, and celiac disease are linked to the development of lymphoma in the involved target organ. For example, Sjogren syndrome is associated with mucosa-associated lymphoid tissue in the parotid gland, Hashimoto thyroiditis is associated with mucosa-associated lymphoid tissue in the thyroid gland, and celiac disease is associated with T-cell lymphoma in the small intestine. Thus, in this patient, further sampling to obtain lymphoid tissue for the evaluation of lymphoma is indicated (Answer D).

In this patient, findings from a core biopsy of the thyroid nodule suggested B-cell lymphoma. As a means of staging disease, not for a diagnostic evaluation, a bone marrow biopsy (Answer C) done after the diagnosis was established and comprehensive imaging, including a fluorodeoxyglucose positron emission tomography scan (Answer A), showed disease dissemination. The avid fluorodeoxyglucose uptake within the patient's neck and its localization with a fused CT scan is shown in the Figure. The patient was referred for chemotherapy and radiotherapy for diffuse large B-cell lymphoma. In addition, his levothyroxine dosage was increased.

<div style="text-align: center;"><img src="imgs/img_in_image_box_191_1029_1172_1451.jpg" alt="Image" width="82%" /></div>


<div style="text-align: center;"><div style="text-align: center;">Primary thyroid lymphoma presents as a rapidly enlarging neck mass, as in this patient. The neck mass is often hard with a smooth surface and can be unilateral or bilateral and associated with lymphadenopathy. Affected</div> </div>


patients may be hypothyroid or euthyroid, depending on whether their Hashimoto disease has been recognized and treated and/or whether their Hashimoto thyroiditis has progressed into hypothyroidism. In 1 case series, approximately one-third of patients had an elevated TSH concentration. Unlike differentiated thyroid cancer, an elevated serum TSH concentration per se is not known to be a risk factor for lymphoma development, as might be anticipated given the association of lymphoma with diverse nonthyroid autoimmune diseases. A rapidly growing neck mass with associated compressive symptoms in a patient with Hashimoto hypothyroidism should alert the treating physician to the possibility of thyroid lymphoma, particularly the more aggressive diffuse large B-cell lymphoma. In fact, the most common type of lymphoma seen in association with Hashimoto thyroiditis is diffuse large B-cell lymphoma, which occurs in more than 50% of lymphoma cases.

Measurement of either calcitonin for medullary thyroid carcinoma (Answer B) or thyroglobulin for differentiated thyroid cancer (Answer E) is incorrect, as the FNAB results are not consistent with either of these diagnoses. Securing a tissue diagnosis is the mandatory first step on the basis of the presentation and the patient's history.

##### ANSWER: C) Cortisol and ACTH measurement

Adrenal insufficiency is a rare disorder that can be caused by autoimmune adrenalitis, infectious adrenalitis, hemorrhagic disease, metastatic invasion, and drugs. Autoimmune causes account for approximately 70% to 90% of adrenal insufficiency cases, although worldwide infectious causes are noted at a higher incidence.

<div style="text-align: center;"><img src="imgs/img_in_image_box_38_938_320_1315.jpg" alt="Image" width="23%" /></div>


Adrenal insufficiency is also known as Addison disease, so named after Thomas Addison who first described this condition. In this vignette, symptoms of nausea, weight loss, and unexplained hypoglycemia, along with the finding of moderate hypotension, should alert the physician to consider Addison disease. In addition, female gender and an existing diagnosis of type 1 diabetes make Addison disease a very likely possibility in this patient (polyglandular autoimmune syndrome type 2). In an ill patient, the diagnosis of primary adrenal insufficiency is simply made by measuring random cortisol and ACTH levels (Answer C). Although cortisol levels are low in adrenal insufficiency (as well as in healthy persons in the afternoon and evening hours), healthy persons will have low simultaneous ACTH levels, while persons with adrenal insufficiency will have elevated ACTH levels. ACTH stimulation testing can help confirm the diagnosis. Hyperpigmentation (see image) is associated with chronic ACTH stimulation and may occur in later stages of the disease. Persons with undiagnosed adrenal insufficiency can present with an acute adrenal crisis if they experience increased stress in the form of severe infection, infarction, or surgery.

Polyglandular autoimmune syndromes are characterized by endocrine glandular destruction related to autoimmune processes. Women often develop primary adrenal insufficiency related to polyglandular autoimmune syndromes, whereas men generally develop this condition as an isolated autoimmune process. The age of onset is typically between 20 and 40 years. Polyglandular autoimmune syndrome type 1 (APS 1) is also called autoimmune polyendocrinopathy-candidiasis-ectodermal dystrophy (APECED). This is a rare autosomal recessive disorder due

to mutations in the autoimmune regulator gene (AIRE). Mucocutaneous candidiasis, mainly involving the mouth, nail beds, and hypoparathyroidism causing hypocalcemia are the initial manifestations, usually seen in childhood. Adrenal insufficiency manifests later. APS 1 is a very unlikely diagnosis in this patient given that she has no evidence of hypoparathyroidism or mucocutaneous candidiasis (thus, Answer E is incorrect).

Malabsorptive autoimmune disorders of the gastrointestinal tract are associated with tryptophan hydroxylase antibodies. Celiac disease is a chronic malabsorptive disorder associated with the presence of IgA antibodies to gliadin and endomysium. Clinical manifestations vary from an asymptomatic state to subclinical disease with nonspecific symptoms of fatigue and mild iron deficiency anemia to classic symptoms of flatulence and diarrhea with bulky, foul-smelling, floating stools due to steatorrhea. Malabsorption can result in growth failure in children weight loss, severe anemia, or deficiency of calcium or vitamins B and D. Type 1 diabetes and celiac disease share multiple genetic loci such as HLA-DR3, HLA-DQ2, and HLA-DQ8 and hence are often seen concurrently. Although this patient has weight loss, her hypoglycemia, electrolyte disturbances, and physical findings are not explained by autoimmune bowel disease (thus. Answers B and D are incorrect).

Thyroid disease is an unlikely cause of this patient's symptoms because she has no goiter or other typical findings of hyperthyroidism such as tachycardia or diarrhea. Thyroid disease is not a common factor causing hypoglycemia. Thus, TSH measurement (Answer A) is an incorrect next step.

##### Educational Objective:

Suspect adrenal insufficiency (Addison disease) in a patient with type 1 diabetes mellitus and order appropriate diagnostic testing.

## 23 ANSWER: C) Denosumab

25 In addition to lifestyle changes, including weight-bearing exercise, avoidance of tobacco, and adequate calcium and vitamin D intake, pharmacologic treatment should be offered to this patient, as she is at increased risk for osteoporosis and fracture. Thus, providing reassurance that she needs no further treatment (Answer E) is inappropriate; treatment is warranted.

Aromatase inhibitors such as anastrozole or letrozole work by inhibiting the conversion of adrenal androgens into estrogens, which is the primary source of estrogen in postmenopausal women. Multiple trials have demonstrated aromatase inhibitors produce significant decreases in bone mineral density and increase fracture risk because estrogen deprivation. The greatest decline in bone density occurs in premenopausal women receiving both ovarian suppression therapy (GnRH agonist) and an aromatase inhibitor because of the rapid reduction in endogenous estrogen levels. GnRH agonist (Answer B) would not be appropriate for this patient since she is postmenopausal.

Denosumab (Answer C), a monoclonal antibody against RANKL, increases the mineral density in women undergoing treatment with aromatase inhibitors and is approved for the prevention of aromatase inhibitor–induced bone loss in postmenopausal women. Practical guidance provided by Hadji et al recommends that patients initiating aromatase inhibitors be advised regarding exercise, calcium and vitamin D supplementation, and base bone mineral density monitoring and that bone-directed therapy be initiated if the T score is less than -2.0 at either the hip or the spine or at least 2 fracture risk factors are detected. Patients with a T score greater than -2.0 and the hip or the spine should be followed up closely with sequential measurement of bone mineral density, and therapy

Tamoxifen (Answer A) has beneficial effects on bone mineral density in postmenopausal women, and several trials have compared tamoxifen with aromatase inhibitors and found an increase in fracture rates with aromatase inhibitors compared with fracture rates with tamoxifen. However, tamoxifen in combination with an aromatase inhibitor has not been studied in terms of bone health, nor would this combination be recognized as appropriate therapy for this patient at her stage of disease management.

Calcitonin (Answer D) is a weak antiresorptive agent approved for prevention of lumbar spine fractures in postmenopausal women. The efficacy of calcitonin, when used concurrently with an aromatase inhibitor, has not been studied. Calcitonin has not been shown to alter bone strength at the femoral neck and hence it is not approved for prevention of hip fracture.

##### Educational Objective:

In patients being treated with aromatase inhibitors, recommend bone-directed therapy on the basis of T score and fracture risk factors.

## 24 ANSWER: E) Postpone surgery and begin bromocriptine, 2.5 mg daily

This patient presents with a macroprolactinoma and associated vision loss. The initial treatment of choice for such patients is a dopamine agonist (Answer E). Either bromocriptine as in Answer E or cabergoline, which was not given as a therapeutic option, is an appropriate choice for dopamine agonist therapy. A trial of dopamine agonist for a patient with a clear diagnosis of prolactinoma is warranted before surgery (thus, Answers A, B, and C are incorrect). Dopamine agonists can rapidly shrink the tumor and improve vision. Surgery is reserved for scenarios when patients have severe, rapid vision loss or when pituitary apoplexy is unresponsive to conservative management. Dopamine agonists normalize prolactin levels in about 80% of men with macroprolactinomas and induce clinically significant pituitary tumor shrinkage in up to 90%. Vision impairment often improves within days of initiating dopamine agonist therapy. Prolactinomas, particularly in men, can grow very large before they are recognized, such as the tumor described in this vignette. Prolactinomas often have a characteristic, very invasive appearance on MRI.

High-dosage dexamethasone (Answer B) does not shrink macroprolactinomas and there is no indication for its use in the treatment of secondary adrenal insufficiency given this patient's normal cortisol level. Octreotide (Answer D) is also not indicated because he has a low GH level and no evidence of GH hypersecretion. Octreotide can shrink GH-secreting tumors, but it rarely achieves this rapidly enough to improve vision, so initial management of GH-secreting pituitary tumors, especially those associated with vision loss, is surgery. Radiotherapy after surgery (Answer C) is reserved for patients with known recurrence not amenable to surgical removal or a hormone-secreting tumor that cannot be treated medically.

Manage a macoprolactinoma presenting with vision loss

## 25 ANSWER: B) Increase his levothyroxine dosage This

25 A This patient is concerned about continued weight gain. One potential explanation would be excessive glucocorticoid replacement, which can lead to multiple adverse effects including lower bone mineral density, lower lean muscle mass, and weight gain. Adequacy of supplementation is often determined by subjective clinical parameters that are nonspecific. In patients with cortisol deficiency who take what is deemed to be appropriate replacement therapy, scores in validated self-assessment surveys of wellness (SF36) are consistently lower than scores of age- and sex-matched control participants. The scores are lowest in patients with a central cause for adrenal insufficiency. However, in view of this patient's height and weight, his current steroid dosage is most appropriate and further reductions are not advised.

Testosterone deficiency is associated with changes in body composition such as increases in percentage of body fat and decreases in lean muscle mass. Replacement of gonadal steroids can improve lean muscle mass. However, this intervention alone is not expected to contribute to weight loss (thus, Answer E is incorrect). GH deficiency also contributes to unfavorable changes in body composition (decreased lean muscle mass). However, the decision to initiate GH supplementation is rarely related to weight management (thus, Answer C is incorrect).

A basal metabolic rate measurement via indirect calorimetry determines basal caloric requirements and can guide dietary therapy when patients attempt to lose weight. Because thyroid hormone is a determinant of energy expenditure, measurement of the basal metabolic rate can also provide information regarding the adequacy of thyroid hormone supplementation. This patient's resting energy expenditure was accurately measured. He achieved a steady state at 1844 kcal, representing 69% of his predicted values on the basis of his age, height, weight. Values less than 20% of predicted are consistent with a hypometabolic state. His levothyroxine dosage was therefore increased (Answer B is correct) to 150 mcg daily and subsequently to 175 mcg daily. In addition, regular physical activity, he was advised to limit his caloric intake to 1800 to 2200 kcal daily. Over the subsequent 6 months, he was able to lose 26.5 lb (12 kg). He felt well and motivated to continue his efforts. Another measurement of resting energy expenditure was planned in 6 to 12 months.

Proponents of combined $T_{4}$ and $T_{3}$ supplementation comment on improved patient well-being, yet there are data to support a weight benefit with this combination (thus. Answer D is incorrect).

DHEA is a steroid intermediate in the pathway of adrenal and gonadal steroid biosynthesis. DHEA supplements have been purported to improve a wide array of symptoms including fatigue, muscle strength, and reproductive function. However, it would not facilitate weight loss and would be more likely to be weight-neutral or cause weight gain (thus, Answer A is incorrect).

26 ANSWER: A) insulin, 3.2 µIU/mL (22.3 pmol/L); C-peptide, 4.0 ng/mL (1.3 nmol/L); proinsulin, 16 pg/mL (1.8 pmol/L); glucose rise after glucagon (Δ), 31 mg/dL (1.72 mmol/L); insulin antibodies, negative; sulfonylurea screen, negative

This patient's presentation is strongly suggestive of an insulinoma. Typically, patients with insulinoma report having symptoms for 6 to 18 months before presentation and, contrary to popular belief, marked weight gain does not always occur. The set of laboratory values present in Answer A are most compatible with an insulinoma. In normal circumstances, endogenous production of  $ \beta $-cell polypeptides should cease, as would be expected when glucose concentrations fall below the 60 to 55 mg/dL threshold (3.33 to 3.05 mmol/L). This is certainly not the case in this scenario where there is inappropriate persistence of  $ \beta $-cell polypeptide secretion. Occasionally, patients who are lean and exercise regularly have undetectable insulin at the time of hypoglycemia. However, a diagnosis of endogenous hyperinsulinemic hypoglycemia can be made when taking into account C-peptide and proinsulin concentrations at the time of hypoglycemia. Moreover, noninsulin surrogates such as measurement of  $ \beta $-hydroxybutyrate and the response to glucagon are helpful in strengthening the diagnosis. The pattern of results in Answer A should prompt attempts to localize the tumor before surgical treatment and potential cure. The tumors mediating hypoglycemia in circumstances such as one presented in this vignette are rarely occult and are easily detected on imaging.

Diagnosing an insulinoma requires the use of an oral hypoglycemic screen capable of detecting fourth-generation sulfonylureas and agents such as repaglinide. Otherwise, use of these agents produces results that are biochemically indistinguishable from those due to an insulinoma. Documenting the use of such agents, as indicated in the results of Answer B, would not prompt further imaging studies or surgical treatment.

The use of exogenous insulin to produce factitious hypoglycemia would produce a biochemical picture compatible with Answer C and would not be an indication for localization studies. A response to glucagon implies the presence of insulin, or an insulinlike factor, which protects hepatic glycogen from glycogenolysis to glucose. This is likely variable in these circumstances and dependent on the timing and dosing of insulin before the development of hypoglycemia. Nevertheless, the presence of elevated insulin (marked) with suppression of the other β-cell polypeptides strongly suggests the use of exogenous insulin.

Insulin antibody-mediated hypoglycemia would explain the laboratory data in Answer D. Indeed, this is the only condition likely to explain such a marked elevation in insulin and the accompanying  $ \beta $-cell polypeptides. This would not require localization studies.

Answer E is compatible with a normal response to a fast. The insulin in this case is at the limit of detectability, but this illustrates several important points. First, since the Endocrine Society guidelines were written, immunoassays and their normal ranges and performance characteristics have changed, so it is important to understand that those values are not to be adhered to blindly. Second, results from the accompanying laboratory tests all strongly suggest that there is no inappropriate insulin secretion at the time of hypoglycemia. Third, when looking at β-cell polypeptides, it is important to appreciate that concentrations represent the net sum of secretion, distribution, and clearance. Given the long half-lives of proinsulin and C-peptide, the fact that these are not detectable bolsters the impression that endogenous insulin secretion was not present at the time the fast ended.

## 27 ANSWER: A) Polycystic ovary syndrome

27 ANSWER: A) This patient had irregular menses before starting hormonal contraception and now has amenorrhea after stopping. Therefore, it is likely that the underlying problem that caused irregular menses at puberty is now resulting in amenorrhea. An evidence-based methodology workshop examining polycystic ovary syndrome recommended that the Rotterdam criteria be used to make the diagnosis. Using these criteria, patients must have 2 out of 3 of the following symptoms or signs to make the diagnosis: (1) hyperandrogenism, either clinical (hirsutism or acne) or biochemical (elevated testosterone); (2) irregular menses; and/or (3) polycystic ovaries of ultrasonography, in the absence of other disorders causing the same symptoms. The patient in this vignette has amenorrhea and hyperandrogenism, as manifested by an elevated testosterone concentration and acne. Polycystic ovary syndrome (Answer A) is on the differential diagnosis list, but it can only be diagnosed after other causes of amenorrhea and hyperandrogenism have been excluded.

Distinguishing between polycystic ovary syndrome and functional hypothalamic amenorrhea (Answer C) can be difficult. This patient has amenorrhea in the setting of training for a half-marathon, suggesting that her increased exercise caused the amenorrhea. However, functional hypothalamic amenorrhea is caused by low energy availability, which suppresses GnRH pulse secretion. The decreased GnRH pulsatility results in decreased LH levels with a normal FSH level, such that FSH is higher than LH. The low LH does not stimulate androstenedione and testosterone production from theca cells, and estradiol is low because of the absence of androgen precursor for aromatization. In the vignette, the patient has an elevated LH-to-FSH ratio, an elevated testosterone concentration, and an estradiol concentration in normal range—this pattern of test results is not consistent with functional hypothalamic amenorrhea.

In addition to hypothalamic amenorrhea, other causes of irregular menses must be excluded to diagnose polycystic ovary syndrome. The patient has a normal FSH level, which excludes primary ovarian insufficiency, which was previously termed premature ovarian failure (Answer E). The prolactin and TSH levels are normal, which excludes hyperprolactinemia and thyroid disease. There is no pregnancy test reported, but if she were pregnant, her estradiol level would be higher and her FSH and LH would be suppressed, so pregnancy (Answer D) is an unlikely cause of her amenorrhea. Other causes of hyperandrogenism must be excluded by history or by laboratory testing. The long history of acne starting at puberty is not consistent with an androgen-secreting tumor. The normal 17-hydroxyprogesterone level rules out nonclassic congenital adrenal hyperplasia; and there is no evidence for Cushing syndrome on physical examination.

Hormonal contraception will not cause amenorrhea after it has been stopped. At the hormone dosages current available, “postpill amenorrhea” (Answer B) is unlikely. FSH begins to rise in the 7 days of the placebo week and follicles can grow in the short interval. Therefore, there is no prolonged suppression of the reproductive axis after stopping hormonal contraception. Taken together, other causes of irregular menses and hyperandrogenism have been ruled out and polycystic ovary syndrome is the correct diagnosis (Answer A).

##### Educational Objective:

Differentiate among the causes of amenorrhea in a woman who has stopped hormonal contraception.

## 28 ANSWER: E) High-resolution and color-flow Doppler ultrasonography The use of amiodarone, an iodine-containing drug (27%)

The use of amiodarone, an iodine-containing drug (37% of its molecular weight), is complicated by the occurrence of thyroid dysfunction in 15% to 20% of cases, either in the form of thyrotoxicosis (amiodarone-

induced thyrotoxicosis [AIT] or hypothyroidism. AIT is more common in iodine-deficient areas and in men, at variance with spontaneous hyperthyroidism, whereas hypothyroidism is more common in iodine-sufficient areas. For a diagnosis of AIT, serum free  $ T_{4} $ and free  $ T_{3} $ concentrations must be increased and serum TSH must be suppressed. Two main forms of AIT exist: type 1 AIT, which is iodine-induced hyperthyroidism, and type 2 AIT, a destructive thyroiditis caused by toxic effects of amiodarone on thyroid cells. However, the 2 mechanisms may occur in the same patient, resulting in mixed or indefinite forms of AIT. Type 1 occurs in patients predisposed to thyroid autoimmunity or in those with preexisting thyroid nodules, whereas type 2 usually develops in patients without clinical, biochemical, and morphologic evidence of thyroid disease. AIT may develop early during amiodarone treatment, but it may also occur several months after drug withdrawal since amiodarone and its metabolites have a long half-life (up to 100 days) and are stored in tissues, most notably fat, from which they are released very slowly. Diagnostic testing to distinguish between the 2 forms is advisable because the treatment differs—type 1 AIT is treated with antithyroid drugs and type 2 AIT is treated with corticosteroids. Combined antithyroid drug and anti-inflammatory treatment is required in those who fail to respond to single modality treatment and in those in whom the type of the disease cannot be unequivocally determined. It is likely that subsequent therapy for the underlying thyroid disease is required in those with type 1 AIT, whereas subsequent hypothyroidism may ensue in those with type 2 AIT. The decision to stop amiodarone in the setting of thyrotoxicosis should be determined on an individual basis in consultation with the cardiologist, based on the presence or absence of effective antiarrhythmic therapy.

This patient most likely has type 2 AIT, the most common form, because he has no apparent predisposing factors to thyroid autoimmunity or any obvious thyroid abnormalities on clinical examination. Moreover, the very low uptake of radioactive iodine on  $ {}^{123} $I scanning is typically seen in patients with type 2 AIT, whereas low-normal, normal, or even increased uptake may be seen in type 1 AIT, despite the iodine load. High-resolution thyroid ultrasonography will be informative in terms of determining the presence of a diffuse or nodular goiter, even in the absence of clinically detectable thyroid enlargement or nodularity. Additionally, color-flow Doppler sonography (Answer E) is a very important diagnostic tool that usually indicates an absent hypervascular pattern in type 2 AIT in contrast to the increased vascularity and blood flow velocity in type 1 AIT. In general, determination of uptake of radioiodine and color-flow Doppler sonography are considered the most useful diagnostic techniques in the initial assessment.

The search for thyroid-directed autoantibodies is likely to be informative regarding the presence of underlying autoimmunity, and the determination of TSH-receptor antibodies is relevant only in patients with AIT whose underlying and preexisting thyroid disorder is Graves disease. Because the patient's history and the radioiodine uptake scan point towards type 2 AIT, Answer A would be less informative than color-flow Doppler sonography.

The measurement of urinary iodine excretion (Answer B) is of limited value, as this will be high because of the high iodine content of amiodarone. It would not aid in the identification of the underlying cause of thyrotoxicosis in this case.

Some centers advocate the use of technetium-99m 2-methoxyisobutyl isonitrile scintigraphy (Tc-99m-MIBI) (Answer C) as a useful diagnostic tool. In a study of 20 consecutive patients with AIT, MIBI diffuse retention, which is indicative of hyperfunctioning tissue, was present in all patients with type 1 AIT. No significant uptake, suggestive of a destructive process, was found in patients with type 2 AIT. In those with mixed or indefinite forms, either faint persistence of MIBI uptake or a quick washout of the tracer was observed. The real usefulness of this expensive and complicated procedure in the differential diagnosis of AIT must be confirmed in larger studies, especially with respect to the diagnosis of more complex and mixed forms of AIT.

Interleukin 6 and C-reactive protein are markers of inflammation and are usually elevated in type 2 AIT. They may also be elevated in patients with type 1 AIT and in patients with subacute thyroiditis. In view of their lack of specificity, their measurement is less useful in this setting (thus, Answer D is incorrect).

##### Educational Objective:

Distinguish between type 1 (iodine-induced) and type 2 (thyroiditis) amiodarone-induced thyrotoxicosis.

## 29 ANSWER: A) Elemental calcium, 2000 mg daily

29 After bariatric surgery, the loss of bone mineral density documented on DXA scan is relatively common. In patient, some of the bone loss seen on DXA is artifact due to extreme changes in fat mass. DXA measurement is limited in the obese population because of the inability to discriminate between cortical and trabecular bone. Indeed studies done with quantitative CT tend to reveal stable bone mass, suggesting that the DXA results may be artifact. However, markers of bone turnover (both formation and resorption) increase markedly after weight-reduction surge and hence the potential for clinically significant bone loss is a possible adverse outcome with these procedures.

Bone loss associated with bariatric surgery is multifactorial. There is a strong association between the low weight loss and loss of bone mineral density in weight-bearing sites including the hip and femoral neck, while is postulated to be due to adaptive changes in skeletal unloading. At baseline, morbidly obese patients have low vitamin D levels because of reduced bioavailability from sequestration into fat tissue. Malabsorption of vitamin D is also found in patients with other nutrients, after bariatric surgery is well recognized. Vitamin D dosages needed to restore normal levels after bariatric surgery can be extremely large, perhaps up to 15,000 or 20,000 IU daily. Ergocalciferol (vitamin D₃) comes in a 50,000 IU preparation and is often used for high-dosage replacement.

This patient has secondary hyperparathyroidism with a normal serum 25-hydroxyvitamin D level. Thus, additional vitamin D (Answers B) is not needed. However, the urinary calcium excretion is exceedingly low and the secondary hyperparathyroidism is thus due to calcium deficiency. Calcium transporters are most prevalent in the duodenum; this is the area of best absorption of both dietary calcium and supplements. After Roux-en-Y bariatric surgery, the duodenum is bypassed. Current guidelines recommend up to 1500 mg of calcium daily a minimum of 3000 IU of vitamin D daily to avoid bone loss. In this patient with declining bone density and calmalabsorption, a higher dosage of calcium such as 2000 mg daily or more, may be needed (Answer A is correct).

Secondary hyperparathyroidism would not be treated with parathyroidectomy. PTH rise is in response to the calcium deficiency, not the primary problem. Calcium receptor mimetics such as cinacalcet can reduce PTH levels but do not address the inherent process causing the problem. Indeed, calcium receptor mimetics reduce calcium levels and could cause clinical hypocalcemia in this patient with circulating calcium levels already at the lower end of the normal range (thus, Answer D is incorrect).

One should not institute antiresorptive therapy with alendronate (Answer C) or any other bisphosphonate the presence of either calcium or vitamin D deficiency.

##### Educational Objective:

Identify calcium deficiency seen in the setting of obesity and bariatric surgery as a common cause of secondary hyperparathyroidism.

Bariatric surgery: Postoperative nutritional management

Sánchez-Hernández J, Ybarra J, Olчи I, et al. Effects of bariatric surgery on vitamin D status and secondary hyperparathyroidism: a prospective study. 2005;15(10):1389-1395.

Yu EW, Bouxsein M, Roy AE, et al. Bone loss after bariatric surgery: discordant results between DXA and QCT bone density. J Bone Miner Res. 2014;29(3):542-550.

## 30 ANSWER: A) Reduced hemoglobin  $ A_{1c} $

Insulin pump therapy has become an increasingly popular mode of insulin delivery in patients with type 1 diabetes since its successful use in the Diabetes Control and Complications Trial. Benefits of continuous subcutaneous insulin infusion (CSII) include adjustable basal insulin dosing, allowing for changes in basal rates depending on time of day and physical activity levels. Insulin pumps also allow use of very small increments of insulin down to 0.05 units, which is useful in highly insulin-sensitive patients, such as the woman presented in this vignette. In clinical trials comparing CSII with multiple daily injections, the benefits of pump therapy are dependent on the types of comparator injectable insulin.

In adults, studies comparing CSII with basal insulin analogue glargine- and lispro-based multiple daily injection regimens predominantly show reductions in hemoglobin  $ A_{1c} $ with CSII (thus, Answer A is correct), but not in frequency of or severity of hypoglycemia (thus, Answer E is incorrect). In these studies, fasting glucose levels are similar in the 2 groups because of insulin titration protocols targeting a fasting glucose goal. A long-term case-control study in children showed a reduction in both total and severe hypoglycemia in CSII-treated patients vs multiple daily injection-treated patients. A 10% to 20% insulin dosage reduction is commonly recommended when insulin pump therapy is initiated in patients with only mildly elevated hemoglobin  $ A_{1c} $ levels. Reductions in the total daily insulin dose can be seen in some patients when compared with the dose in multiple daily injection-treated patients, perhaps because of more consistent insulin absorption from a single subcutaneous insulin depot in pump therapy (thus, Answer D is incorrect). Overall, ketoacidosis rates are not higher in patients treated with insulin pump therapy vs multiple daily injections, although ketoacidosis can develop more rapidly in patients treated only with short- or rapid-acting insulin should the insulin infusion be interrupted (thus, Answer C is incorrect).

Properly dosed basal rates using pump therapy or basal glargine or detemir insulins do not precipitate hypoglycemia if a meal is missed or delayed (thus, Answer B is incorrect).

##### Educational Objective:

Counsel a patient with type 1 diabetes mellitus on potential outcomes of switching from a multiple daily injection regimen to insulin pump therapy.

Insulin pump therapy in adults with type 1 diabetes mellitus

##### Reference(s):

31 ANSWER: C) Switch from the combined oral contraceptive pill to transdermal estrogen and progestin GH-mediated stimulation of hepatic IGF-1 production can be influenced by gonadal steroids; specifically, oral estrogen is a negative regulator of this process. Therefore, continuation of oral estrogen replacement in this patient could reduce the effectiveness of GH therapy or result in a requirement for a higher than necessary GH dosage to achieve satisfactory IGF-1 levels. This negative effect on IGF-1 production is less evident when estrogen is administered transdermally (thus, Answer C is correct and Answer A is incorrect).

In contrast, there is evidence that androgen replacement may stimulate IGF-1 production. Therefore, DHEA (Answer E) can be associated with a decrease in the GH dosage required to reach satisfactory IGF-1 levels. However, this is not an indication to start DHEA replacement. Indeed, the Endocrine Society Clinical Practice Guideline on androgen therapy in women recommends against making such a diagnosis because there is no clearly defined clinical syndrome associated with androgen deficiency in women, the natural history of androgen production in healthy women is unclear, and there is no consistent benefit associated with androgen replacement in clinical trials. Finally, in the United States, DHEA is widely available in over-the-counter formulations, which has implications for quality control.

GH replacement lowers serum cortisol levels, probably due to enhanced conversion of cortisone to cortisol in the GH-deficient state, thus unmasking central hypoadrenalism. Therefore, it is important to ensure that the hypothalamic-pituitary-adrenal axis is normal before commencing GH therapy. However, if the patient is already on an adequate dosage of corticosteroid replacement (and this patient is), there is no automatic requirement to adjust glucocorticoid replacement dosages (Answer B).

Finally, it has been demonstrated that GH replacement can lower serum free  $ T_{4} $ levels, perhaps due to increased deiodination of  $ T_{4} $. Thus, free  $ T_{4} $ levels should be monitored during GH treatment, and the levothyroxine dosage may need to be increased rather than decreased (although this is not often necessary) (thus, Answer D is incorrect).

Assess whether adjustment of other endocrine replacement therapies is necessary in a patient with panhypopitற்றal, who is commencing growth hormone treatment.
