Porretti S, Giavoli C, Ronchi C, et al. Recombinant human GH replacement therapy and thyroid function in a large group of adult GH-deficient patients who does L-T(4) therapy become mandatory? J Clin Endocrinol Metab. 2002;87(5):2042-2045.

## 32 ANSWER: E) Congenital adrenal hypoplasia

The combination of primary adrenal insufficiency and pubertal delay make congenital adrenal hypoplasia (Answer E) the most likely diagnosis. This is a rare disorder that occurs in approximately 1 in 12,500 live births and can present in 4 different ways:

1. Sporadic form, associated with pituitary hypoplasia

2. Autosomal recessive form

3. X-linked form, associated with hypogonadotropic hypogonadism

4. X-linked form, associated with glycerol kinase deficiency, Duchenne muscular dystrophy, and psychomotor retardation

This vignette illustrates an example of an X-linked form of congenital adrenal hypoplasia. Although congenital adrenal hypoplasia often presents in the neonatal period, the X-linked form may not present until in later childhood, adolescence, and even early adulthood. X-linked congenital adrenal hypoplasia is caused by deletion or mutations in the NROB1 gene (also known as DAX1), which is located on the short arm of the X chromosome. This part of the X chromosome also includes the glycerol kinase and Duchenne muscular dystrophy loci that may be deleted, resulting in more severe clinical consequences (although not in this clinical vignette).

The protein encoded by NR0B1, DAX1, is a nuclear receptor protein that is fundamental to adrenal and gonadal development and is expressed in the pituitary, hypothalamus, adrenals, and gonads. It acts as a dominant negative regulator of transcription of other nuclear receptors such as SF1 and is an antagonist of the protein encoded by SRY, a nuclear protein responsible for male sexual development. The disorder presents with adrenal insufficiency during the neonatal period in sporadic and autosomal recessive forms or later in life in the X-linked forms. Hypogonadotropic hypogonadism develops at puberty and is due to a combination of gonadal, pituitary, Sertoli-cell defects that develop because of DAX1 deficiency.

In a retrospective review of 103 children with adrenal insufficiency, the following causes were reported: congenital adrenal hyperplasia, 73%; Addison disease, 13%; peroxisomal disorders, 5%; lysosomal acid lipase deficiency (Wolman disease), 3%; unknown cause, 6%; and adrenal hypoplasia, 1%. Thus, adrenal insufficiency childhood is most often due to classic congenital adrenal hyperplasia.

In this vignette, a diagnosis of classic congenital adrenal hyperplasia due to 21α-hydroxylase deficiency (Answer D) is incorrect because this always presents in the neonatal period rather than during adolescence. In addition, testosterone production should not be affected by 21α-hydroxylase deficiency. However, affected patients can develop primary hypogonadism due to development to testicular rest tissue. There is no evidence of this clinically in the vignette and the biochemistry suggests secondary gonadal failure. Classic congenital adrenal hyperplasia caused by 11β-hydroxylase deficiency (Answer C) is also incorrect because this would present in infancy with adrenal failure and mineralocorticoid hypertension. Testosterone production is unaffected.

Although autoimmune adrenal failure and gonadal failure (autoimmune polyglandular syndrome) (Answer A) can present in children, primary adrenal failure at a young age should make the endocrinologist suspicious of a more unusual underlying cause. In addition, primary gonadal failure would occur in autoimmune polyglandular syndrome and this would be associated with elevated gonadotropin levels, which this patient does not have.

Peroxisomal disorders (adrenoleukodystrophy [Answer B] and adrenomyeloneuropathy) are rare X-linked disorders of β-oxidation that result in accumulation of very long chain fatty acids in tissues. They can present with adrenal insufficiency alone or in combination with spastic paraparesis (in adrenomyeloneuropathy) or severe neurologic features including quadriparesis, blindness, and severe cognitive dysfunction (in adrenoleukodystrophy). Adrenoleukodystrophy is an incorrect diagnosis given the lack of cognitive or neurologic features that would typically be seen by age 14 years. Although adrenal insufficiency can rarely be the only clinical feature of adrenoleukodystrophy, the presence of secondary hypogonadism makes congenital adrenal hypoplasia a more likely diagnosis.

Reuters AT, Achermann JC, Ito M, et al. Clinical and functional effects of mutations in the DAX-1 gene in patients with adrenal hypoplasia congenita. J Clin Endocrinol Metab. 1999;84(2):504-511.

##### ANSWER: A) Indicated and should be initiated now

This patient seeks advice for primary prevention of cardiovascular disease. He has several cardiovascular disease risk factors including hypertension, age, sex, and ethnicity. Although his family history is unknown, he does not smoke cigarettes and he is reasonably physically active, he still has a 10-year risk of a cardiovascular event greater than 10% regardless of whether the Adult Treatment Panel III (ATP III) or the new American Heart Association/American College of Cardiology (AHA/ACC) Omnibus risk calculator is used (mobile apps are available for use on smart phones to facilitate simple calculation of these risks). The recent analysis by the expert panel that wrote the AHA/ACC guidelines recommends moving away from threshold-based triggers for initiation of therapy because the primary prevention trials clearly demonstrate that treating individuals at risk for cardiovascular disease with statins has similar relative risk reductions in essentially all primary prevention groups studied. The absolute benefit in terms of cardiovascular risk reduction depends on the baseline absolute risk for cardiovascular disease, not the specific lipid levels. Thus, the recommendation of both the new AHA/ACC guidelines and the previous ATP III guidelines is that statin therapy should be considered for an individual with 10-year risk greater than 10% (≥7.5% in the AHA/ACC guidelines) (thus, Answer A is correct).

Although statins do have adverse effects including excess diabetes rates, the potential benefits of statin therapy to reduce cardiovascular events are significantly greater than the potential harm. Adverse effects observed with statin therapy depend on the agent and dosage. Per 100 statin-treated patients each year, there are 0.1 to 0.3 excess

cases of diabetes, 0.01 excess cases of myopathy, and 0.01 excess cases of hemorrhagic stroke. Thus, the relations of diabetes, 0.01 excess cases of myopathy, and 0.01 excess cases of hemorrhagic stroke. Thus, the relations of diabetes, 0.01 excess cases of myopathy, and 0.01 excess cases of hemorrhagic stroke. This patient does not have any indication for a fibrate (thus, Answer B is incorrect).

## 34 ANSWER: D) Change from pregabalin to duloxetine therapy

34 A. Approximately one-half of persons with diabetes develop peripheral neuropathy at some point in the course of their disease. The most common clinical manifestations are symptoms such as numbness and pain, typically symmetric and developing in the feet but later advancing proximally. Weakness due to motor involvement may occur but is less common than sensory deficits. Not all individuals with peripheral neuropathy experience neuropathic pain; however, when this does occur, it may adversely affect functional status and quality of life. Approximately 10% to 20% of persons with diabetes have painful peripheral neuropathy, although this may be an underestimate because patients do not consistently report their discomfort. Thus, many patients with clinical significant neuropathic pain are most likely untreated or inadequately treated.

Painful diabetic peripheral neuropathy is a result of damage to small, unmyelinated fibers rather than the large myelinated fibers responsible for proprioception and touch. Thus, as is the case for this patient, marked neuropathic damage may be present despite preservation of the latter senses. Normal responses to testing with monofilament, tuning fork, or even electrophysiologic testing do not exclude the possibility of painful diabetic neuropathy. Hyperglycemia contributes to the development of neuropathy in both type 1 and type 2 diabetes, the pathophysiology that leads to this complication is most likely quite complex. In type 2 diabetes, concomitant conditions including hypertension, dyslipidemia, and insulin resistance at the level of the neuron are probably contributory. More intensive glycemic control appears to significantly reduce the development of neuropathy in persons with type 1 diabetes, but it has not been consistently documented to be as successful in preventing this complication in type 2 diabetes. Importantly, improvement in hemoglobin  $ A_{1c} $ does not reduce neuropathic pain and, in fact, very rapid normalization of glycemia may in some instances worsen pain. Although intensification this patient's glycemic control (Answer A) may be otherwise indicated, this intervention would not be expected to improve his neuropathic symptoms.

Recent evidence-based guidelines for the treatment of painful diabetic neuropathy have been outlined by groups including the American Academies of Neurology, the American Association of Neuromuscular and Electrodiagnostic Medicine, and the American Association of Physical Medicine and Rehabilitation. This set of guidelines and subsequently published review articles describe several first-line classes of drugs as effective treatment of painful diabetic neuropathy. These include voltage-gated calcium channel  $ \alpha_{2}\delta $ ligand anticonvulsant (pregabalin and gabapentin), serotonin-norepinephrine reuptake inhibitors (venlafaxine and duloxetine), and tricyclic antidepressants. Of these agents, pregabalin and duloxetine are approved by the US Food and Drug Administration for the treatment of painful diabetic peripheral neuropathy. Any of these drug classes is appropriate to use as initial therapy for the management of this condition. If on the maximally effective and/or tolerated dosage of one such medication there is no improvement in neuropathic pain, drug therapy should be changed in a first-line agent of a different drug class. This patient has not experienced any noticeable relief of pain with the maximal appropriate dosage of pregabalin; thus, the addition of duloxetine (Answer B) or amitriptyline (Answer C)

C) would not be the best next steps in management. However, if the patient had experienced partial relief of symptoms (described by some authors as a 30% or greater reduction in pain), then the addition of either of these agents to pregabalin therapy would be indicated. Discontinuing the patient's pregabalin, as it was ineffective, and substituting duloxetine therapy (Answer D) would be the most appropriate next step because duloxetine is a first-line agent from a different drug class. Discontinuing pregabalin and substituting gabapentin (Answer E), even though gabapentin is considered an effective first-line agent, does not represent the best alternative therapy because pregabalin and gabapentin are drugs of the same class.

Identify effective interventions for the management of painful diabetic neuropathy.

## 35 ANSWER: E) No additional therapy now

This young man presented with gigantism, which is defined as acromegaly due to a GH-secreting pituitary tumor that presents in a patient younger than age 18 years. Postoperatively, his IGF-1 level has been falling, although it is not yet normal 2 months after surgery. His random GH level is low, but this alone is not used to distinguish remission from disease persistence. Only a random GH level that is very low, and less than the glucose-suppressed GH cutoff expected for remission, could be used to support a remission. Given his decreasing IGF-1 level and other aspects of his surgical and postoperative course, this patient is most likely in remission. IGF-1 levels often continue to fall during the first 3 months after successful resection of GH-secreting pituitary tumors, and some patients who achieve remission do not have a normal IGF-1 level until even longer after surgery. In a patient with a decreasing IGF-1 level and a GH level suggestive of remission, continued monitoring of IGF-1 and GH should be instituted and additional treatment of acromegaly should not be started too early after surgery. IGF-1 levels fall with age after puberty, so the upper normal limit of IGF-1 will fall every year for this young man. If he is truly in remission, this will be proven with continued follow-up. In another month (3 months postoperatively), IGF-1 and glucose-suppressed GH should be measured again. His glucose-suppressed GH level obtained 3 months after surgery should be less than 0.5 ng/mL (0.5 μg/L) when measured with most commercially available GH assays. Even without certainty about his disease status now, his clinical course should be followed without intervention for the time being (thus, Answer E is correct and Answers A, B, C, and D are incorrect).

A number of pitfalls exist when interpreting GH and IGF-1 levels both for diagnosis and determining remission in patients with acromegaly. Discrepant test results, with one hormone being elevated and the other being normal, is fairly common and can make determination of disease status difficult. In the diagnostic evaluation for acromegaly or in the evaluation of postoperative remission in a patient with known acromegaly, it is important to consider other conditions that can lead to discrepant GH and IGF-1 results. IGF-1 levels are only rarely elevated in conditions other than acromegaly, but they can be high in the setting of puberty, pregnancy, or hyperthyroidism. IGF-1 levels can be low in the context of hypothyroidism, malnutrition, poorly controlled type 1 diabetes mellitus, liver failure, renal failure, and oral estrogen use. GH can be elevated and fail to suppress normally in the absence of an acromegaly diagnosis in patients with liver disease, renal insufficiency, malnutrition, anorexia nervosa, or hyperthyroidism, as well as during adolescence or in patients taking oral estrogen.

Educational Objective: Guide postoperative management of acromegaly on the basis of the biochemical evaluation and describe the pitfalls of testing.

Freda PU. Monitoring of acromegaly: what should be performed when GH and IGF-I levels are discrepant? Clin Endocrinol (Oxf). 2009;71(2):166-170. Feelders RA, Bidingmaier M, Strasburger CJ, et al. Postoperative evaluation of patients with acromegaly: clinical significance and timing of oral glucose tolerance testing and measurement of (free) insulin-like growth factor I, acid-labile subunit, and growth hormone-binding protein levels. ## 36 ANSWER: C) Measure transferrin saturation and ferritin concentration

36 ANSWER: Men with osteoporosis should be evaluated for secondary causes because in almost half of cases, a modern condition causing bone loss is identified. Although this patient reports fatigue and has a history of hypothyroidism, the is on levothyroxine replacement, and his serum TSH and free T₄ concentrations are normal. In patients with hemochromatosis (as is the case here), secondary hypothyroidism is possible and the TSH level may be misleadingly low. With a normal free T₄ level, the diagnosis of secondary hypothyroidism is excluded. Moreover, secondary hypothyroidism is uncommon and without signs of hypothyroidism on examination (bradycardia, delayed relaxation phase of reflexes, etc), this diagnosis would be highly unlikely. Measuring free T₄ levels (Answer A) would probably not provide additional diagnostic information and would not explain osteoporosis, hypogonadism, and new-onset diabetes. Measuring hemoglobin A₁c (Answer B) would better characterize his diabetes control, but would not provide a unifying diagnosis. Likewise, measuring free testosterone (Answer D) would not be appropriate to evaluate for an adenoma, mass, and infiltrative causes of central hypogonadism with resultant osteoporosis. Involvement of only the pituitary would not explain the new-onset diabetes.

Hemochromatosis is a hereditary disorder that results in iron deposition in multiple organs, including the endocrine glands. Diabetes mellitus, both primary and secondary hypogonadism, secondary hypothyroidism, and hepatic dysfunction can all result from iron overload. In fact, this patient has an elevated fasting glucose concentration, consistent with β-cell destruction by iron overload. Iron accumulation also occurs in the pituitary, which causes secondary hypogonadism, reflected by low FSH and LH levels, as observed in this patient. Additionally, there are reported cases of testicular failure due to gonadal iron deposition. The hypogonadism results from hemochromatosis most likely contributes to osteoporosis, and this patient should receive testosterone replacement, at least until testicular function may be restored. Depending on the severity and duration of untreated hemochromatosis, pituitary function may return with iron-depletion strategies. Interestingly, the rates of osteoporosis and fractures are higher than would be predicted by hypogonadism alone, and can even occur in the absence of hypogonadism. Patients with hemochromatosis have a higher incidence of osteopenia and osteoporosis as well as an increased fracture rate. Phlebotomy and reduction of iron accumulation can improve bone mineral density, and proper diagnosis is crucial. A fasting transferrin saturation and ferritin concentration (Answer C) should be assessed.

##### Educational Objective:

Evaluate for hemochromatosis in a patient with osteoporosis in the setting of hypogonadism and diabetes mellitus

## 37 ANSWER: C) Presence of TPO antibodies

37 Subclinical hypothyroidism is diagnosed when a patient has an elevated serum TSH value accompanied by normal thyroid hormone levels. The natural history of subclinical hypothyroidism is particularly affected by 2 factors: the magnitude of the serum TSH elevation and the presence or absence of TPO antibodies.

Regarding TSH elevation, the study showed that 52% of patients documented to have baseline serum TSH values that were mildly elevated (5.0-9.9 mIU/L) had normal TSH values at the end of a 3-year period. The percentage of baseline TSH values in the ranges of 10.0 to 14.9 mIU/L and 15.0 to 19.9 mIU/L that normalized after 3 years in the same study were 13% and 5%, respectively. These observations have been confirmed in other studies. For example, in a study of patients older than 65 years, resolution of subclinical hypothyroidism occurred at the 2-year follow-up in 46% of those with TSH values between 4.5 and 6.9 mIU/L, in 10% of those with values between 7.0 and 9.9 mIU/L, and in 7% of those with values of 10.0 mIU/L or greater.

Nevertheless, there is an increased risk of developing overt hypothyroidism even with successively increasing baseline TSH values that fall within the normal range. This risk is affected by TPO antibody status. A study examined the risk of developing overt hypothyroidism during a longer follow-up period of 13 years according to initial TSH groupings of less than 2.5 mIU/L, 2.5 to 4.0 mIU/L, and greater than 4.0 mIU/L in patients with positive thyroid antibodies. Compared with an odds ratio of 1.4 in the group with TSH values less than 2.5 mIU/L, the odds ratio for hypothyroidism was 10 in the group with TSH values between 2.5 and 4.0 mIU/L and 108 for the group with TSH values greater than 4.0 mIU/L. The odds ratios were lower (1, 1.8, and 50, respectively) for the same TSH groups in patients without thyroid antibodies. Thus, some patients with mild TSH elevation, such as this patient, progress to overt hypothyroidism over prolonged periods and monitoring of thyroid function is indicated. A prospective study showed that the calculated risk ratios of overt hypothyroidism in the 3 TSH categories of less than 6.0 mIU/L, 6.0 to 12.0 mIU/L, and greater than 12.0 mIU/L were 1.2, 6.4, and 11.5, respectively, over 10 years of follow-up. These risk ratios increased to 5.3, 10.5, and 15.6 in patients with markedly elevated TPO antibody titers. Similarly, in one of the aforementioned studies, progression to overt hypothyroidism occurred with greater frequency in those with positive thyroid autoantibodies. Conversely, another study demonstrated that resolution of subclinical hypothyroidism is less likely in patients older than 65 years who have positive TPO antibodies than in those with negative antibodies (15% vs 48% of patients) (thus, Answer C is correct). The results of such studies have led to the recommendation to consider measurement of TPO antibodies as part of the evaluation of patients who have subclinical hypothyroidism.

However, in all these studies, patient age did not appear to predict progression to overt hypothyroidism (thus, Answer B is incorrect). Patient sex does not affect the likelihood of progression in some studies, although other studies do find a greater risk of progression in women. The reason for these discrepant findings with respect to sex may relate to the association between female sex and TPO antibodies, such that they are not independent factors. In the studies that have examined BMI, this factor did not affect the risk of developing overt hypothyroidism (thus, Answer E is incorrect). In some studies, the presence of a goiter increases the risk of future hypothyroidism. In one study of populations with different iodine intakes, a high-iodine intake is a further risk factor for development of overt hypothyroidism in patients with TPO antibodies (thus, Answer D is incorrect). Smoking has either been found to have no influence on progression to overt hypothyroidism or has been associated with less likelihood of having TPO antibodies (thus, Answer A is incorrect).

##### Educational Objective:

Explain the natural history of subclinical hypothyroidism and determine the factors most likely to affect its progression to overt hypothyroidism.

38 ANSWER: E) Genetic testing for a KCNJ11 mutation (ATP-sensitive potassium channel gene)

This patient initially presented with acute hyperglycemia consistent with the diagnosis of neonatal diabetes is categorized as transient or permanent. It is defined as permanent hyperglycemia if it occurs in the first few months of life, lasts more than 2 weeks, and requires medication for management. This patient has permanent neonatal diabetes on the basis of his history. The course of this condition and duration of hyperglycemia are dictated by the underlying genetic defect.

Mutations in the gene encoding the ATP-sensitive potassium channel (KCNJ11) (Answer E) can cause permanent neonatal diabetes mellitus, whereas mutations in the gene encoding the sulfonylurea receptor subunit (SUR1) (ABCC8) can result in either permanent or transient neonatal disease. The mutations that activate the ATP-sensitive potassium channel cause hyperpolarization of the  $ \beta $ cells, thereby preventing the release of insulin. The potassium ATP channel is composed of a small subunit (Kir6.2) that surrounds a central pore and 4 regulatory proteins with a KCNJ11 mutation may be small-for-gestational-age and have a constellation of SORBS1 subunits. Patients with a KCNJ11 mutation may be small-for-gestational-age and have a constellation of SORBS1 subunits. Patients with a KCNJ11 mutation may be small-for-gestational-age and have a constellation of SORBS1 subunits. Patients with a KCNJ11 mutation may

Most infants with transient neonatal diabetes have either paternal uniparental disomy of chromosome 6 or an unbalanced duplication of paternal chromosome 6. Another cause of transient neonatal diabetes is a mutation in the imprinted gene ZAC/PLAGL1, a transcriptional regulator of the type 1 receptor for pituitary adenylate cyclase-activating polypeptide, which is an important regulator of insulin secretion. PLAGL1 is located on chromosome 6q24. Neonatal diabetes mellitus is also associated with mutations in other genes including RFX6, PDX1 (previously IPF1), EIF2AK3, FOXP3, PTF1A, GLIS3, and INS. Mutations in some of these genes result in pancreatic agenesis or hypoplasia or absent β cells. FOXP3 mutations (Answer D) cause a rare X-linked disorder that can result in a constellation of symptoms of IPEX syndrome (immune dysregulation, polyendocrinopathy, enteropathy). This patient does not have any of these features.

Maturity-onset diabetes of the young (MODY) is the most common form of monogenic diabetes, and it is diagnosed in approximately 2% to 5% of patients with diabetes. This condition is usually diagnosed in young adults (<25 years) and is associated with an autosomal dominant inheritance pattern. Thus, in affected families, more than 2 generations often have a history of diabetes. Persons with MODY 2 have a defect in the glucokinase enzyme (encoded by the GCK gene [Answer C]), which phosphorylates glucose to glucose-6-phosphate and acts as a glucose sensor. Affected persons experience a higher threshold for glucose-stimulated insulin secretion. The hyperglycemia resulting from this defect is a mild, stable fasting hyperglycemia, and affected persons are not able to develop diabetes-associated complications. Thus, they can simply be treated with lifestyle modification. This patient had severe hyperglycemia as a neonate, which responded well to sulfonylurea treatment, and he is very unlikely to have a mutation associated with monogenic diabetes.

He did not present with typical biochemical features of diabetic ketoacidosis and had a normal anion gap despite severe hyperglycemia, making the underlying diagnosis of type 1 diabetes very unlikely. Therefore, he is unlikely to have detectable islet-cell antibodies (Answer A) or glutamic acid decarboxylase antibodies (Answer B).

Gloyn AL, Cummings EA, Edghill EL, et al. Permanent neonatal diabetes due to paternal germline mosaicism for an activating mutation of the KCNJ11 gene encoding the Kir6.2 subunit of the beta-cell potassium adenosine triphosphate channel. J Clin Endocrinol Metab. 2004;89(8):3932-3935.

von Mühlendahl KE, Herkenhoff H. Long-term course of neonatal diabetes. N Engl J Med. 1995;333(11):704-708.

Stoy J, Steiner DF, Park SY, Ye H, Phuipson LH, Bell GI. Clinical and molecular genetics of neonatal diabetes due to mutations in the insulin gene [published correction appears in Rev Endocr Metab Disord. 2012;13(1):79-81]. Rev Endocr Metab Disord. 2010;11(3):205-215.

## 39

##### ANSWER: A) 22q11.2 deletion syndrome (formerly known as DiGeorge syndrome)

This patient underwent genetic testing, which revealed a deletion in the DiGeorge chromosomal region, diagnostic of 22q11.2 deletion syndrome (thus, Answer A is correct). This disorder can result in a developmental defect of the third and fourth branchial pouches, which leads to parathyroid aplasia or hypoplasia (seen in roughly half of these patients). This contiguous gene deletion syndrome is inherited in an autosomal dominant manner, although most cases result from de novo deletions. It is now known to be the underlying etiology of DiGeorge syndrome, velocardiofacial syndrome, Shprintzen syndrome, etc. 22q11.2 deletion syndrome encompasses a range of clinical manifestations including characteristic facial features (such as cleft palate), developmental delay, congenital heart disease (most commonly tetralogy of Fallot or ventricular septal defects), immune deficiency, autoimmune conditions (such as hypothyroidism), and thymic hypoplasia. Learning disabilities are present in up to 90% of affected individuals.

This patient does not have additional features of autoimmune polyglandular syndrome type 1, which is also referred to as autoimmune polyendocrinopathy-candidiasis-ectodermal dystrophy syndrome (Answer B). Patients with this disorder have mutations in the autoimmune regulator gene (AIRE), which lead to immunologic destruction of the parathyroid glands. AIRE is also expressed in other endocrine organs including the pancreas, adrenal cortex, lymphatic system, and liver. Hypoparathyroidism and chronic mucocutaneous candidiasis typically present in childhood or adolescence, followed by adrenal insufficiency. Autoimmune polyglandular syndrome type 1 is inherited in an autosomal recessive manner. This patient has no evidence of oral mucosal lesions, adrenal insufficiency, or other autoimmune disorders, thus making this condition an unlikely diagnosis.

Pseudohypoparathyroidism (Answer C) is an incorrect diagnosis because this patient has an undetectable PTH concentration. Pseudohypoparathyroidism represents a constellation of disorders characterized by end-organ resistance to PTH, primarily in the kidney. Biochemical findings in the PTH resistance syndromes are notable for low serum calcium and elevated phosphorus and PTH.

Wilson disease (Answer D) is an unlikely diagnosis in this patient given the lack of other neurologic findings. Parathyroid insufficiency has been described with a variety of infiltrative diseases including Wilson disease, hemochromatosis, granulomatous disease, and malignancy. Wilson disease is an autosomal recessive disorder of cellular copper transport. The condition is due to mutations in the ATP7B gene. Prevalence has been reported to be 1 in 30,000 persons across various populations. Impaired copper excretion leads to deposition in multiple organs, primarily the brain, nervous tissue, eye, and liver. Copper accumulation begins at birth and symptoms present in the teens and 20s. Affected persons experience dysarthria, dystonia, cognitive impairment, and eventually cirrhosis. Neuropsychiatric symptoms may precede liver disease and can also include subtle abnormalities at the onset with poor school performance and depression. Physical examination almost universally reveals Kaiser-Fleisher

rings, resulting from copper deposition in the Descemet membrane at the sclerocorneal junction. Laboratory abnormalities include elevated transaminase values related to hepatic damage.

Vitamin D resistance (Answer E) is an autosomal recessive hereditary disorder and is caused by mutations in the gene encoding the vitamin D receptor (VDR). VDR mutations lead to end-organ resistance to 1,25-hydroxyvitamin D. Biochemical findings include hypocalcemia, hypophosphatemia, elevated PTH, and elevated alkaline phosphatase. Alopecia is noted in about half of described cases. Treatment with calcitriol is

minimally effective in these patients. In contrast, vitamin D-dependent rickets, which is another autosomal recessive disorder that is caused by inactivating mutations in the gene encoding  $ 1\alpha $-hydroxylase, presents with hypocalcemia but can be treated effectively with small doses of calcitriol.

##### Educational Objective:

Outline the differential diagnosis of hereditary and nonhereditary forms of hypoparathyroidism.

UpToDate Topic Review(s):

Mantovani G. Clinical review: pseudohypoparathyroidism: diagnosis and treatment. J Clin Endocrinol Metab. 2011;96(10):3020-3030.

## 40 ANSWER: E) Myelolipoma This man has a history of subor

This man has a history of suboptimal hypertension control, biochemical abnormalities suggesting catecholamine excess, and an adrenal imaging abnormality. The diagnosis of pheochromocytoma seemed to appear to the surgical team and α-adrenergic blockade was given in preparation for an adrenalectomy. However, careful assessment of his medication profile and the adrenal imaging abnormalities suggest that this might be something

Tricyclic antidepressants are the number one cause of false-positive biochemical testing for pheochromocytoma. A tricyclic antidepressants (including cyclobenzaprine) increase endogenous production of catecholamines and may cause impressive elevations (eg, more than 4-fold above the upper normal limit for norepinephrine and normetanephrine) and false-positive biochemical testing for pheochromocytoma. Fortunately, the most popular class of psychoactive drugs is in the United States, selective serotonin reuptake inhibitors, do not cause false-positive testing for pheochromocytoma. Other drugs that may increase endogenous catecholamines include levodopa, amphetamines and other stimulants, buspirone and antipsychotics, prochlorperazine, reserpine, and withdrawal from clonidine and other drugs (eg, alcohol, narcotics). With older fluorometric assays for total metanephries, some drugs (eg, labetalol) directly interfered with the assay and caused false-positive results. However, the use of liquid chromatography with tandem mass spectrometry has eliminated any drug interference. α-Adrenergic antagonists (eg, prazosin), phenoxybenzamine, and most antihypertensive agents do not chronically affect endogenous catecholamine production or metabolism. In addition, chromogranin A, a ubiquitous, biologically inert peptide secreted in concert with many other neuroendocrine peptides, is often elevated in patients with pheochromocytoma. However, the specificity is very poor. Most importantly, proton pump inhibitors cause marked elevations in chromogranin A (via gastrin release) and are probably the most common cause of high chromogranin A concentrations, given the widespread use of these agents. Therefore, the biochemical abnormalities in this patient that suggest a pheochromocytoma (Answer B) could be easily explained by his use of amitriptyline and omeprazole.

Adrenal imaging abnormalities are common. At least 3% to 4% of adults have an adrenal nodule, and most are benign and nonsecretory. The adrenal nodule in this patient has a very low attenuation value (−59 Hounsfield units). This means that the lesion is composed of mostly fat and is diagnostic of an adrenal myelolipoma (Answer E). These lesions are easy to identify by Hounsfield units that are always very low (less than −10). Myelolipomas are benign neoplasms composed of both mature adipose and hematopoietic tissue. Very large myelolipomas have been reported in persons with congenital adrenal hyperplasia due to 21-hydroxylase, 17-hydroxylase, or 11-hydroxylase deficiencies. Most such patients have either untreated or poorly treated congenital adrenal hyperplasia. However, these lesions are typically small, innocuous, and have no biologic significance. After they are identified, they rarely grow and can usually be managed conservatively.

In contrast to myel�pomias, pneocromocytomas contain very little fat and always have a high attenuation value (Hounsfield units >15). The use of  $ ^{123} $I-meta-iodobenzylguanidine (MIBG) scintigraphy in this patient caused great confusion. MIBG uptake is routinely seen in normal adrenal glands and it is usually asymmetric, with the left adrenal gland uptake appearing more prominently. Unfortunately, the nuclear medicine physician did not correlate this uptake with the CT imaging abnormality.

Adrenal cysts (Ansoer A) are uncommon and most are endothelial cysts or pseudocysts. They can be very large. Of course, some adrenal neoplasms (such as ganglioneuroma, pheochromocytoma, adrenocortical carcinoma, and, rarely, adrenal adenoma) may undergo cystic change. Pure adrenal cysts have water density (0-20 Hounsfield units) with thin, smooth walls that lack enhancement. Peripheral calcifications may be seen in 15%. Pseudocysts are usually low density, but they may have thick walls, internal septations, and calcifications. There may be higher density within the cyst because of hemorrhage. Cystic change in an adrenal myelopoma has not been reported

A solitary metastatic lesion to the adrenal gland (Answer D) as the initial presentation of an unknown malignancy is very rare. Of 1639 patients with cancer at MD Anderson, the adrenal gland was identified as a site of involvement at presentation in 95 (5.8%); however, involvement was limited to only the adrenal gland in 4 patients (0.2%). All 4 of these patients had large adrenal tumors ( $ \geq $6 cm), 3 of the 4 had bilateral involvement, and all had symptoms that otherwise mandated evaluation for an occult malignancy. None had a true adrenal incidentaloma. Moreover, metastatic lesions in the adrenal gland have little fat and are accompanied by an attenuation value greater than 20 Hounsfield units.

Adrenal ganglioneuroma (Answer C) is a benign neoplasm arising from neural crest tissue that is usually discovered incidentally in young adults. Composed of mature ganglion cells and Schwann cells in a fibrous stroma, these lesions do not secrete catecholamines or steroid hormones. Adrenal ganglioneuomas may be quite large, but there is no vessel involvement. In addition, these lesions are often mistaken for something more sinister such as cancer or pheochromocytoma since they usually have a slightly high and heterogenous T2-weighted signal on MRI. The nonenhanced attenuation value is usually 20 to 40 Hounsfield units and calcifications are common. As a result of the neural crest origin of these lesions, it is not surprising that they may occasionally demonstrate positive MIBG imaging and be confused with pheochromocytoma.

This patient had an adrenalectomy. Histopathology documented an adrenal myelolipoma. There was no evidence of a pheochromocytoma. Additional hormonal testing in this man showed the following: total testosterone, 229 ng/dL (7.9 nmol/L); LH, 24 mIU/mL (24 IU/L); and FSH, 67 mIU/mL (67 IU/L). His nocturnal sweating improved with transdermal testosterone administration.

##### Educational Objective:

Anticipate imaging and biochemical pitfalls in the evaluation of patients with suspected pheochromocytoma.

Giusti M, Sidoti M, Augeri C, Rabitti C, Minuto F. Effect of short-term treatment with low dosages of the proton pump inhibitor omeprazole in serum chromogranin A levels in man. Eur J Endocrinol. 2004;150(3):299-303.

## 41 ANSWER: B) Fenofibrate

Despite his low LDL-cholesterol level, this man has already experienced a myocardial infarction and is at very high risk for further cardiovascular disease events. He does not smoke cigarettes, his blood pressure is controlled, and he is taking appropriate cardiovascular medications (aspirin,  $ \beta $-adrenergic blocker, ACE inhibitor). Thus, the only other modifiable cardiovascular risk factor is his dyslipidemia. Most guidelines recommend statins in patients with established cardiovascular disease to further reduce risk; the Adult Treatment Panel III (ATP

III) guidelines recommend treatment to achieve an LDL-cholesterol concentration less than 100 mg/dL (<2.59 mmol/L) with an optional goal of less than 70 mg/dL (<1.81 mmol/L). The newer American Heart Association American College of Cardiology guidelines have moved away from target-driven therapy, and instead recom割high-intensity statin therapy to further reduce risk in patients at high risk such as this one, regardless of LDL-cholesterol level. This recommendation is based in part on studies such as Treat to New Targets (TNT), which compared high-dosage vs low-dosage atorvastatin and found further reductions in events in participants with low LDL-cholesterol levels. However, this patient has had intolerable myalgias with at least 3 statins. Although he not tried pravastatin (Answer A), the fact that he had myalgias with atorvastatin, simvastatin, and rosuvastatin, which differ in their relative hydrophilicity, hepatic metabolism, clearance, and elimination half-life, suggests the will most likely not tolerate pravastatin either. The prevalence of muscle symptoms in statin users is reported to be 5% to 10%; however, the placebo groups generally have a similar prevalence of muscle symptoms, which reflects the high population prevalence of muscle symptoms. In general, if a patient has myalgias to one statin, trying another statin with different pharmacokinetic profiles is appropriate. However, if several have been tried they each cause the same reaction, a tolerable statin is unlikely to be found. A growing body of literature address intermittent statin dosing (such as alternate day) to increase statin tolerability; however, this has not yet been tested with respect to cardiovascular outcomes.

Although statins have the best evidence to support their reduction of cardiovascular disease events, other lip lowering agents can also improve outcomes. Ezetimibe (Answer D) is a cholesterol absorption inhibitor that low LDL cholesterol via a different mechanism than statins. Although this patient previously reported myalgias when taking a statin + ezetimibe combination pill, the symptoms were almost certainly due to the statin component. It would most likely tolerate ezetimibe as monotherapy. However, to date, ezetimibe has not been shown to decrease risk of cardiovascular events. Given that this patient's main lipid abnormalities are low HDL cholesterol and triglycerides, and his LDL-cholesterol level is already fairly low, further LDL lowering with an agent that has not yet been shown to improve outcomes is not the best choice.

Fish oil (Answer C) has been used for its triglyceride-lowering effects, but it is usually recommended for severe hypertriglyceridemia—when triglyceride concentrations are greater than 500 mg/dL (>5.65 mmol/L) and there is a risk of pancreatitis. Fish oil has not been shown to reduce cardiovascular events.

Fibrates (Answer B) have been studied in a variety of monotherapy trials for both primary and secondary prevention. Although fibrates have minimal effects on LDL cholesterol, they can lower triglycerides by up to 50% and raise HDL-cholesterol levels by 10% to 15%. In several studies, including the VA-HIT trial, fibrates reduced cardiovascular events. Similarly, niacin (Answer E), which can lower triglycerides and LDL cholesterol and raise HDL cholesterol, reduced recurrent cardiovascular events and mortality in the Coronary Drug Project. Thus, either fibrate or niacin therapy is a good alternative to statin therapy in statin-intolerant patients. There are no comparisons between the agents regarding cardiovascular risk reduction, and the decision of which agent to prescribe should thus be guided by potential adverse effects. Fibrates and niacin can cause myalgias and myopathy, and rhabdomyolysis has been reported; however, this risk tends to be greatest when either of these agents is used with a statin, which is not the plan in this vignette. Fibrates have been associated with elevations in transaminases or creatinine, and cholelithiasis may occur. Niacin is also associated with elevation in transaminases and is known to cause flushing, rash, diarrhea, nausea, and pruritus. Niacin can increase glucose levels and worsen insulin resistance, which may be a concern in this patient who has impaired fasting glucose as at high risk for diabetes. In general, fibrates are very well tolerated, with discontinuation rates of 5% or less trials. Conversely, niacin has been a study discontinuation rate of up to 16%, with more than 60% of patients reporting flushing. Thus, on the basis of this patient's lipid profile, the adverse effects of the agents, and the outcome of available, fenofibrate (Answer B) is the best choice.

##### Educational Objective:

Select lipid-lowering therapy in a statin-intolerant patient.

UpToDate Topic Review(s):

Abdel-Maksoud M, Sazonov V, Gutkin SW, Hokanson JE. Effects of modifying triglycerides and triglyceride-rich lipoproteins on cardiovascular outcomes. J Cardiovasc Pharmacol. 2008;51(4):331-351.

## 42 ANSWER: D) Move his insulin glulisine dose to 15 minutes before meals

42 $ ^{A} $ This patient's mean glucose concentration of 131 mg/dL (7.27 mmol/L) using measurements tested before meals and at bedtime is lower than one would expect on the basis of his hemoglobin  $ A_{1c} $ level. A hemoglobin  $ A_{1c} $ reading of 7.5% (0.075) equates to a mean blood glucose concentration of about 165 mg/dL (9.16 mmol/L). This supports the idea that hyperglycemia is occurring at times when glucose levels are not tested, namely in the postprandial state. Continuous glucose monitoring has demonstrated that unrecognized postprandial hyperglycemia is common in patients with type 1 diabetes and that it contributes adversely to overall glycemic control as measured by hemoglobin  $ A_{1c} $.

Postprandial hyperglycemia can be confirmed by having patients test fingerstick glucose levels after meals. Peak glucose levels occur 1 to 2 hours after meals, depending on meal composition. Foods with a higher glycemic index (low in fiber and/or higher in simple sugars) lead to a more rapid and higher peak glucose level than do foods with a lower glycemic index (thus, Answer E is incorrect). The maximum insulin level after injection of one of the rapid-acting insulin analogues occurs about 90 minutes after injection. Reduced postprandial glucose excursions are seen if the insulin injection is moved from immediately before the meal to 15 minutes before the meal (thus, Answer D is correct).

The insulin sensitivity factor can be calculated using the following equation: 1800/total daily insulin dose. In this case, with the patient using about 40 units of insulin daily, the calculated sensitivity factor would be 1:45, or 1 unit to drop the glucose concentration 45 mg/dL (2.50 mmol/L). In this vignette, increasing the patient's sensitivity factor to 1:30 (Answer C) could lead to increased hypoglycemia.

Two-hour postmeal glucose levels are a better predictor of cardiovascular events and mortality than fasting glucose levels, and they are a better predictor of retinopathy in type 2 diabetes mellitus than hemoglobin  $ A_{1c} $. Postprandial hyperglycemia increases oxidative stress and is associated with endothelial dysfunction in patients with type 2 diabetes. Compared with regular insulin, rapid-acting insulin analogues lead to better postprandial glycemic control in patients with type 1 diabetes and has been shown to improve cardiovascular risk markers. However, randomized prospective trials targeting postprandial hyperglycemia have not shown reductions in cardiovascular outcomes in patients with type 2 diabetes and have not been performed in patients with type 1 diabetes.

Interventions that lower postprandial glucose responses to a meal include use of rapid-acting insulin analogues rather than human regular insulin, the consumption of lower-glycemic index carbohydrates, warming the injection site before an insulin bolus, the use of continuous glucose monitors, and bolusing insulin 15 minutes before meals rather than immediately before. The addition of protein to a meal increases the postmeal glucose response and the late postmeal insulin requirement. Improvements in postprandial glucose levels can be documented by having a patient check postmeal glucose levels before and after making the change in timing of the premeal rapid-acting insulin dose.

Prandial insulin coverage can effectively control postprandial glucose levels either with set insulin doses within fixed carbohydrate amounts at mealtimes or by having patients learn to count the grams of carbohydrates in a meal and adjust the prandial insulin dose based on a calculated insulin-to-carbohydrate ratio. A formula commonly used to calculate an individual patient's insulin-to-carbohydrate ratio is 500/total daily insulin dose. For this patient, 500/40 units daily results in an insulin-to-carbohydrate ratio of about 1:12 (thus, Answer B is incorrect). Switching him to an insulin-to-carbohydrate ratio would also not be expected to significantly lower postmeal glucose levels because he is eating a set amount of carbohydrates at mealtimes to match his fixed prandial insulin dose. His standard deviation of glucose values is below 50 mg/dL (2.78 mmol/L), indicating a low degree of glucose variability. Higher standard deviations often are seen in patients whose prandial insulin dose does not closely match their carbohydrate intake.

Increasing the insulin glargine dose (Answer A) is a common consideration for patients whose glucose levels are elevated throughout the day and when the glucose level consistently rises overnight while a patient is fasting. In this patient, glucose levels are stable overnight and he periodically experiences nocturnal hypoglycemia. Increasing his insulin glargine dose may increase his risk of nocturnal hypoglycemia.

Educational Objective: Recognize and manage postprandial hyperglycemia in a patient with type 1 diabetes mellitus.

UpToDate Topic Review(s):

Insulin therapy in adults with type 1 diabetes mellitus

Luijf YM, van Bon AC, Hoekstra JB, Devries JH. Premeal injection of rapid-acting insulin reduces postprandial glycemic excursions in type 1 diabetes. Diarr. Care. 2010;33(10):2152-2155.

43 ANSWER: B) Administer radioiodine with concurrent control with the rate ofனхey disease. This patient has developed symptoms and signs of Graves' ophthalmopathy, an inflammatory eye disease. In most cases, it occurs in patients that develop in the orbit in association with autoimmune thyroid disorders. In most cases, it occurs in patients with current or previous Graves' disease and may present during, before, or after presentation with thyrotoxicosis. Approximately half of patients with Graves' disease have symptoms and signs of ophthalmopathy and 5% have severe disease, sometimes sight-threatening. The natural history of the disease is one of rapid deterioration followed by gradual improvement towards the baseline. Graves' ophthalmopathy is assessed by evaluation of disease activity and severity. The activity is determined using the clinical activity score, which generates 1 point each of the following: pain in primary gaze; pain with eye movement; chemosis; eyelid swelling; eyelid erythema; conjunctival redness; caruncula swelling; and, over the past 3 months, decreased visual acuity, increased diplopia, and proptosis. The maximum score is 10. Graves' ophthalmopathy is considered "active" with a clinical activity score of 3 or higher. The severity is graded as mild, moderate-to-severe, and sight-threatening as agreed upon by a consensus statement of the European Group of Graves' Orbitopathy (EUGOGO). Both activity and severity must be considered in therapeutic decisions regarding treatment of eye disease itself, as well as the treatment of hyperthyroidism. On the basis of the provided information, this patient has mild active Graves' ophthalmopathy.

She is a current smoker, which is the most important effect of smoking on Graves' ophthalmopathy, and several studies have shown a detrimental effect of smoking on Graves' ophthalmopathy in patients treated with radioactive iodine. The risk is directly proportional to the number of cigarettes smoked per day. Former smokers have significantly lower risk than current smokers. Clinicians should advise all patients, and especially those with Graves' ophthalmopathy, to stop smoking and should refer them to a structured smoking-cessation program. Besides smoking, risk factors for the deterioration of Graves' ophthalmopathy after radioiodine therapy include high pretreatment T₃ values, active and progressive Graves' ophthalmopathy over the preceding 3 months, high pretreatment TSH-receptor antibodies, and development of pyothyroidism after treatment. Current American Thyroid Association guidelines recommend steroid prophylaxis patients with mild active Graves' ophthalmopathy. Typical steroid regimens involve a high dosage of oral sucocorticoids for 2 months, although a lower dosage given for 6 weeks could be equally effective.

This patient has developed adverse effects of antithyroid drug treatment and specifically requests a particular acceptable form of treatment for her condition. After discussion of the logistics, benefits, and drawbacks, administration of radioiodine with concurrent glucocorticoid therapy (Answer B) is a reasonable approach. reasons described above, she should be advised to stop smoking, although radioiodine is not contraindicated. light of her smoking status. Radiation protection guidance is variable across the globe, but most national and international protection rules advise against prolonged and close contact with children, especially those aged less than 5 years.

Adverse effects of antithyroid drugs occur in roughly 3% of treated patients and most are minor and transient for adverse effects are rare and include agranulocytosis induced by both methimazole and propylthiouracil, etogenic effects from methimazole, and toxic hepatitis from propylthiouracil. Persistent minor adverse effects of antithyroid drugs should be managed by cessation of medication and recommending radioactive iodine or gery or switching to the other antithyroid drug when radioiodine or surgery is not an option. Replacement methimazole with propylthiouracil (Answer A) is a reasonable strategy in this case, although in view of the need for the treatment (an appropriate therapeutic option in the situation), preference should be given to this treatment strategy.

Although corticosteroid therapy may reduce this patient's symptoms of Graves ophthalmopathy, it is not a long-term solution and only with cessation of the methimazole will the intolerance subside (Answer C is incorrect).

Total thyroidectomy (Answer D) is incorrect because she is requesting radioactive iodine therapy and this is not contraindicated in the current situation.

Because she has had persistent adverse effects from methimazole for 2 months, it is inappropriate to encourage her to continue to take this treatment (Answer E).

## 44 ANSWER: C) Clomiphene citrate Women with polycystic ovary syndrome

Women with polycystic ovary syndrome have infertility related to irregular menstrual cycles and anovulation. Clomiphene citrate (Answer C) is the best first-line medication to induce ovulation in this setting. Clomiphene citrate is a selective estrogen receptor modulator that inhibits estradiol's negative feedback at the hypothalamus, resulting in increased GnRH pulse frequency. The increased GnRH pulsatility stimulates LH and FSH, which then drive follicle growth and estradiol production. The improvement in pregnancy and live birth rate is significant when compared with outcomes using placebo.

Metformin (Answer B) is used off-label for polycystic ovary syndrome because it increases menstrual cycle frequency and pregnancy rate. However, the pregnancy rate is lower than with clomiphene citrate, and metformin does not improve the live birth rate. Therefore, it is not the treatment of choice for infertility. Further, the pregnancy rate using a combination of clomiphene citrate and metformin (Answer D) is not superior to clomiphene alone. In women who do not respond to treatment with clomiphene citrate, recombinant human FSH (Answer A) is the second-line treatment choice.

A recent meta-analysis demonstrated that letrozole, an aromatase inhibitor (Answer E), improves the pregnancy and live birth rate in women with polycystic ovary syndrome when compared with rates observed with clomiphene citrate. However, the quality of the evidence was deemed to be low and it was suggested that these results be regarded with caution. Therefore, an aromatase inhibitor is not yet the recommended treatment for ovulation induction in women with polycystic ovary syndrome until stronger evidence is available.

##### Educational Objective:

Select the most appropriate treatment for a woman with polycystic ovary syndrome who wants to become pregnant.

UpToDate Topic Review(s):

Treatment of polycystic ovary syndrome in adults

##### Reference(s):

Legro RS, Arslanian SA, Ehrmann DA, et al; Endocrine Society. Diagnosis and treatment of polycystic ovary syndrome: an Endocrine Society Clinical Practice Guideline. J Clin Endocrinol Metab. 2013;98(12):4565-4592.

Tang T, Lord JM, Norman RJ, Yasmin E, Balen AH. Insulin-sensitising drugs (metformin, rosiglitazone, pioglitazone, D-chiro-inositol) for women with polycystic ovary syndrome, oligo amenorrhoea and subfertility. Cochrane Database Syst Rev. 2012;5:CD003053.

Franik S, Kremer JA, Nelen WL, Farquhar C. Aromatase inhibitors for subfertile women with polycystic ovary syndrome. Cochrane Database Syst Rev. 2014;2:CD010287.

## 45 ANSWER: E) Measure 24-hour urinary calcium and creatinine excretion PTH-depr

45 ANSWER: E) Meas.

PTH-dependent hypercalcemia was correctly diagnosed in this patient. The problem is that the evaluation was incomplete. Although most patients with this pattern of laboratory results have primary hyperparathyroidism, other diagnoses can present with similar biochemistry. Of note, the PTH level in this patient was in the “normal” range. While inappropriately normal PTH levels are not atypical in patients with primary hyperparathyroidism, most have frankly elevated PTH levels. In addition, this patient had no previously documented calcium values for comparison, so it was not possible to distinguish an acquired problem from one that is congenital. Evaluation of such patients should include measurement of 24-hour urinary calcium excretion (Answer E), not so much to determine whether a patient is an appropriate surgical candidate, but rather to exclude familial hypocalciuria, hypercalcemia (FHH). Indeed, this patient’s 24-hour urinary calcium excretion was very low and the fractional excretion of calcium (calcium-to-creatinine ratio) was less than 0.01.

Calculating a calcium-to-creatinine clearance ratio is helpful to distinguish FHH from primary hyperparathyroidism, as 80% of patients with FHH have a ratio less than 0.01 and most patients with primary hyperparathyroidism have a ratio greater than 0.02. The calcium-to-creatinine clearance is calculated with the following equation:  $ [24\ h\ urinary\ calcium \times serum\ creatinine]/[serum\ calcium \times 24\ h\ urinary\ creatinine] $.

FHH is an autosomal dominant disorder with high penetrance and is caused by inactivating mutations in the CASR gene. More than 200 CASR mutations have been described. In this disorder, the parathyroid glands have reduced sensitivity to calcium and higher serum calcium levels are needed to reduce parathyroid release. The calcium-PTH curve is shifted to the left, resetting the serum calcium to a higher level. In the kidney, this produces an increase in tubular calcium and magnesium reabsorption. Typical biochemical abnormalities include hypercalcemia, hypocalciuria, normal or slightly elevated PTH, and elevated magnesium. Genetic testing in the patient revealed that he was heterozygous for a novel mutation in the CASR gene. Persons who are heterozygous for an inactivating CASR mutation typically have mild hypercalcemia due to only partial loss of calcium-sensing receptor function. Although genetic testing might be considered, additional genetic causes unrelated to the CASR gene (eg, the AP2S1 gene) can be responsible for the clinical picture of FHH. Thus, CASR genetic testing is reserved for situations when the patient makes this request or when the urinary fractional excretion of calcium borderline (eg, ~0.01).

Although further imaging of the neck with ultrasonography (Answer A) or 4D CT (Answer B) would be indicated if the patient had persistent primary hyperparathyroidism despite attempted surgical resection, visualization of the parathyroid glands is not indicated in the setting of FHH. Indeed, resection of all 4 glands would result in severe hypocalcemia and thereby cause harm. Most patients with FHH are asymptomatic; do not develop nephrolithiasis due to the low urinary calcium excretion; and do not experience complications such as bone loss, hypertension, or peptic ulcer disease. Neither bone densitometry (Answer D) nor surgery (Answer C) is indicated.

##### Educational Objective:

Include familial hypocalciuric hypercalcemia in the differential diagnosis for persistent hyperparathyroidism a parathyroid surgery.

## 46 ANSWER: E) Free testosterone measurement

46 Measurement of serum total testosterone by a reliable assay is the best screening test for the diagnosis of male hypogonadism. Documentation of low serum total testosterone levels (measured on at least 2 occasions) accurately identifies androgen deficiency in most patients. However, when alterations in serum sex hormone-binding globulin (SHBG) concentrations are suspected, further diagnostic steps are required to confirm the diagnosis. Obesity is one such condition in which circulating serum concentrations of SHBG are reduced. Insulin resistance (resulting in hyperinsulinemia) is posited to be the mechanism, as insulin suppresses SHBG production from the liver. Laboratory studies have confirmed that the addition of insulin in the culture medium containing human hepatoma cell line suppresses SHBG production from these cells (Figure). Indeed, low serum total testosterone levels are commonly seen in obese men. Therefore, further diagnostic steps are required to confirm the diagnosis of testosterone deficiency. The next step in these men is the measurement of free testosterone by accurate and reliable methods. These methods include calculation of free testosterone by mass action equation (using SHBG and total testosterone [measured by a reliable assay]) and direct measurement of free testosterone using the equilibrium dialysis method. The former is more often used because it is readily available through commercial laboratories. Although some laboratories have started performing high-throughput assays using equilibrium dialysis, it is still not widely available to all clinicians.

This patient is obese with signs of insulin resistance and his SHBG levels are most likely low. Furthermore, his libido (a specific symptom of androgen deficiency) is normal and his fatigue and drowsiness (nonspecific for androgen deficiency) could be indicative of another underlying condition such as obstructive sleep apnea. Therefore, measurement of free testosterone (Answer E) is the next step in this patient's evaluation. This patient's SHBG levels were documented to be low at 0.79 μg/mL (7.0 nmol/L) and his calculated free testosterone was normal at 12 ng/dL (0.42 nmol/L). Polysomnography confirmed sleep apnea and treatment with continuous positive airway pressure improved his symptoms.

Measurement of gonadotropins (Answer D) is an important aspect in the evaluation of a patient with suspected hypogonadism because gonadotropins indicate whether hypogonadism is primary or secondary. However, they should only be measured after the diagnosis of androgen deficiency is confirmed, which is not the case in this patient. In other words, their measurement in this patient would have been indicated only if his free testosterone were low. Similarly, pituitary MRI (Answer A) and serum prolactin levels (Answer C) should be pursued only after the diagnosis of androgen deficiency is confirmed. Iron studies (Answer B) are indicated if hemochromatosis is suspected, a

condition that can cause either primary or secondary hypogonadism (the latter being more common). The possibility hemochromatosis should only be entertained once the diagnosis of androgen deficiency is confirmed.

47 ANSWER: E) The petrosal sinus sampling study

This patient has clear clinical and biochemical evidence of Cushing syndrome and a high ACTH level, supporting a diagnosis of ACTH-dependent Cushing syndrome. The next step in the evaluation is to distinguish between ACTH-dependent Cushing syndrome (Cushing disease) due to an ACTH-secreting pituitary tumor vs an ectopic source of ACTH hypersecretion. This patient underwent dexamethasone suppression testing to help differentiate among the causes. This patient failed to suppress serum cortisol (50%). Suppression of either cortisol levels is a common condition that is not well-classified by the patient. The patient is also a patient who has been农家-skilled with other health-related conditions, such as the use of anticoagulants, and other medical methods.

Petrosal sinus sampling is susceptible to a number of pitfalls. Importantly, for accurate results, hypercortisolemia must be present when inferior petrosal sinus sampling is done. Hypercortisolism in ectopic ACTH syndrome suppresses normal pituitary corticotrophs; thus, administration of corticotropin-releasing hormone does not stimulate ACTH secretion during the sampling procedure. In the presence of eucortisolemia, however, endogenous ACTH production from normal pituitary corticotrophs may no longer be suppressed and stimulation can occur. In this patient, ketoconazole therapy had normalized his cortisol excess, which therefore led to a false-positive result after corticotropin-releasing hormone stimulation (Answer E). The lack of central-peripheral gradient at baseline sampling might be suspicious in this patient, but the stimulation after corticotropin-releasing hormone would be the criterion used to determine whether a positive central-to-peripheral stimulation exists. Guidelines for interpretation of the ACTH gradient obtained from inferior petrosal sinus sampling state that a central-to-peripheral gradient greater than 2.0 at baseline or greater than 3.0 after corticotropin-releasing hormone stimulation is consistent with a pituitary source of Cushing disease. Were the inferior petrosal sinus sampling to have been done properly without pretreatment with ketoconazole, the results would have yielded the little or no central-to-peripheral ACTH gradient, consistent with ectopic ACTH syndrome.

Failure to fully cannulate petrosal sinus vessels (Answer B) can also be a pitfall in test interpretation. Result may fail to show a central-to-peripheral ACTH gradient despite the presence of an ACTH-producing pituitary

tumor if a catheter was not fully advanced into the petrosal sinus. If blood was sampled from a different vein where ACTH hypersecretion was diluted, false-negative results could be obtained. In this patient, a central-to-peripheral gradient was seen after stimulation with corticotropin-releasing hormone, proving the catheters were in place, although the lack of a gradient at time “0” relates to the ectopic source of the ACTH.

Because the patient has a high ACTH level, he clearly does not have ACTH-independent Cushing syndrome from an adrenal source (Answer A). Although patients with pseudo-Cushing syndrome (Answer C) have a central-to-peripheral ACTH gradient on petrosal sinus sampling, as do healthy persons, this patient has clear clinical evidence of Cushing syndrome. Therefore, pseudo-Cushing syndrome is unlikely. A false-positive central-to-peripheral gradient is more likely in this case than a tumor in the cavernous sinus (Answer D). An ectopic corticotropin-releasing hormone-producing pituitary tumor can be associated with false-positive stimulation of ACTH after corticotropin-releasing hormone administration. However, such tumors are extremely rare. Another cause of false-positive central-to-peripheral gradient is intermittent hormonogenesis from an ectopic ACTH source.

##### Educational Objective:

Identify pitfalls of inferior petrosal sinus sampling for the differential diagnosis of Cushing syndrome.

48 ANSWER: C) Change to U500 regular insulin, 0.30 mL (150 units) 3 times daily before meals

This patient has very poor glycemic control despite use of 450 units of U100 insulin daily. He has severe insulin resistance, generally defined as an insulin requirement greater than 200 units of insulin daily. Use of such very high insulin doses is inconvenient because multiple injections per dose are required and high volumes may lead to injection site discomfort. In addition, the absorption of insulin from large-volume subcutaneous depots may be poor and/or unpredictable. In patients such as this, conversion from U100 to 5-fold concentrated U500 regular insulin (U500R) represents a very reasonable alternative glycemic management strategy. In particular, conversion to U500R permits administration of large insulin doses via much smaller volumes per injection and fewer injections per day, which in turn may improve insulin absorption and perhaps regimen adherence. Studies of U500R insulin action suggest that this insulin formulation has an onset of action similar to that of U100 regular insulin and a duration of action similar to that of U100 NPH insulin. Small studies of patients with uncontrolled type 2 diabetes who switch from U100 to U500R insulin have demonstrated improvements in hemoglobin  $ A_{1c} $ level ranging from 1.0% to 3.29%, without a significant increase in rates of severe hypoglycemia. In addition, conversion to U500R in such patients has been associated with improvements in patient satisfaction with treatment and quality of life. Although U500R insulin vials are more expensive than U100 insulin vials, this does not necessarily increase the overall costs of care. The per-unit cost of U500R is estimated to be less than that of U100 insulin, and the costs of needles and syringes may also be reduced because fewer injections are needed.

Although no significant increase in severe hypoglycemia has been reported in clinical trials, use of U500R in clinical practice has resulted in instances of clinically significant hypoglycemia because of dosing errors. Presently, U500R is available only in a vial, and thus must be drawn up and administered via a syringe. Some prescribers prefer to have patients draw up U500R in a U100 insulin syringe with guidance to adapt these devices for the more concentrated insulin. However, the Institute for Safe Medication Practices has recommended “consistent use of a

tuberculin syringe with U-500 insulin, with total doses expressed in terms of both units and volume, i.e., 200 units (0.4 mL). This recommendation has been reiterated in a recent publication dated October 2013. Irrespective of type of syringe chosen for insulin administration, the patient and any associated caregivers must be fully educated regarding the correct drawing up and administration of prescribed doses. Very careful attention must be paid to insulin dosing when users of U500R are hospitalized, as many reported errors in insulin dosing have occurred in hospitalized patients. Guidelines to enhance the safety of insulin use during hospitalizations have also been issued by the Institute for Safe Medication Practices. Delivery devices to administer concentrated insulin more safely currently under development.

Recommendations for the conversion from U100 insulin to U500R generally incorporate the baseline U100 insulin doses, as well as hemoglobin  $ A_{1c} $ level, to determine appropriate doses of the concentrated insulin. In general, experts recommend that the total daily insulin dose be increased by 10% in patients with a hemoglobin  $ A_{1c} $ level above 10% (>0.100); that the total daily insulin dose be maintained in patients with a hemoglobin  $ A_{1c} $ level between 8% and 10% (0.080 and 0.100); and that the total daily dose be decreased by 10% to 20% in patients with a hemoglobin  $ A_{1c} $ level below 8% (<0.080) at the time of conversion. It should be noted, however, that some experts recommend total daily insulin dose reductions of 10% to 20% for all patients when their regimen converted to U500R. In patients using 150 to 300 units of insulin daily, the total amount may be administered in 3 divided doses, while users of 300 to 600 units daily require 3 to 4 injections daily or continuous subcutaneous insulin infusion. Users of more than 600 units of insulin daily should have their regimens converted to either 4 insulin injections daily or continuous subcutaneous insulin infusion.

insulin injections daily or continuous subcutaneous insulin inpatient daily or continuous daily or continuous daily or continuous daily or U100 insulin daily and has a hemoglobin  $ A_{1c} $ level of 9.1% (0.091). Conversion to U500R at 0.2 mL (100 units) 3 times daily (Answer A) represents a greater than 100 of U500R at 0.45 mL (225 units) twice daily (Answer B) provides an overall appropriate (maintained) total daily dose of insulin; however, users of this much daily insulin should divide the total amount into at least 3 separate injections. Conversion to 0.3 mL (150 units) 3 times daily (Answer C) is correct because the patient's total daily insulin dose is unchanged and divided into an appropriate number of doses. Conversion to U500R dosing of 0.4 mL (200 units) 3 times daily (Answer D) represents a more significant increase than is generally recommended, even for patients with a hemoglobin  $ A_{1c} $ level greater than 10% (>0.100). U500R insulin delivered via continuous subcutaneous insulin infusion is a therapeutic option; however, this is not a requirement for U500R use and it has not been demonstrated to be more effective than the use of U500R via multiple daily injections. Thus, referral for insulin pump training before conversion to U500R (Answer E) would unnecessarily delay implementation of effective therapy and would certainly increase therapeutic costs. Although some data suggest that delivery of U500R via continuous subcutaneous insulin infusion may cause less weight gain than administration via multiple daily injection the 2 delivery strategies have not been adequately compared in randomized controlled trials. Thus, any perceived metabolic advantage to the use of U500R via continuous subcutaneous insulin infusion remains hypothetical.

##### Educational Objective:

Guide conversion from a U100 to a U500 regular insulin regimen in a patient with severe insulin resistance.

## 40 ANSWER: D) Increase dexamethasone to 0.5 mg at bedtime

In nonclassic congenital adrenal hyperplasia, the adrenal zona fasciculata's production of cortisol is partially reduced such that the hypothalamic-pituitary drive to the adrenal gland is increased to restore normal cortisol levels.

a result, the intermediate steroid metabolites between cholesterol as it is converted to cortisol accumulate. In particular, with disturbances in 21-hydroxylase activity, ACTH secretion rises above normal and 17-hydroxyprogesterone and DHEA-S are increased because they are proximate to the enzymatic defect. DHEA-S is a weak androgen, so elevated DHEA-S levels can inhibit hypothalamic GnRH, pituitary LH, and testicular testosterone secretion.

Nonclassic congenital adrenal hyperplasia is not life-threatening because of compensated production of glucocorticoids. It presents in childhood or early adulthood. In females, elevated adrenal androgen production can manifest with acne, hirsutism, and irregular menses. In males, adrenal androgen production can increase before the time of normal puberty and lead to a premature growth spurt and virilization. Hypertension occurs, albeit infrequently, if intermediate molecules with mineralocorticoid activity are increased.

The central question related to this vignette focuses on glucocorticoid replacement when symptoms from the partial enzymatic defect arise. For this patient, adrenal androgen production initially resulted in premature puberty and later in reduced gonadal function and erectile dysfunction. To address his concerns, longer-acting glucocorticoid medications should be administered at bedtime with a goal of reducing early-morning ACTH secretion and subsequent adrenal hormone synthesis. Generally, 1 mg of dexamethasone taken at bedtime will suppress most, if not all, ACTH secretion in normal individuals. Hence, dexamethasone dosages below 1 mg daily are often sufficient to prevent adrenal androgen hypersecretion in patients with nonclassic congenital adrenal hyperplasia. However, metabolism of dexamethasone varies among individuals, and some patients require more, or less, dexamethasone to experience a satisfactory response without signs and symptoms of glucocorticoid excess.

This patient was clearly undertreated at a dexamethasone dosage of 0.25 mg at bedtime. Thus, an increase to 0.5 mg at bedtime would be a reasonable next step (thus, Answer D is correct). Because of dexamethasone's long duration of action, splitting it into 2 doses is not needed and taking the dexamethasone in the morning would exert a suppressive action on ACTH at a time when it would not be elevated (ie, the afternoon and evening) (thus, Answer E is incorrect).

Generally, the ratio of glucocorticoid action for dexamethasone to hydrocortisone is ~25:1. A dose of 12.5 mg of hydrocortisone is roughly equivalent to 0.5 mg of dexamethasone. However, one would not use hydrocortisone at bedtime to suppress ACTH secretion because of its shorter duration of action (thus, Answer A is incorrect). Similar to splitting the dexamethasone dose, using twice-daily hydrocortisone is also unlikely to be helpful (thus, Answer B is incorrect).

Adding testosterone (Answer C) would increase circulating testosterone levels and might improve the erectile dysfunction. However, adding an additional medication should not be needed if the dexamethasone dosage is properly adjusted.

##### Educational Objective:

Treat nonclassic congenital adrenal hyperplasia and differentiate among glucocorticoid preparations.
