## 74 ANSWER: C) PTHrP

The markedly elevated carbohydrate antigen 19-9 (CA19-9) level is highly suggestive of cholangiocarcinoma, which was confirmed by FNAB of the patient's scapular mass. Cholangiocarcinoma is a rare epithelial tumor of the biliary tree, often locally advanced at the time of initial presentation and associated with high mortality. It metastasizes via the lymphatic system and less commonly spreads to bone with a predilection for the axial skeleton.

The hypercalcemia seen in cholangiocarcinoma is due to either of 2 mechanisms: tumor secretion of PTHrP or the local destruction and release of calcium from bone (which is not an answer option in this vignette). Most commonly, and as described in multiple case reports, hypercalcemia is associated with cholangiocarcinoma due to tumor PTHrP production, as was seen in this patient (thus, Answer C is correct).

PTHrP shares 70% homology with the first 13 amino acids of the aminoterminal end of PTH. Binding of PTHrP to the PTH 1 receptor simulates the major effects including osteoblastic bone resorption and distal tubular calcium reabsorption, which impairs calcium excretion but blunts proximal tubular reabsorption of phosphorus. Beyond the first 13 amino acids, the protein is unique and shows no further sequence homology with PTH. PTHrP additionally enhances malignant cell growth and survival via the secretion of tumor-activating transforming growth factor- $ \beta $ that stimulates local cancer cells.

Patients with PTHrP-mediated hypercalcemia have lower 1,25-dihydroxyvitamin D₃ levels than patients with primary hyperparathyroidism given less stimulation of 1α-hydroxylase activity. PTHrP therefore would not be expected to increase intestinal absorption of calcium. The increased level of alkaline phosphatase in this patient could be the result of direct bony involvement by the tumor, PTHrP-stimulated bone turnover, or a combination of the two.

24,25-Dihydroxyvitamin D (Answer A) is an inactive metabolite of vitamin D and would not cause hypercalcemia. Ectopic PTH (Answer B) is considerably less common than PTHrP in all types of malignancy. Local factors, such as PTHrP and transforming growth factor-β, can mediate osteoclast activity and bone resorption in malignancy. Elevated fibroblast growth factor 23 levels are associated with tumor-induced osteomalacia, which is an acquired disorder of renal phosphate wasting that typically occurs with mesenchymal tumors. Tumor-induced osteomalacia is not associated with hypercalcemia (thus, Answer D is incorrect). Hypercortisolism (Answer E) is associated with bone loss for a number of reasons including diminished osteoblast activity, enhanced renal calcium excretion, and secondary hyperparathyroidism. However, hypercalcemia is not a finding in hypercortisolism. Cholangiocarcinomas have not been reported to make ACTH, ectopic production of which is seen in malignancies of the lung and other tissues. Moreover, there is no evidence of hyperpigmentation in this patient, although in malignancy-associated ectopic ACTH and Cushing syndrome, the time course may be so rapid that dermal responses to the high ACTH are not evident.

#### Educational Objective:

Describe the mechanisms of bone metastasis and PTHrP-related hypercalcemia of malignancy.

udananathan G, Dasanu CA. Cholangiocarcinoma and paraneoplasation of median years. J. Gastroenterol. Med. 2005;12(3):549-583.

G, Guise TA. Hypercalcemia of malignancy and basic research on mechanisms of response. J. Gastroenterol. Med. 2005;12(3):549-583.

5 ANSWER: E) Do not start a statin; it has no cardiovascular risk and the role of statins to reduce cardiovascular risk has been studied in many groups of patients, and the role of statins to reduce cardiovascular disease and mortality in the settings of primary and secondary care. However, end-stage kidney disease (patients on the setings of primary and secondary care) is more likely to be associated with the risk of the risk of

The AURORA trial (A Study to Evaluate the Use of Rosuvastatin in Subjects on Regular Hemodialysis: An Assessment of Survival and Cardiovascular Events) evaluated rosuvastatin's effect on cardiovascular end points in patients receiving hemodialysis and found no benefit compared with observations in the placebo group. The Study of Heart and Renal Protection (SHARP) randomly assigned patients with chronic kidney disease 3 through 7 to placebo or simvastatin plus ezetimibe. Although there was evidence of benefit overall, the subgroup analysis suggested that this was mainly driven by the nondialysis group and the patients undergoing dialysis had no benefit. It is important to note that statins reduce the relative risk of cardiovascular disease to a similar extent in patients with predialysis chronic kidney disease as they do in patients without chronic kidney disease, but because patients with chronic kidney disease have a greater baseline risk, the absolute risk reduction with statin treatment is greater in patients with predialysis chronic kidney disease than in patients without chronic kidney disease.

Although several post hoc analyses of these trials have suggested potential benefit for various subgroups of patients receiving dialysis, conclusions from 3 major trials have not found a conclusive benefit for statin use in patients receiving dialysis. Statins are not routinely recommended; however, they are not contraindicated (thus, Answers D is incorrect). There are no guidelines for patients who are taking statins at the time of progression to dialysis; thus, the decision to continue or cease statin therapy should be made on a personalized basis. However, there is fairly good evidence that statin therapy improves cardiovascular survival after renal transplant and statin should be considered in that setting.

##### Educational Objective:

Advise patients on the use of statins in the setting of chronic kidney disease.

## 76 ANSWER: E) Raise glycemic targets in the short-term

76 Hypoglycemic unawareness is a common problem in patients with tightly controlled type 1 diabetes. Loss of adrenergic symptoms of hypoglycemia is more frequent with increasing duration of diabetes, and it is a major risk factor for severe hypoglycemia requiring assistance. Recurrent hypoglycemia reduces the normal counterregulatory hormone response of glucagon and epinephrine secretion to low glucose levels and decreases hypoglycemia awareness. Antecedent hypoglycemia was identified in the Diabetes Control and Complications Trial as the most common predictor of a future severe hypoglycemic event.

Although this patient's hemoglobin  $ A_{1c} $ level has run consistently in a good range, his hypoglycemic unawareness indicates that good mean glucose levels are most likely occurring at the expense of recurrent hypoglycemia. Prevention of future severe hypoglycemia, a potentially life-threatening complication of insulin

therapy, should be improved by hypoglycemia for periods as short as 3 days has been shown to improve hypoglycemia awareness for up to 3 months and therefore reduce the risk of future hypoglycemia. Relaxing glycemic control in the short-term by raising glucose targets to prevent hypoglycemia (Answer E) will help reduce his risk of a recurrent severe hypoglycemic event.

increased frequency of blood glucose monitoring is critical to alert the patient to times of the day when glucose levels are running low and thus guide insulin dose reductions. This patient would be best advised to perform fingerstick glucose monitoring at minimum before meals and at bedtime, before driving, and when he has any symptoms of hypoglycemia (with or without use of a continuous glucose monitor). Studies have documented that patients are often inaccurate when estimating their glucose concentration unless levels are very high or low. Therefore, dosing insulin lispro on the basis of the perceived blood glucose level (Answer C) would not be expected to reduce his frequency of hypoglycemia.

Continuous glucose monitors should be considered for patients with persistent hypoglycemia unawareness because these devices can alert patients to unrecognized hypoglycemia. Continuous glucose monitors have been shown to lower hemoglobin  $ A_{1c} $ levels in adults and children with type 1 diabetes without increasing the rate of mild or severe hypoglycemia, and have been shown to reduce the frequency of severe hypoglycemia. A continuous glucose monitoring device has been introduced that communicates with an insulin pump and automatically suspends the basal insulin infusion when the glucose level is low and unrecognized. Use of this device reduces the amount of time spent in a hypoglycemic state overnight.

This patient's insulin regimen is nonphysiologic, and he would benefit from a change. Physiologic insulin replacement using a long-acting basal insulin and premeal rapid-acting insulin has been shown to lower the risk of hypoglycemia when compared with NPH-based insulin regimens. Insulin pump therapy, the most physiologic of insulin replacement regimens, would similarly be expected to reduce hypoglycemia rates in this patient. The intake of high glycemic index foods will acutely raise glucose levels, but such a dietary intervention (Answer B) is not an effective way prevent hypoglycemia in a patient on a nonphysiologic insulin regimen, and would lead to excessive postprandial hyperglycemia.

If this patient is found to have marked glucose variability because of variable carbohydrate intake at mealtimes, having him adjust his bolus insulin for anticipated carbohydrate intake using an insulin-to-carbohydrate ratio should reduce glucose variability and rates of hypoglycemia. An insulin-to-carbohydrate ratio can be estimated from his total daily dose of insulin. A formula commonly used to calculate an individual patient's insulin-to-carbohydrate ratio is 500/total daily dose. His current total daily dose of 42 units would lead to a calculated insulin-to-carbohydrate ratio of about 1 unit per 12 g of carbohydrate. Given frequent hypoglycemia, his insulin-to-carbohydrate ratio may need to be reduced, perhaps to 1 unit per 14 or 15 g of carbohydrate (thus, Answer D is incorrect). Referral to a nutritionist would be helpful to guide carbohydrate counting.

Patients on insulin should be educated that exercise increases insulin sensitivity and reduces insulin requirements. Exercise is associated improvements in glycemic control and in cardiovascular risk factors. Rather than reducing the amount of prescribed exercise (Answer A), this patient would benefit from being educated in ways to prevent exercise-induced hypoglycemia. This would include reducing basal or prandial insulin-dosed based on when exercise is planned, or by supplementing with extra carbohydrates before and/or during exercise.

##### Educational Objective:

Manage hypoglycemic unawareness in patients with tightly controlled diabetes mellitus.

UpToDate Topic Review(s):

77 ANSWER: B) Start intravenous insulin infusion

77 ANSWER: B) Start intravenous insulin infusion

This patient has clinical evidence of dehydration, polyuria, and hypernatremia. These are all features that could be consistent with inadequately treated diabetes insipidus. However, she also has hyperglycemia that may reflect transient steroid-induced diabetes or unmasking of underlying diabetes mellitus by high-dosage corticosteroid treatment. In this setting, the urine osmolality is typically similar.

In this unusual situation, hyperglycemia and subsequent marked glucosuria can lead to an osmotic diuresis that is resistant to antidiuretic hormone or desmopressin. In this setting, the urine osmolality is typically similar to the plasma osmolality and polyuria is driven by the solute load. The administration of insulin (Answer B) to correct the hyperglycemia will restore responsiveness to antidiuretic hormone.

Given that there is resistance to antidiuretic hormone driven by glycosuria, the patient will not benefit from adjusting the desmopressin dosage or the method of administration (Answers D and E).

Patients with diabetes insipidus who are unwell may require treatment with intravenous dextrose and water (because intravenous sterile water without dextrose causes hemolysis). In adults, the intravenous administration of dextrose and water at a rate of more than 1000 mL/h (Answer A) results in a glucose delivery rate that exceeds endogenous metabolic capacity for glucose even in patients without diabetes mellitus. This can cause severe and symptomatic hyperglycemia and would exacerbate the hyperglycemia already seen in this case. In patients with diabetes insipidus, intravenous rates of fluid replacement with dextrose in water should be limited to a maximum of 500 to 750 mL/h.

500 to 750 mL/h.

Chlorpropamide (Answer C) may, at first, seem to be a reasonable treatment option because it is used in diabetes insipidus and it is also a glucose-lowering agent in the setting of diabetes mellitus. However, it is used in the management of nephrogenic diabetes insipidus where it increases sensitivity of the nephron to vasopressin, and it is not routinely used in the management of central diabetes insipidus. In addition, although in this vignette chlorpropamide may lower blood glucose and enhance responsiveness to exogenous vasopressin, its long duration of action and inherent risk of hypoglycemia means that it would not be safe. This patient's blood glucose would be more effectively and safely lowered by intravenous insulin.

Administer insulin in the setting of hyperglycemia that is reducing the effectiveness of antidiuretic hormone treatment of diabetes insipidus.

78 ANSWER: D) Initiate treatment with methyldopa for management of her blood pressure

79 Women with pregestational type 1 diabetes may encounter several risks in pregnancy. Preconception counseling is very important to educate women about these risks and preempt exacerbation of existing complications or the development of new ones.

Uncontrolled hypertension can be a serious complication of pregnancy in women with diabetes and can precipitate retinal, renal, and cardiac dysfunction. ACE inhibitors (Answer C) are teratogenic, so they are intraindicated for this patient who is contemplating pregnancy despite the presence of microalbuminuria. Editions other than ACE inhibitors are the mainstay of hypertension treatment during pregnancy. Specifically, thyldopa (Answer D) has been used for many years, and it is safe for both the mother and developing fetus. Her antihypertensive agents that can safely be used during pregnancy include calcium channel blockers and drenergic blockers such as hydralazine. β-Adrenergic blockers can be used with caution.

Physiologic ocular changes that can occur in pregnancy include alterations of corneal sensitivity and thickness, reased tolerance to contact lenses, decreased intraocular pressure, hermalopia, and refractive errors. Pregnancy exacerbate preexisting retinopathy. Some women with diabetes develop de novo retinopathy during pregnancy, the development of any new ocular symptoms should be assessed promptly. Thus, dilated eye examinations

should ideally be performed before and during the first trimester of pregnancy, even in women such as this one who do not have preexisting retinopathy (thus, Answer A is incorrect). Subsequent retinal examinations should be based on the results of the preliminary examination. Retinal examinations should not be delayed until after completion of the pregnancy, as this strategy would miss de novo retinopathy if it occurs. In the Diabetes in Early Pregnancy Study that examined 140 women without proliferative retinopathy at conception, progression of retinopathy occurred in 20% of those with microaneurysms or mild nonproliferative retinopathy and in 55% of those with moderate-to-severe nonproliferative retinopathy. Ten percent of women

Hyperglycemia during the first trimester of pregnancy is associated with a higher rate of fetal loss. For this and other reasons, avoiding elevated glucose levels is a key preconception goal. However, low hemoglobin  $ A_{1c} $ values (such as <6.0% [<0.060]) could be associated with significant hypoglycemic events and is generally not ideal for most women with type 1 diabetes (thus, Answer B is incorrect).

Her TSH level is appropriate for pregnancy, and increasing her levothyroxine dosage (Answer E) is not warranted now. However, there will be an anticipated increase in her thyroid requirement once she is pregnant, and this should be managed appropriately.

##### ANSWER: C) Change the current regimen to weekly injections of 100 mg

79 Intramuscular testosterone esters have been used for the treatment of hypogonadism for the past 7 decades. Injections are the least expensive modality of androgen replacement. Additionally, on-treatment serum testosterone levels with injections are predictable and reflect the dose injected. Some of the most common adverse effects of testosterone esters are fluctuations in mood, energy levels, and sexual function that occur a few days before the next injection. These are due to pharmacokinetic properties of these esters that consist of peaks and troughs ("roller-coaster" pharmacokinetics). For example, after administration of an injection, serum testosterone concentrations rapidly increase, often exceeding the upper normal limit. Patients generally feel well during this period and often report "a boost in energy," although there have been some reports of irritability, insomnia, and anger during this phase. Testosterone levels are then maintained in the serum via gradual release from their muscle depot. However, before the next dose, these concentrations significantly decrease, often falling below the lower normal limit. These fluctuations are more pronounced when a larger dose of testosterone is administered at a lower frequency, as is the case with this patient. The premise behind this regimen is to limit the burden of frequent injections to avoid discomfort to the patient. Pharmacokinetic studies of testosterone esters have shown that the dosage of 100 mg weekly results in testosterone concentrations within the normal range with minimal fluctuations (Figure). Therefore, decreasing the testosterone dose and increasing the frequency of its administration (Answer C) will most likely benefit this patient and is the best next step in his care.

Although his serum testosterone level at mid-point (1 week postinjection) is normal, his symptoms are due to subtherapeutic levels before the next dose. Hence, continuing the current regimen (Answer A) is not optimal. Increasing his testosterone dosage to 400 mg every 4 weeks (Answer D) would result in even greater peaks and troughs (Figure) and is likely to worsen his symptoms.

The monthly cost of transdermal testosterone gel (Answer E) is as high as the cost of the testosterone patch and would not be financially feasible for this patient. The pharmacokinetics of testosterone enanthate are similar to those of testosterone cypionate. Hence, switching esters will not address his problem (Answer B).

## 80 ANSWER: E) TSH-secreting adenoma

The unifying diagnosis that would explain all the data presented in this vignette is development of a TSH-secreting adenoma (Answer E) superimposed on a preexisting diagnosis of Hashimoto hypothyroidism. The patient's serum TSH concentrations have been increasing despite an escalation in her levothyroxine dosage, thus suggesting autonomous TSH production. The headaches that the patient is experiencing, along with the recent motor vehicle crashes perhaps related to altered peripheral vision (bitemporal hemianopsia), could indicate a space-occupying lesion within the sella turcica. In a series of 25 patients with TSH-secreting adenomas followed up at the National Institutes of Health, headaches and visual field deficits occurred in 39% and 37% of patients, respectively. A pituitary MRI would be the next step to determine whether this patient does, in fact, have a pituitary adenoma. A visual field examination would determine whether such a lesion, if present, was associated with visual field defects.

TSH-secreting tumors usually present in a hyperthyroid patient who has an inappropriately normal or elevated TSH level in the face of an elevated free T $ _{4} $ level. In some instances, the patient may have previously undergone

therapy for hyperthyroidism. The patient presented here had a preexisting diagnosis of Hashimoto hypothyroidism. Presumably, her functional pituitary tumor gradually manifested over the years as a trend for needing higher levothyroxine dosages intended to return her serum TSH value to the reference range. It is the consequence of increasing levothyroxine replacement that has generated her hyperthyroidism, not the endogenous thyroid hormone production as described earlier, as presumably the patient has minimal functional thyroid tissue as a result of her Hashimoto disease.

Anomalous or discordant results from thyroid function studies have a wide differential diagnosis that includes assay problems, interfering drugs, regimen adherence issues, TSH-secreting tumors, and resistance to thyroid hormone. Both TSH and free T₄ concentrations in the upper part of the reference range can be seen when a patient resumes levothyroxine therapy, possibly with addition of “make-up” doses after a period of abstinence. However, this is not the best answer in this vignette because her free T₄ levels do track appropriately with the changing levothyroxine dosage. Moreover, patients who intermittently take thyroid hormone replacement would not manifest constant hyperthyroidism (thus, Answer A is incorrect). Similarly, altered absorption of levothyroxine (Answer B) is incorrect because her free T₄ concentration has increased beyond the upper end of the reference range.

is incorrect because Prituitary resistance to thyroid hormone (Answer C) is a rare disorder. Seventy-five percent of these cases are dominantly inherited, such that an abnormal pattern of thyroid function is often seen in first-degree relatives. The onset of abnormal thyroid function would not occur in adulthood, as it has in this patient. Potential means of distinguishing between a TSH-secreting pituitary adenoma and resistance to thyroid hormone include documenting a high pituitary glycoprotein hormone α-subunit level, a high sex hormone-binding globulin level, and an attenuated response to thyrotropin-releasing hormone injection—findings that are characteristic of TSH-secreting tumors. Certain antibodies such as heterophilic antibodies or human anti-animal antibodies can interfere with immunometric 2-site TSH assays and give a falsely elevated TSH value. Normally, TSH binds to both a capture antibody and a detection antibody, thus generating a true signal. These interfering antibodies similarly bind both the capture and detection antibodies, generating a false signal and artificially elevating the TSH reading. This explanation would not account for the patient's headaches and the continual upward trend in her serum TSH values (thus, Answer D is incorrect).

##### Educational Objective:

Develop a differential diagnosis for a patient who has elevated concentrations of TSH and free  $ T_{4} $ while being treated with levothyroxine.

UpToDate Topic Review(s):

Thyrotropin (TSH)-secreting pituitary adenomas

##### Reference(s):

81 ANSWER: B) Order a selective arterial calcium stimulation test

In patients with recurrent hypoglycemia 14 months after resection of a solitary insulinoma, the differential diagnoses are: (1) a “fractured” insulinoma (ie, residual tissue is left after attempted enucleation), (2) another insulinoma exists that was missed at the time of initial evaluation has now led to symptoms, or (3) metastatic disease has now progressed to the point that the patient has symptoms. Given these possibilities, initiating medical therapy with its attendant cost and comorbidity (fluid retention, nausea, bloating, hair growth, and breakthrough hypoglycemia) without attempting to regionalize the source of abnormal insulin secretion is inappropriate (thus, Answer A is incorrect). Considering the negative imaging studies obtained thus far, performing a selective arterial calcium stimulation test (Answer B) would be reasonable. Assuming that regionalization is congruent with the previous tumor location, it would make sense to proceed with surgical reexploration. On the basis of information

available in the vignette, there is no indication for an extended distal pancreatectomy (Answer D) or Whipple procedure (Answer E). Ethanol ablation under guidance of endoscopic ultrasonography (Answer C) would be indicated if this imaging modality could visualize residual tissue, but this is unlikely to be the case in the short interval since surgery.

Educational Objective:

Evaluate recurrent hypoglycemia after resection of an insulinoma.

Insulinoma

## 82 ANSWER: D) Refer to medical oncology for adjuvant chemotherapy

82 Parathyroid carcinoma is a rare cause of primary hyperparathyroidism that is seen in less than 1% to 2% of cases. Affected persons typically present in the fourth and fifth decades of life with high serum calcium and markedly elevated PTH concentrations. A palpable neck mass may be present along with overt renal and bone disease. The incidence of parathyroid carcinoma may be increasing because of earlier detection, although these are often rapidly growing. An elevated PTH level in the presence of hypercalcemia is seen in both benign primary hyperparathyroidism and parathyroid carcinoma, although PTH levels in the latter are often greater and the degree of hypercalcemia more severe. Nonetheless, the 2 conditions overlap in severity such that there is no single level of PTH or calcium that is diagnostic of either condition. Additionally, 1% of parathyroid cancers are nonfunctioning and are not identified by elevated PTH levels or symptomatic hypercalcemia and are ultimately diagnosed on the basis of a large and rapidly expanding neck mass.

Parathyroid sestamibi scintigraphy may not reliably distinguish parathyroid adenoma from carcinoma, and if parathyroid carcinoma is suspected, then a tissue diagnosis is required (thus, Answer C is incorrect). FNAB of suspected parathyroid carcinomas is associated with cutaneous spread of carcinoma along the needle tract (thus, Answer A is incorrect). Surgery is required for a definitive diagnosis, and stratification of patients at low or high risk depends on invasion into surrounding tissue or distant metastasis. Therefore, referral for nodule resection and neck dissection (Answer D) is the best management plan in this vignette. Standard parathyroidectomy does not entail evaluation of local and regional lymph nodes. In most centers, the surgical approach includes en bloc resection of the parathyroid tumor and removal of any tissue invaded by tumor. This may additionally include ipsilateral thyroidectomy and lymph node dissection with or without resection of the recurrent laryngeal nerve. Parathyroid carcinomas are difficult to treat beyond surgical cure. That is, failing total resection, most of these tumors progress and are ultimately fatal either due to the tumor metastasizing or to the sequelae of hypercalcemia. Chemotherapy (Answer E) is generally ineffective in this condition.

Calcitonin is a tumor marker essential for the diagnosis and follow-up of medullary thyroid cancer. Measuring calcitonin would be appropriate if the patient had a family history of hypercalcemia or other features that would raise suspicion of multiple endocrine neoplasia type 2A. Otherwise, a tissue diagnosis from nodule resection and neck dissection would still be the best next step (thus, Answer B is incorrect).

Interestingly, recent data suggest a genetic basis for some parathyroid carcinomas. Family members of a person with an identified CDC73 disease-causing mutation may benefit from genetic testing: absence of the mutation provides reassurance and presence of the mutation prompts a recommendation for prospective surveillance. Lifelong surveillance recommendations for persons in whom a CDC73 mutation is detected include measurement of serial calcium and PTH levels every 6 months with a low threshold for parathyroid surgery. Surveillance is recommended regardless of whether a patient with a CDC73 mutation has hypercalcemia.

##### Educational Objective:

Determine the best diagnostic and therapeutic approach to a neck mass that is most likely a parathyroid carcinoma.

Wei CH, Harari A. Clinical and molecular genetics of parathyroid neoplasms. Best Pract Res Clin Endocrinol Metab. 2010;24(3):491-502. Sharretts JM, Simonds WF. Cancer and Its Influences on the Development of a Comprehensive Epidemiology System. J Pediatrics 2012;13(1):11-23.

Guernieri V, Battista C, Muscarella LA, et al. CDC73 mutations and parafibromin immunohistochemistry in parathyroid tumors: clinical correlations in a single-centre patient cohort. Cell Oncol (Dordbr). 2012;35(6):411-422.

## 83 ANSWER: B) Add a hormonal contraceptive

83 The therapeutic goals for women with classic congenital adrenal hyperplasia are to maintain regular menses and alleviate symptoms of androgen excess while avoiding the complications of glucocorticoid replacement (eg, iatrogenic Cushing syndrome, dyslipidemia, glucose intolerance, hypertension, obesity, bone loss, and cardiovascular disease). This patient has no signs of adrenal insufficiency but does have signs of androgen excess including irregular menses, hirsutism, and acne, which are her main concerns. Her testosterone level is also elevated; therefore, additional treatment of androgen excess is needed. However, increasing the glucocorticoid dosage (Answer C) to suppress adrenal androgens is not the best choice for treating elevated testosterone in this patient who has no symptoms of adrenal insufficiency. Women with congenital adrenal hyperplasia can develop secondary ovarian androgen excess resulting in elevated testosterone despite appropriate glucocorticoid replacement. Hormonal contraception (Answer B) is the treatment of choice for hirsutism. It suppresses ovarian androgens and increases sex hormone-binding globulin, which decreases free testosterone. Hormonal contraception will also protect the uterus, preventing endometrial hyperplasia when menses are irregular. Hormonal contraception is therefore the best treatment strategy in this patient.

The goal of mineralocorticoid replacement with fludrocortisone is to suppress renin so that there is no hypovolemia driving ACTH secretion. In this patient, the renin activity is suppressed, blood pressure is normal, there are no orthostatic symptoms, and potassium is normal, which suggests that mineralocorticoids are not over-replaced or under-replaced. Therefore, there is no need to increase the fludrocortisone dosage (Answer A).

Glucocorticoid replacement for congenital adrenal hyperplasia can be given in many different forms. Hydrocortisone has the shortest half-life and is least likely to result in over-replacement. Although the short half-life warrants dosing 3 times per day, it is often tolerated very well when given twice daily in adults with congenital adrenal hyperplasia. Therefore, there is no need to increase the hydrocortisone dosage (Answer C). The glucocorticoid dosage should not be titrated to suppress the 17-hydroxyprogesterone into the normal range because the 17-hydroxyprogesterone levels tend to be much higher than androgen levels and normalization will result in over-treatment. Synthetic, long-acting glucocorticoids (Answer D) may suppress androgens better than hydrocortisone, particularly the increased androgens in the morning when ACTH peaks. However, these long-acting glucocorticoids are more likely to cause iatrogenic Cushing syndrome.

Spironolactone (Answer E), used off-label, does act as an antiandrogen and decreases the testosterone action at the receptor level. It can be used in women with congenital adrenal hyperplasia, but it must be paired with increased fludrocortisone because of its diuretic effect. Spironolactone can also result in ambiguous genitalia in a male infant, and it should therefore be used with contraception in female patients.

##### Educational Objective:

Select appropriate treatment for a woman with classic congenital adrenal hyperplasia transitioning to adult care.

UpToDate Topic Review(s):

Treatment of classic congenital adrenal hyperplasia due to 21-hydroxylase deficiency in adults

##### Reference(s):

Auchus RJ. Management of the adult with congenital adrenal hyperplasia. Int J Pediatr Endocrinol. 2010;614107.

Auchus RJ, Arlt W. Approach to the patient: the adult with congenital adrenal hyperplasia. J Clin Endocrinol Metab. 2013;98(7):2645-2655.

Martin KA, Change RJ, Ehrmann DA, Ibanez L, Lobo RA, Rosenfield RL, Shapiro J, Montori VM, Swiglo BA. Evaluation and treatment of hirsutism in premenopausal women: an Endocrine Society clinical practice guideline. J Clin Endocrinol Metab. 2008;93(4):1105-1120.

## 84 ANSWER: A) ABCD1 (adrenoleukodystrophy) This

This child presents with primary adrenal insufficiency. Statistically, the most likely cause of primary adrenal failure in children is classic congenital adrenal hyperplasia, which can be due to mutations in the CYP21A2 gene (most common) (Answer D) or the HSD3B2 gene (Answer E), which encode the enzymes 21α-hydroxylase and 3β-hydroxysteroid-dehydrogenase, respectively. However, classic congenital adrenal hyperplasia due to these enzyme defects presents in the neonatal period with adrenal insufficiency and salt wasting, as well as ambiguous genitalia in girls. Therefore, given that this child did not present with adrenal failure until age 9 years, classic congenital adrenal hyperplasia is an incorrect diagnosis. Nonclassic congenital adrenal hyperplasia can present with hyperandrogenism at the time of puberty, or shortly thereafter, but does not present with adrenal insufficiency.

The combination of primary adrenal insufficiency in a child with a behavioral disorder and mildly abnormal neurologic findings makes adrenoleukodystrophy (ALD) the most likely underlying cause. ALD is an X-linked disorder caused by mutations in the ABCD1 gene (Answer A). Mutations in ABCD1 prevent transport of very long chain fatty acids into peroxisomes, thus inhibiting their oxidation and breakdown. The subsequent accumulation of very long chain fatty acids in affected organs (CNS, Leydig cells of the testes, and adrenal cortex) results in the various clinical presentations of ALD, which can be categorized into 3 main forms:

#### Childhood Cerebral Forms (35%)

Childhood cerebral forms of ALD typically present in children between the ages of 4 and 8 years. The initial neurologic manifestations are learning disabilities and behavioral disorders that may be labeled as attention deficit hyperactivity disorder, as was the case in this vignette. Subsequent neurologic deterioration ensues (cognitive impairment, blindness, quadriparesis), and death occurs within 5 to 10 years of diagnosis. Most affected persons also have primary adrenal insufficiency.

#### Adrenomyeloneuropathy (40%-45%)

adrenomyeloneuropathy usually presents in adulthood with a peak age of onset of 20 to 40 years. The neurologic abnormalities seen in adrenomyeloneuropathy are less severe than those in the childhood cerebral forms of ALD and typically consist of spinal cord dysfunction with eventual spastic paraparesis. Gonadal and adrenal dysfunction also typical.

#### Isolated Adrenal Insufficiency (10%)

drenal insufficiency occurs in isolation in up to 10% of ALD cases. Adrenal insufficiency usually presents at a young age (before age 7.5 years), but it can occasionally present in adulthood. Therefore, boys who present with parent Addison disease in early childhood should be evaluated for ALD.

LD is diagnosed by measuring very long chain fatty acid levels in either blood or fibroblasts. These are elevated nearly all males with the disorder and in 85% of affected females (although females with an  $ ABCD1 $ mutation are carriers and are usually not seriously affected). In most affected male patients, the combination of typical clinical features and markedly elevated very long chain fatty acid levels is sufficient to establish the diagnosis of -linked ALD. To confirm the clinical diagnosis and to facilitate genetic counseling, molecular genetic testing of  $ ABCD1 $ gene may be performed. Genetic testing is particularly important in patients with borderline very long chain fatty acid levels or atypical features, as well as in female patients.

Mutations in the NR0B1 gene (also known as DAXI) (Answer B) and the SF1 gene (Answer C) are associated with congenital adrenal hypoplasia. This is also a rare X-linked disorder that presents with adrenal insufficiency but no neurologic or behavioral findings. Affected patients develop hypogonadotropic hypogonadism that is evident at puberty. Thus, the presence of apparent attention deficit hyperactivity disorder and abnormal neurologic findings make ALD a more likely diagnosis in this vignette.

##### educational Objective:

Identify X-linked adrenoleukodystrophy as a cause of primary adrenal insufficiency presenting in childhood and

adolescence.

##### ToDate Topic Review(s):

drenoleukodystrophy

Berger J, Gärtner J. X-linked adrenoleukodystrophy: clinical, biochemical and pathogenetic aspects. Biochim Biophys Acta. 2006;1763:1721-1732. Moser HW, Loes DJ, Melhem ER. X-Linked adrenoleukodystrophy: overview and prognosis as a function of age and brain magnetic resonance imaging abnormality. A study involving 372 patients. Neuropediatrics. 2000;31(5):227-239.

85 This patient has a large nontoxic multinodular goiter associated with retrosternal extension, tracheal deviation, and mild tracheal compression. The symptoms and signs associated with a goiter depend on the size and location. Direct compression of the trachea or esophagus (most commonly by substernal goiter) can worsen dysphagia to solids, positional dyspnea, and dysphonia. Dyspnea, which may be reported as orthopnea, is the most common symptom. Positional dyspnea, defined as difficulty breathing at rest improved by position change, is more common with retrosternal goiter and is highly associated with tracheal compression on cross-sectional imaging. Obesity may also contribute to positional dyspnea. Dysphagia or difficulty swallowing solids is commonly associated with goitrous compression, but it is also common in elderly patients for a variety of reasons. Similar to dyspnea, dysphagia is more often seen in substernal goiters than in cervical goiters. Hoarseness or dysphonia is a change in quality or volume of voice and requires indirect or fiberoptic laryngoscopy to evaluate laryngeal nerve function and/or alternative causes such as laryngeal neoplasm. The Pemberton maneuver is a physical examination tool demonstrating latent pressure in the thoracic inlet and is achieved by asking the patient to elevate both arms until they touch the sides of the face. A positive Pemberton sign is marked by the presence of facial congestion and cyanosis, as well as respiratory distress after approximately 1 minute. A positive Pemberton sign is indicative of superior vena cava syndrome, commonly due to a mass in the mediastinum. The sign most often indicates the presence of a retrosternal goiter where the goiter “corks off” the thoracic inlet, it but may be positive in any patient with adenopathy, tumor, or fibrosis involving the mediastinum.

The diagnostic evaluation of goiter includes the measurement of serum TSH to identify evidence of thyroid dysfunction. The standard imaging modality for assessment of thyroid enlargement is thyroid ultrasonography, which is useful in determining the number and size of thyroid nodules, as well as identifying features suggestive of thyroid malignancy. Because ultrasonography is limited in cases with substernal extension, cross-sectional imaging should be considered and is frequently helpful, especially in assessing for mass effect on the trachea and determining the diameter of the trachea. Tracheal compression is described as the greatest percentage reduction in tracheal diameter and is more common in substernal goiter than in cervical goiter. Earlier reports recommended thyroidectomy for any degree of luminal narrowing on CT imaging, but more recent studies have found that positional dyspnea improves or resolves after surgery for substernal goiter, varied stepwise with the degree of preoperative tracheal compression. Narrowing of less than 35% is associated with a 65% to 70% likelihood of resolution of positional dyspnea, whereas narrowing of 35% or greater is associated with a 95% to 98% chance of resolution after resection. A consensus statement concludes that surgery may be reasonable for patients with smaller degrees of tracheal compression if they are symptomatic or for younger patients to prevent progression. Asymptomatic, elderly, and/or medically infirm patients may be followed up conservatively for evidence of progression. The patient described in this vignette has relatively minor symptoms: his CT scan shows less than 35% (20%) narrowing of the tracheal lumen and he does not want to undergo surgery. Therefore, conservatively assessing his rate of progression in 6 months is the best strategy (Answer C is correct and Answer D is incorrect).

A prolonged course of prednisone is useful in the management of chronic obstructive pulmonary disease. However the clinical features described in the vignette do not suggest that the dyspnea is due to worsening or uncontrolled pulmonary disease. Corticosteroid treatment is of limited benefit in reducing the inflammation associated with large retrosternal goiters, but it may be used after radioiodine administration (thus, Answer A is incorrect).

Medical treatment options for management of nontoxic goiter include administration of suppressive dosages of levothyroxine and radioiodine treatment. Thyroid hormone suppressive treatment is the less effective treatment, and when used long-term, it is associated with adverse effects on the cardiovascular system and bone mineral density caused by iatrogenic thyrotoxicosis. Therefore, levothyroxine therapy (Answer B) is generally not recommended for long-term treatment of nontoxic multinodular goiter.

Radioactive iodine ablation treatment of goiter is associated with a 40% to 60% reduction in volume within 2 years of therapy, but most shrinkage occurs within the first few months of treatment. Pretreatment with

recombinant human TSH may reduce the required radioiodine dose, potentially reducing the risk of complications to other organs from radiation exposure. In the absence of sufficient evidence of clinically significant tracheal compression and narrowing, it is more appropriate to adopt a conservative approach with clinical and radiologic re-evaluation in 6 months (thus, Answer E is incorrect).

##### Educational Objective:

Interpret clinical and radiologic findings related to retrosternal goiter and manage nontoxic goiter associated with mild tracheal compression.

##### UpToDate Topic Review(s):

Diagnostic approach to and treatment of goiter in adults

##### ANSWER: E) Basal insulin with titration instructions

86 A

Patients with type 2 diabetes mellitus have lost a clinically significant degree of insulin secretion by the time of diagnosis. The progressive decline in β-cell function after diabetes diagnosis contributes to failure of antihyperglycemic therapies over time. Insulin is commonly required to attain treatment targets with increasing duration of disease. The effectiveness of a new antihyperglycemic agent is determined to a significant degree by the baseline hemoglobin  $ A_{1c} $. Studies have shown significantly greater hemoglobin  $ A_{1c} $ reductions with basal insulin (Answer E) when compared with adding a third oral agent or a glucagonlike peptide 1 agonist (Answer C), especially when baseline hemoglobin  $ A_{1c} $ levels are 9.5% or greater ( $ \geq0.095 $). When compared with addition of a basal insulin in patients with type 2 diabetes who have a hemoglobin  $ A_{1c} $ level below 9.5% (<0.095), the addition of a glucagonlike peptide 1 agonist to oral agent therapy is associated with weight loss rather than weight gain, less hypoglycemia, and comparable or better lowering of hemoglobin  $ A_{1c} $ levels, even though fasting glucose levels run lower in basal insulin-treated patients.

In patients with type 2 diabetes and a baseline mean hemoglobin  $ A_{1c} $ level of 8.3% (0.083), a head-to-head study showed that basal insulin added to oral agent therapy led to less weight gain and edema and more hypoglycemia than the addition of a thiazolidinedione (Answer D), with comparable improvement of glycemic control.

No studies have been conducted that compare the addition of a sodium-glucose cotransporter 2 inhibitor (Answer B) vs basal insulin in patients with type 2 diabetes uncontrolled by oral agents. Addition of a sodium-glucose cotransporter 2 inhibitor leads to a modest reduction in hemoglobin  $ A_{1c} $ (0.4%-0.9%) and would not be expected to provide adequate glycemic control in this patient.

The frequency of hypoglycemia increases after the addition of basal or prandial insulin to oral antihyperglycemic agents, at a rate proportional to the degree of glycemic control targeted. Basal insulins added to oral agents lead to comparable hemoglobin  $ A_{1c} $ lowering compared with rapid-acting insulin analogues (Answer A), with less hypoglycemia and less weight gain. Self-titration of basal insulin has been shown to be safe and effective in a number of treat-to-target studies. In this patient, whose high fasting glucose levels reflect the degree of elevation in hemoglobin  $ A_{1c} $, addition of basal insulin alone to his oral agent regimen is a good treatment option, although his regimen may need to be changed to multiple daily injections in the future.

##### Educational Objective:

Recommend basal insulin in a patient with type 2 diabetes mellitus that is uncontrolled with oral agent therapy.

Donner T, Muñoz M. Update on insulin therapy for type 2 diabetes. J Clin Endocrinol Metab. 2012;97(5):1405-1413. {J, et al; 4-T Study Gro.

## 87 ANSWER: D) History of deep venous thrombosis

87 Bariatric surgery has become an accepted treatment for the management of medically complicated obesity. The potential benefits regarding weight-related comorbidities such as type 2 diabetes, obstructive sleep apnea, and others are well recognized. Equally important is recognition of the risk factors associated with higher perioperative morbidity and mortality. Patients should fully understand their risks and physicians must recognize opportunities for intervention that can modify the risks.

The most common cause of perioperative mortality associated with bariatric surgery is thromboembolic disease, particularly pulmonary embolism. This patient has a history of deep venous thrombosis (Answer D). The second most common cause of death is anastomotic leaks. For patients who develop an anastomotic leak, the risk of pulmonary embolism rises significantly. The two most recognized risk factors associated with a higher morbidity and mortality are a history of deep venous thrombosis or pulmonary embolism and untreated obstructive sleep apnea (thus, Answer A is incorrect). Impaired functional status with the inability to walk 200 feet has consistently been shown to increase perioperative risk. Extreme BMI values are also associated with higher perioperative risks; however, the definition has varied among studies from BMI values greater than 50 kg/m² to values greater than 60 kg/m² (thus, Answer C is incorrect). A history of cigarette smoking (Answer E) has not been identified as a strong risk factor for higher perioperative mortality; however, it can increase the risk for anastomotic complications. Thus, smoking cessation is recommended. Among comorbidities, a history of coronary artery disease (Answer B) has not been associated with increased morbidity and mortality.

##### Educational Objective:

Advise patients contemplating bariatric surgery regarding risk factors for higher perioperative mortality.

UpToDate Topic Review(s):

Short-term medical outcomes following bariatric surgery

The Longitudinal Assessment of Bariatric Surgery (LABS) Consortium. Perioperative safety in the longitudinal assessment of bariatric surgery. N Engl J Med. 2009;361(5):445-454.

Finks JF, English WJ, Carlin AM, et al; Michigan Bariatric Surgery Collaborative: Center for Healthcare Outcomes and Policy. Predicting risk for venous thromboembolism with bariatric surgery: results from the Michigan Bariatric Surgery Collaborative. Ann Surg. 2012;255(6):1100-1104.

## 88 ANSWER: D) Octreotide TSH-secreting tumors are

TSH-secreting tumors are rare, accounting for less than 1% of pituitary adenomas. Results of preoperative laboratory testing and pathologic examination of this patient's tumor (results not included in the vignette) confirmed a TSH-secreting tumor. There is no reason to suspect a primary thyroid etiology of the hyperthyroidism or direct therapy at the thyroid itself unless efforts to control the tumor are unsuccessful.

Surgical treatment, typically by a transsphenoidal route, is the initial treatment of choice for TSH-secreting tumors. In patients not cured by surgery, treatment with a depot somatostatin analogue, such as octreotide (Answer D) is usually effective in controlling TSH hypersecretion and thus thyroid hormone levels. Octreotide can control hyperthyroidism and its symptoms by inhibiting TSH secretion from the tumor. Somatostatin analogues are not only

effective inhibitors of GH secretion, but also of TSH secretion. In one series of tumors, 21 of 25 patients treated with octreotide experienced normalized thyroid function for 61 months (the length of the study). Lanreotide also effectively reduces TSH secretion and shrinks tumors in one-third of patients.

In the past, secondary hyperthyroidism was often treated with radioactive iodine (Answer B) or antithyroid drugs (Answer A). Although these treatments can reduce  $ T_{4} $ levels and signs and symptoms of hyperthyroidism, thyrotoxicosis returns in most patients. These treatments inhibit the negative feedback of  $ T_{3} $ on TSH and can increase TSH production from the tumor. Antithyroid drugs might quickly lower thyroid levels, but therapy directed at the tumor is more desirable since somatostatin analogues can be used as very long-term therapy for TSH-secreting tumors. Saturated solution of potassium iodide (Answer E) would only control hyperthyroidism temporarily.

This patient has symptoms of hyperthyroidism, so a therapy directed at control soon is indicated. Gamma-knife radiotherapy (Answer C) could be considered for long-term disease control if somatostatin analogues are ineffective or not desired, but it would not be appropriate treatment to control hyperthyroidism now.

Educational Objective:

Summarize the recommended medical treatment options for TSH-secreting pituitary tumors.

UpToDate Topic Review(s):

Thyrotropin (TSH)-secreting pituitary adenomas

Reference(s):

Beck-Peccoz P, Persani L, Mannavola D, Campi I. Pituitary tumours: TSH-secreting adenomas. Best Pract Res Clin Endocrinol Metab. 2009;23(5):597-606.

89 Answer: B) Sodium, 126 mEq/L (126 mmol/L); Potassium, 5.2 mEq/L (5.2 mmol/L); Cortisol, 0.9 µg/dL (24.8 nmol/L); ACTH, 456 pg/mL (100.3 pmol/L); Aldosterone, 2.6 ng/dL (72.1 pmol/L)

Before the introduction of CT, bilateral adrenal hemorrhage (as seen in the vignette's image) was often fatal and identified only at postmortem examination. Adrenal hemorrhage was initially identified in association with fulminant meningococccemia in children (Waterhouse-Friderichsen syndrome), but this is a rare event now. Adrenal hemorrhage is usually seen in patients with a history of thromboembolic disease or recent surgery requiring either therapeutic or prophylactic anticoagulation. Coagulopathies such as heparin-induced thrombocytopenia and antiphospholipid syndrome associated with lupus anticoagulant are also well-appreciated causes of bilateral adrenal hemorrhage. The symptoms are often not dramatic with pain of varying severity in the abdomen, flank, lower back, or chest. There are usually few clues on physical examination except for a low-grade fever. Interestingly, hypotension is a late finding and usually heralds the onset of catastrophic shock that can be mistaken for septic shock.

Primary adrenocortical insufficiency is always expected in these patients with subnormal cortisol and elevated ACTH, which is illustrated in the laboratory profile of Answer B. The most common laboratory findings are hyponatremia and hyperkalemia related primarily to cortisol deficiency and the lack of glucocorticoid negative feedback on vasopressin, as well as mineralocorticoid deficiency contributing to decreased potassium clearance. The mechanisms associated with adrenal hemorrhage are multifactorial: because each adrenal gland has only 1 vein, any rise in venous pressure with or without adrenal vein thrombosis creates a “vascular dam” and, in the presence of a coagulopathy (endogenous or exogenous), it will lead to a possible hemorrhagic event. Stress with increases in adrenal blood flow may contribute to the pathogenesis. The eccentric muscular arrangement in the adrenal vein may make it particularly vulnerable to the formation of platelet thrombi, especially when there is turbulence and stasis. Of course, the adrenal veins have very high epinephrine levels, which may also contribute to platelet aggregation.

Hyperatremia (Answer C) would not be expected in patients with bilateral adrenal hemorrhage, but it might be expected in patients with hypothalamic-pituitary shock with secondary adrenal insufficiency and neurogenic diabetes insipidus associated with severe anoxic brain injury. Pituitary apoplexy (Answer E) might also cause secondary adrenal insufficiency in the setting of anticoagulation. Bilateral adrenal enlargement can certainly be seen in patients with ACTH-dependent Cushing syndrome (Answer A), but the adrenal enlargement is usually symmetric and follows the contour of the adrenal glands. Critically ill patients may also have dissociation between cortisol and aldosterone (Answer D) with preferential secretion of cortisol. Critically ill patients maintain adequate cortisol secretion despite ACTH levels that actually decline during the course of their stay in the intensive care unit. This is related to several factors including increased adrenal sensitivity to ACTH, as well as decreased renal and hepatic metabolic clearance of cortisol during critical illness.

Diagnose bilateral expected from primary adrenal insomnia.

Causes of primary adrenal insufficiency (Addison's disease)

E. Vervenne H, Meersseman P, et al. Reduced cortisol metabolism during critical illness. N Engl J Med. 2013;368(16):1477-1488.

## 90 ANSWER: D) Autoimmune thyroid disease with current predominance of thyrotropin receptor-stimulating antibodies

It is certainly unusual for a patient who had been euthyroid while maintained on a midrange levothyroxine dosage to be capable of maintaining a euthyroid state off any levothyroxine. However, the patient's initial TSH and TPO antibody elevation, combined with a levothyroxine requirement for 8 years, lends support to the correctness of her original diagnosis. A shift from hypothyroidism to hyperthyroidism with transition to a euthyroid state in between, although rare, has been described in the literature. This may be caused by fluctuating concentrations of thyrotropin receptor-blocking antibodies and thyrotropin receptor-stimulating antibodies, such that a euthyroid state occurs when the 2 antibodies are in balance, and a hyperthyroid phase is seen when thyrotropin receptor-stimulating antibodies predominate. Thyrotropin receptor-blocking antibodies, however, were not measured in this patient when Hashimoto hypothyroidism was first diagnosed. In addition, a shift between hypothyroidism, euthyroidism, and hyperthyroidism associated with alteration of thyrotropin receptor-stimulating antibody concentration or activity in persons with TPO antibodies has also been reported. This may be the explanation for the sequence of events described in the vignette, with a patient who was euthyroid on levothyroxine replacement now maintaining euthyroidism without levothyroxine (Answer D). This scenario would also explain why the patient had a slightly enlarged thyroid gland despite having been treated for hypothyroidism for 8 years.

A 2008 survey suggests that use of over-the-counter medications and dietary supplements is quite common. In this survey, 46% of those taking prescription medications also used over-the-counter medications, while 52% were concurrently taking dietary supplements. Dietary supplements or nutraceuticals that are marketed as enhancing thyroid function may contain iodine, tyrosine, thyroid hormone extract, or thyroid hormone analogues. Given these types of statistics, it is wise for the physician to inquire whether a patient is taking supplements of any kind, particularly because interactions between these supplements and prescribed medications can be hazardous. In addition, if clinical findings or laboratory results do not make sense, it is reasonable to investigate the possibility of supplement use.

Liothyronine (Answer A) is a prescription medication that is not used as standard or care because of its short half-life and the difficulty in avoiding intermittent thyrotoxicosis because of the peaks associated with its administration. If this patient were taking liothyronine at the time of her last laboratory test, her total T₃ level would be expected to be higher, while her free T₄ level would be expected to be lower. Therefore, liothyronine is unlikely to be the explanation. A similar disproportionate T₃ thyrotoxicosis would also be expected to be associated with thyroid extract (Answer E). Tiratricol (Answer B) is a metabolite of T₄ with intrinsic thymomimetic activity that has been used for “thyroid stimulation” and weight loss. The US Food and Drug Administration has banned products containing tiratricol, but they can still be obtained through various sources. However, if this patient were taking tiratricol alone at the time of her last laboratory assessment, her free T₄ concentration would be expected to be lower. The recommended daily allowance of iodine is 150 mcg. Large iodine doses are sometimes taken by patients in an attempt to stimulate thyroid function, and these excessive doses can lead to either hypothyroidism or hyperthyroidism. Hypothyroidism typically ensues if the patient has underlying Hashimoto thyroiditis, whereas hyperthyroidism may be precipitated in individuals with underlying thyroid autonomy such as Graves disease. It

would appear unlikely that this patient's current thyroid test results are associated with an iodine overload (Answer C) because her urinary iodine excretion falls within the reference range.

##### Educational Objective:

Investigate the underlying etiology of a patient transitioning from hypothyroidism to hyperthyroidism and appreciate that thyroid autoantibody levels can change over time.

## 91 ANSWER: E) Referral to a dietician

91 A Just as the oral glucose tolerance test has no role in the evaluation of hypoglycemia, having a patient who has undergone Roux-en-Y gastric bypass consume a meal with calories in liquid form will most likely precipitate a constellation of symptoms often called “dumping” and low glucose values may be observed. This is a well-known effect of such surgery, and patients should be urged to adhere to dietary instructions after bariatric procedures. Up to 10% of healthy patients exhibit post-oral glucose tolerance test glucose concentrations less than 50 mg/dL without symptoms of neuroglycemia, and the same considerations apply after a patient has had Roux-en-Y gastric bypass. In the postprandial setting, one should be wary of applying fasting criteria for endogenous hyperinsulinemic hypoglycemia given that insufficient time has elapsed to clear all secreted  $ \beta $-cell polypeptides from the peripheral circulation. Indeed, this is borne out by the normal results after a mixed meal that meets the criteria for reasonable nutrition after Roux-en-Y gastric bypass, a surgical procedure that results in unregulated emptying of liquid from the stomach. In such circumstances, ingestion of a caloric liquid often produces vasomotor symptoms.

For a patient such as the one in this vignette, enlisting the services of a dietician (Answer E) is the best management step. The dietician should be experienced in the care of patients who have undergone bariatric surgery that can lead to dumping. Typically these patients do not have neuroglycopenia. It is important to note that this patient had postprandial blood glucose values between 60 and 70 mg/dL (3.33 and 3.88 mmol/L), but these values may be normal in this setting and there was no accompanying neuroglycopenia. Limited weight loss after Rouxen-Y gastric bypass is often an indicator of poor dietary counseling and/or poor dietary adherence.

Calcium arterial stimulation testing (Answer A) should not be considered. Abnormalities because normative values have not been established for patients after upper gastrointestinal surgery. Calcium arterial stimulation testing should not be undertaken until after a firm diagnosis of a hypoglycemic disorder has been made and surgical therapy is being considered. The poor long-term response to distal pancreatectomy in such situations has cooled enthusiasm for this procedure in such patients. Likewise, endoscopic ultrasonography (Answer B) would only be indicated if evidence of hypoglycemia after a standard mixed meal is present and the Whipple triad is fulfilled. This requires documentation of symptoms that are compatible with neuroglycopenia, which occur in the presence of hypoglycemia and are reversed with amelioration of hypoglycemia.

A 72-hour fast (Answer C) is unlikely to be helpful given that these symptoms usually occur after meals. Diagnostic imaging such as CT (Answer D) and ultrasonography should be performed only after hyperinsulinemia hypoglycemia is confirmed, and they have no role until the diagnosis is established.

#### Educational Objective:

Educate hypoglycemia that occurs after Roux-en-Y gastric bypass with a standardized solid mixed-meal challenge.

## 02 ANSWER: B) FNAB of the 1.2-cm nodule

The incidence of differentiated thyroid cancer has been increasing in recent years, at least in part due to increased diagnosis. Thyroid cancer is predicted to become the third most common malignancy diagnosed in women and to exceed the incidence of colorectal cancer by the year 2019. Despite the steadily rising incidence of thyroid cancer, the number of deaths caused by thyroid cancer has remained relatively stable. Although this patient's imaging was performed because of a palpable thyroid nodule, her malignant nodule was actually incidentally found.

This patient's 2.2-cm cystic nodule does not require FNAB (thus, Answers A and C are incorrect). Purely cystic nodules without a mural component do not require FNAB, even if they are larger than 1.5 or 2.0 cm. Cystic composition and spongiform appearance is each an excellent predictor of a benign nodule. Cystic nodules are typically only biopsied if there are other components, such that the nodule is mixed cystic and solid, with the solid component being the target of the biopsy. The risk of malignancy in mixed cystic and solid nodules decreases as the cystic proportion of the nodule increases. Cystic nodules may require aspiration to alleviate compressive symptoms or pain if they increase in size. In addition, such nodules may be managed by surgical excision if the fluid reaccumulates after repeated aspirations.

The smaller 1.2-cm nodule shown in the vignette's image was incidentally found on ultrasonography initially performed to evaluate the palpable cystic nodule. This incidental nodule is suspicious in many ways. It has microcalcifications, irregular borders, and hypoechogenicity. Thus, this nodule should be biopsied now (Answer B) despite the fact that it was incidentally found and is not much larger than 1.0 cm. Given the suspicious features of this nodule, simply obtaining follow-up ultrasonography in 6 months (Answer D) is incorrect. In a recent meta-analysis, nodules with taller than wide shape had the highest odds ratio of being malignant. Various other studies show that microcalcifications, irregular margins, and taller than wide structure have good specificity for malignancy (approximately 89%-92%). Hypoechogenicity of nodules has a reasonable sensitivity for malignancy. Extrathyroidal extension and abnormal cervical lymph nodes are particularly worrisome features. In the case of a patient with multiple thyroid nodules, the nodules should be prioritized for biopsy on the basis of presence or absence of the features described above and the size. The risk of malignancy is thought to be similar regardless of whether the patient has a solitary nodule or a multinodular gland.

A thyroid scan and uptake (scintigraphy) (Answer E) will determine whether a nodule is functioning or nonfunctioning, but is not the best test to determine whether a thyroid nodule is malignant. Although many thyroid cancers have decreased isotope uptake compared with the surrounding thyroid tissue (i.e., appear "cold"), other lesions, such as the larger cystic nodule present in this patient, may also appear "cold" with such imaging.

Note: Image in the vignette is reprinted from Bahn RS, Castro MR. Approach to the patient with nontoxic multinodular goiter. J Clin Endocrinol Metab. 2011;96(5):1202-1212.

#### Educational Objective:

Interpret ultrasound characteristics of a thyroid nodule to determine whether fine-needle aspiration biopsy is warranted.

Brito JP, Gionfriddo MR, Al Nofal A, et al. The accuracy of thyroid nodule ultrasound to predict thyroid cancer: systematic review and meta-analysis. J Clin Endocrinol Metab. 2014;99(4):1253-1263.

Frates MC, Benson CB, Charboneau JW, et al; society of Radiologists in Ultrasound. Management of thyroid nodules detected at US: Society of Radiologists in Ultrasound consensus conference statement. Radiology. 2005;237(3):794-800.

Fish SA. Langer JE, Mandel SJ. Sonographic imaging of thyroid nodules and cervical lymph nodes. Endocrinol Metab Clin North Am. 2008;37(2):401-417.

## 93 ANSWER: B) A bisphosphonate Oster

Osteoporosis remains an under-recognized condition in men despite the fact that one-third of hip fractures that occur worldwide are in men. Furthermore, men also have a higher mortality rate than women in the first year after a hip fracture. Osteoporosis is generally a silent condition until the first fracture occurs. Height loss, however, is an important clinical manifestation of underlying osteoporosis (seen in this patient). This man has osteoporosis of both the spine and the hip. In addition to low bone density, he has other factors that add to his fracture risk: age older than 70 years, parental history of fracture, and current smoking.

Hypogonadism is a known risk factor for low bone mass. In young men with pathologic hypogonadism (hypothalamic/pituitary or testicular disease) such as Kallmann syndrome, Klinefelter syndrome, or pituitary

Testosterone in Older Men: Lumbar Bone Mineral Density

<div style="text-align: center;"><img src="imgs/img_in_chart_box_92_706_513_1125.jpg" alt="Image" width="36%" /></div>


<div style="text-align: center;"><div style="text-align: center;">Adapted from Snyder PJ, Peachey H, Hannoush P, et al 1999 Effect of testosterone treatment on bone mineral density in men over 65 years of age. J Clin Endocrinol Metab 84:1966-1972.</div> </div>


lesions, testosterone therapy improves bone mass. The anabolic role of androgens is also evident in hyperprolactinemic men whose testosterone levels normalize after treatment with dopamine agonists. To the contrary, the effect of testosterone therapy on bone mass in older men with age-related decline in serum testosterone (which is generally mild) remains unclear. In a study of 108 men who were 65 years and older with a mean baseline serum testosterone concentration of 367 ng/dL (12.7 nmol/L), no significant increase in either lumbar spine or hip bone density was seen after 6 months of testosterone replacement vs placebo. Interestingly, a post hoc analysis of this trial based on pretreatment serum testosterone levels showed that testosterone therapy increased lumbar spine bone density by 5.9% in only those men whose pretreatment testosterone was less than 200 ng/dL (6.9 nmol/L) (Figure 1). This suggests that the only men whose bone mineral density responds to testosterone therapy are those whose baseline testosterone level is significantly low. This patient's testosterone level (279 ng/dL [9.68 nmol/L]) is only slightly below normal.

<div style="text-align: center;"><div style="text-align: center;">Figure 1.</div> </div>


A meta-analysis of randomized controlled trials that mainly enrolled older men with age-related low testosterone levels demonstrated a modest benefit in spine bone mineral density in only those men who received intramuscular testosterone, while no benefit was seen with transdermal therapy (presumably because of higher testosterone [and estradiol] levels achieved via the intramuscular route) (Figure 2A). No benefit was seen in hip bone mineral density (Figure 2B). Even more importantly, there are no data showing that testosterone therapy reduces fracture rates. Therefore, the Endocrine Society Clinical Practice Guidelines for Male Osteoporosis recommend that in men who are at high risk for fracture, an agent with proven antifracture effectiveness be initiated, such as a bisphosphonate (Answer B) or teriparatide. In fact, the addition of such an agent is recommended even in those patients at high risk who might already be on testosterone therapy. The use of testosterone therapy in lieu of a “bone drug” is only recommended in men who are at borderline-high risk of

fracture and have testosterone levels below 200 ng/dL (6.9 nmol/L) along with symptoms of hypogonadism or in men with contraindications to approved pharmacologic agents for osteoporosis. Alendronate, zoledronic acid, and risedronate have been shown to possess antifracture efficacy, while fracture data on ibandronate are not available.

This patient's testosterone level is only slightly low and he has no symptoms of hypogonadism. Hence, no form of testosterone therapy (Answers C, D, and E) is indicated in this patient. ## 94 ANSWER: B) Intravenous zoledronic acid

Vertebral compression fractures are a risk factor for subsequent vertebral fractures, as well as other osteoporotic fractures. They are also associated with significant morbidity, including chronic back pain, restricted lung function, reduced mobility, and decreased quality of life. Unfortunately, most patients with fragility fractures are not treated for osteoporosis, despite evidence that treatment is beneficial.

Patients who present with vertebral fractures should be worked up for secondary causes of osteoporosis. This patient had a full secondary osteoporosis evaluation, which was unrevealing. Of interest, there is a potential connection between proton-pump inhibitors and bone loss, such as is seen in this patient. Although the exact mechanism remains unproven, the increase in gastric pH reduces the absorption of calcium and thereby results in one loss when these medications are used long term.

This vignette presents a common scenario in which an asymptomatic vertebral compression fracture is noted on imaging obtained for other reasons. It is the clinician's role to recognize these events as skeletal failure that requires prompt evaluation and treatment. This patient's atraumatic spinal compression fracture confirms the diagnosis of osteoporosis, despite the fact that his bone mineral density does not meet the T-score definition of osteoporosis (-2.5 or less). The National Osteoporosis Foundation recommends the following: (1) initiating pharmacologic treatment in patients with hip or vertebral fractures (clinical or asymptomatic) regardless of T-score; (2) initiating therapy in patients with T scores of -2.5 or less at the femoral neck, total hip, or lumbar spine by OXA after appropriate evaluation; and (3) initiating therapy in postmenopausal women and men 50 years or older who have low bone mineral density (T score between -1.0 and -2.5 [osteopenia]) at the femoral neck, total hip, or lumbar spine by DXA and 10-year probabilities of hip fracture or major osteoporotic fracture of ≥3% and ≥20%, respectively (based on the US-adapted WHO absolute fracture risk model [FRAX; www.NOF.org and www.shefac.uk/FRAX]). Although the FRAX score in this patient is under the threshold for treatment, as it only accounts for femoral neck T score, on the basis of his recent vertebral fracture, treatment is warranted.

Oral bisphosphonates (such as alendronate and risedronate) and zoledronic acid are good options for first-line therapy because they reduce the risk of hip and spine fracture. Although oral alendronate (Answer A) would be a treatment option in this patient, given his recent duodenal ulcer and esophageal reflux disease, it may not be the best choice. Gastroesophageal disease is not an absolute contraindication to use of oral bisphosphonates, but caution should be used in patient with active upper gastrointestinal problems such as Barrett esophagus, dysphagia, other esophageal diseases, gastritis, duodenitis, or ulcers. Intravenous zoledronic acid (Answer B) confers no gastrointestinal risk for this patient and therefore would be the preferred choice.

Calcitonin (Answer D) reduces risk of spine fracture and may offer analgesic effects in some patients with back pain related to acute fracture; however, it is less potent than other available options and this patient is asymptomatic. Although results from randomized, placebo-controlled trials have suggested little or no benefit in patients with acute vertebral compression fracture, kyphoplasty (Answer C) (the installation of bone cement after inflation of the vertebral body) has been used in patients with severe, incapacitating pain related to the fracture expansion of the vertebral body. These procedures also have been shown to provide clinical pain relief in when pain management has failed. However, kyphoplasty is not the best choice because this patient is patients with fractures due to metastatic disease. However, kyphoplasty is not the best choice because this patient is asymptomatic and the age of the compression fracture appears to be on the order of months.

Adding calcium (Answer E) would not be appropriate as the sole intervention, as calcium alone does not reduce hip fracture risk. In addition, he is already consuming at least 600 mg of calcium daily in his diet, as well as 500 mg daily in his multivitamin.

#### Educational Objective:

Diagnose osteoporosis on the basis of a fragility fracture, even without a T score of -2.5 or less, and recommend appropriate osteoporosis treatment.
