#### ANSWER: E) Proceed with the angiogram; no additional treatment required

95 A substantial increase in the use of radiocontrast-enhanced imaging studies has occurred over the past 2 decades (particularly CT and coronary angiography). Thyroid dysfunction may result as a consequence of sudden exposure to high iodide levels, and both hyperthyroidism and hypothyroidism may occur as a consequence of using iodinated contrast medium (ICM). Radiocontrast agents vary with respect to their iodine content. The usual iodine load in coronary angiography is 100 mL of ICM, conveying 13,500 mcg of free iodide and 30 to 35 g of bound iodine. If coronary angiography is associated with percutaneous coronary intervention, up to 250 mL may be used, resulting in an iodine load of 75 to 87.5 g. The recommended daily intake of iodine for nonpregnant adults is only 150 mcg. However, supraphysiologic doses are well tolerated and the approximate levels above which significant adverse effects are likely to occur in the healthy population are actually much higher. The US Institute of Medicine reports the tolerable upper limit of iodine as 1100 mcg daily.

In individuals with preexisting autoimmunity, supraphysiologic levels of iodine may lead to iodine-induced hyperthyroidism (Jod-Basedow phenomenon). Iodine overload is accompanied by the Wolff-Chaikoff effect, which inhibits iodine organization and thereby reduces formation and release of thyroid hormones. After 8 to 10 days, there usually is an escape phenomenon secondary to low inorganic iodine concentration as a consequence of downregulation of the sodium-iodide symporter on the basolateral membrane of follicular thyroid cells. The presence of autonomous thyroid function permits the synthesis and release of excess quantities of thyroid hormones, resulting in iodine-induced thyrotoxicosis. Risk factors for this condition include Graves disease, nontoxic diffuse or multinodular goiter with thyroid autonomy, age older than 65 years, and residing in an iodine-deficient area. The link between exposure to ICM and new thyroid dysfunction is more common than was previously thought. Iodine-induced thyrotoxicosis cannot easily be differentiated from other forms of thyrotoxicosis.

Failure to escape from the Wolff-Chaikoff effect results in iodine-induced hypothyroidism. This may occur in apparently healthy individuals, especially in neonates and infants; in patients older than 65 years; or in patients

with chronic nonthyroidal illness such as cystic fibrosis, chronic dialysis, thalassemia major, or anorexia nervosa. It may also occur in patients with underlying thyroid disease including euthyroid Graves disease previously treated with surgery, in patients treated with antithyroid drugs or radioiodine, in patients with Hashimoto thyroiditis, and in euthyroid patients with a history of thyroiditis. Furthermore, patients treated with other goitrogens including lithium, phenytoin, carbamazepine, and rifampicin are at risk of ICM-induced hypothyroidism.

The patient described in this vignette has a number of risk factors for ICM-induced thyrotoxicosis including a family history of thyroid dysfunction and the presence of a multinodular goiter. Prophylactic measures have been proposed for patients at high risk, especially if the serum TSH is undetectable. The patient has a low but detectable serum TSH and taking the information provided together, he is not at high risk of ICM-induced hyperthyroidism (thus. Answer E is correct).

(thus, Answer E is correct).

Various regimens have been proposed to prevent hyperthyroidism after administration of ICM. The use of combined therapy with antithyroid drugs and perchlorate has been proposed as effective preventative therapy, although since perchlorate is not widely available, the use of antithyroid drug therapy starting the day before the examination and for 7 to 14 days afterwards (Answers C and D) is a viable alternative. There is insufficient evidence that the patient described is at high risk of ICM-induced thyrotoxicosis; because the proposed treatments have a risk of potentially serious adverse effects, neither of these options is correct.

β-Receptor antagonists (Answer A) are widely used in the management of hyperthyroidism; however, they do not have proven effectiveness in preventing the development of thyrotoxicosis in at-risk patients. The use of oral corticosteroids (Answer B) would also not be indicated in the prevention of thyroid dysfunction in this patient.

##### Educational Objective:

Provide advice regarding the use of iodinated contrast medium and prevention of thyroid dysfunction in this setting.

Nolte, Muller R, Siggelkow H, Emrich D, Hufner M. Prophylactic application of thyrostatic drugs during excessive iodine exposure in euthyroid patients with thyroid autonomy: a randomized study. Eur J Endocrinol. 1996;134(3):337-341.

##### ANSWER: A) Inaccurate glucose meter data due to dialysate type

This patient's diabetes is complicated by end-stage renal disease requiring the relatively recent initiation of renal replacement therapy. Subsequent to the initiation of peritoneal dialysis he has had persistent elevation in his self-monitored blood glucose values, resulting in recent increases in his prescribed insulin doses. Although the patient was not reported to have had hypoglycemia at home, he has experienced an episode of severe hypoglycemia on this occasion requiring the intervention of emergency medical personnel.

In this situation, it is critical to note that the patient is using an icodextrin-containing peritoneal dialysate solution. Icodextrin is commonly used as an osmotic agent in such solutions, and it is primarily metabolized to the sugar maltose. Many glucose meters and test strips do not measure glucose via glucose-specific methodologies. These devices cannot distinguish glucose from xylose, maltose, or galactose, and they may provide falsely elevated readings in the presence of such sugars. Indeed, numerous reports document individuals using icodextrin-containing dialysate being prescribed excessive insulin doses in response to erroneous glucose meter data. In some cases, this has resulted in severe hypoglycemia, coma, and even death. Thus, inaccurate glucose meter data due to the patient's dialysate type (Answer A) is most likely the primary contributor to his hypoglycemia. Patients prescribed this type of dialysate should always use glucose-specific monitors and test strips. A list of devices (glucose-specific vs non-glucose-specific) is available at www.glucosesafety.com. Also important to note is that in patients with clinically significant renal dysfunction, icodextrin and maltose metabolites may not be cleared for 2 or more weeks after icodextrin is discontinued.

Compared with individuals with normal renal function, patients with chronic kidney disease are at increased risk for hypoglycemia. In patients with diabetes and end-stage renal disease, multiple factors contribute to this increased risk. These factors may include decreased renal gluconeogenesis due to decreased renal mass (Answer B), decreased renal clearance of insulin and other antihyperglycemic medications (Answer C), decreased degradation of insulin in peripheral tissues, and reduced glycogen stores due to anorexia. Although all of these factors may contribute to this increase due to bacterial peritonitis (Answer D) is always a possibility in a patient receiving peritoneal disease.

Sepsis due to bacterial peritonitis (Answer D) is always a possibility in a patient receiving peritoneal dialysis. This patient was hypotensive on presentation, but he has responded well to intravenous fluid resuscitation. He is also afebrile and has no signs suggestive of peritonitis on physical examination; thus, sepsis due to peritonitis does not appear a likely explanation of his current hypoglycemia. Although previously undetected adrenal insufficiency (Answer E) should be considered in a patient with unexplained hypotension and hypoglycemia, it is not the most likely explanation for this patient's severe hypoglycemic episode.

#### Educational Objective:

Anticipate the potential effect of icodextrin-containing dialysate on glucose meter results.

UpToDate Topic Review(s):

Insulin therapy in type 2 diabetes mellitus

Sloand JA. Dialysis patient safety: safeguards to prevent iatrogenic hypoglycemia in patients receiving icodextrin. Am J Kidney Dis. 2012;60(4):514-516. Moen MF, Zhan M, Hsu VD, et al. Frequency of hypoglycemia and its significance in chronic kidney disease. Clin J Am Soc Nephrol. 2009;4(6):1121-1127.

## 97 ANSWER: E) No antithyroid treatment is indicated The i

The interpretation of thyroid function test results in pregnancy can be challenging because serum TSH concentrations fall in early pregnancy as a physiologic response to the stimulating effects of hCG on the TSH receptor with peak hCG concentrations between 7 and 11 weeks' gestation. Most studies also report a substantial decrease in serum free  $ T_{4} $ concentrations with the progression of gestation. Serum free  $ T_{4} $ measurements in pregnant women are complicated by increased thyroxine-binding globulin and decreased albumin concentrations, which result in marked interassay and interindividual variability when immune-mediated assays are used. Hence total  $ T_{4} $ measurement by extraction/liquid chromatography/tandem mass spectrometry has been recommended, but this is not widely available. Many centers have now adopted the use of gestation-specific reference ranges that are assay and population dependent. In the absence of assay-specific ranges, the Endocrine Society and the American Thyroid Association have recommended TSH reference ranges of 0.1 to 2.5 mIU/L, 0.2 to 3.0 mIU/L, and 0.3 to 3.0 mIU/L in the first, second, and third trimesters, respectively.

During pregnancy, any subnormal TSH value should be evaluated in conjunction with serum free  $ T_{4} $. The diagnosis of clinical hyperthyroidism is confirmed in the presence of suppressed or undetectable serum TSH and elevated serum free  $ T_{4} $. Untreated hyperthyroidism can have severe consequences for the pregnant woman, pregnancy outcome, and the fetus. In the presence of an undetectable or very low serum TSH and elevated serum free  $ T_{4} $, the differential diagnosis in most cases is between Graves hyperthyroidism and gestational transient thyrotoxicosis. The central element in Graves disease is autoimmunity to the TSH receptor, and the hyperthyroidism is caused by TSH receptor-stimulating antibodies. In gestational transient thyrotoxicosis, the thyrotoxicosis is due to high concentrations of hCG stimulating the TSH receptor because of structural homology between hCG and TSH molecules. In Graves disease, thyroid production of  $ T_{3} $ is high and serum total  $ T_{3} $ or serum free  $ T_{3} $ concentrations are typically more elevated than serum total  $ T_{4} $ or free  $ T_{4} $ concentrations. Gestational transient thyrotoxicosis is usually associated with  $ T_{4} $ thyrotoxicosis that can be diagnosed by documenting serum total  $ T_{4} $ or free  $ T_{4} $ levels above the upper normal limit, but with only slightly raised or normal serum total  $ T_{3} $ or free  $ T_{3} $ concentrations.

A careful history and clinical examination are of utmost importance in establishing the etiology of thyrotoxicosis. In both situations, common clinical manifestations include palpitation, anxiety, hand tremor, and

heat intolerance. Factors suggestive of gestational thyrotoxicosis include the absence of a personal history of thyroid disease and of a family history of autoimmune thyroid or other disorders, diagnosis in the first trimester of pregnancy, the absence of symptoms or signs before pregnancy, an association with hyperemesis, a relationship with multiple gestation, the absence of clinical signs of Graves disease (including goiter and thyroid-associated ophthalmopathy), and mild or absent clinical signs of thyrotoxicosis.

The management of gestational hyperthyroidism depends on symptom severity. In women with hyperemesis gravidarum, control of vomiting and treatment of dehydration with intravenous fluids compose the typical treatment. The patient in this vignette has hypokalemia and correction of electrolyte abnormalities is important. The course of gestational thyrotoxicosis is usually self-limiting and serum T₄ typically returns to normal by 14 to 18 weeks' gestation (thus, Answer E is correct). The obstetric outcome is not improved in isolated cases in which gestational thyrotoxicosis is treated with antithyroid drugs. There are no studies reported comparing antithyroid drugs with supportive treatment, but current guidelines do not recommend administration of antithyroid drugs in the management of gestational hyperthyroidism (thus, Answers A and B are incorrect). In situations in which it is difficult to arrive at a definitive diagnosis, a short course of antithyroid drugs is reasonable. If the hyperthyroidism returns after discontinuation of antithyroid drugs, Graves hyperthyroidism is the most likely diagnosis and may require further therapy.

Blocking of β-adrenergic receptors is an important adjunctive treatment in the management of thyrotoxicosis.

-adrenergic blockers (Answer C) are often used in pregnancy for various indications, and a meta-analysis

-aminizing use of the agents in the first trimester did not identify any increased odds of all or major congenital anomalies, although organ-specific malformations were observed with increased odds of cardiovascular defects,

-left lip/palate, and neural tube defects. The use of β-blockers in a patient with asthma is relatively contraindicated

-though selective β-1 adrenergic receptor blockers could be used. However, because of the self-limiting nature of gestational thyrotoxicosis, initiation of β-blockers is not required.

Administration of inorganic iodine (SSKI/Lugol's solution) (Answer D) leads to rapid decreases in both  $ T_{4} $ and concentrations, and it may be used in the management of thyroid storm or preoperatively in patients undergoing roidectomy for Graves disease. The effect is short-lived and its use is not indicated in this patient.

ablish the differential diagnosis of thyrotoxicosis in pregnancy and outline the appropriate management of

stational transient thyrotoxicosis.

##### ANSWER: B) Add verapamil

Diabetic nephropathy is the leading cause of end-stage renal disease in the United States, accounting up 45% of patients who require dialysis. The disease is characterized by microalbuminuria, which, if left out, can progress to macroalbuminuria, rising creatinine levels, and renal failure. Microalbuminuria is a predictor of renal failure and cardiovascular disease.

tors that should prompt an investigation of nondiabetic renal disease in patients with type 1 diabetes mellitus onset of proteinuria within 5 years of the diabetes diagnosis; the presence of hematuria or cellular casts;

a rapid decline in renal function; a decline in glomerular filtration rate of more than 30% within 3 months after initiation of ACE inhibitor or angiotensin-receptor blocker therapy; or the absence of retinopathy or neuropathy, microvascular complications that typically accompany nephropathy in individuals with type 1 diabetes mellitus.

interventions that slow the progression of diabetic nephropathy at the stage of microalbuminuria include glycemic control and antihypertensive therapy, particularly with ACE inhibitors and angiotensin-receptor blockers, although other antihypertensive agents can also be effective. The protective effect of ACE inhibitors against the development of nephropathy (defined as albuminuria >200 μg/min) in patients with type 1 diabetes mellitus and microalbuminuria is also seen in normotensive patients, supporting the formation of ACE inhibitors in this patient population. There is a progressive improvement of blood pressure control and proteinuria with increasing lisinopril dosages up to 40 mg daily. Dosages higher than 40 mg daily (Answer C) do not provide additional benefit. Studies of patients with diabetic nephropathy using combined ACE inhibitors and angiotensin-receptor blockers (Answer E) have shown no slowing in the decline in renal function or cardiovascular events, but an increased risk of complications including acute renal failure requiring dialysis, hyperkalemia, and hypotension. No study has demonstrated that angiotensin-receptor blockers are more effective than ACE inhibitors when treating diabetic nephropathy (thus, Answer D is incorrect).

Among antihypertensive agents not affecting the renin-angiotensin-aldosterone system, verapamil (Answer B) and diltiazem have demonstrated the greatest protein lowering in patients with diabetic nephropathy. These effects are additive to those of ACE inhibitors. The protective effect of these agents appears to be due to selective lowering of intraglomerular pressure. Long-term studies using calcium channel blockers to evaluate preservation of renal function have not been performed in patients with diabetic nephropathy. Studies of dihydropyridine calcium channel blockers and β-adrenergic blockers have shown variable effects on proteinuria in this patient.

channel Uncontrolled diabetes accelerates the development and progression of diabetic nephropathy. Improvement in glycemic control can reduce proteinuria. This is best evidenced in patients with established nephropathy who have undergone pancreas transplant leading to euglycemia in whom proteinuria is typically stabilized or improved. Although improved glycemic control is advised in this patient with uncontrolled diabetes, changing to insulin pump therapy alone (Answer A) would not guarantee improved glycemic control and would not have the same renal protective effect as better blood pressure control.

##### Educational Objective:

Recommend interventions to slow the progression of diabetic nephropathy.

ANSWER: D) Methylmalonic acid measurement

Roux-en-Y gastric bypass remains the most common operation performed to manage medically complicated obesity. The risks for nutritional deficiencies after this operation have been long recognized and close surveillance is recommended. The most common nutritional abnormality reported is anemia as a result of iron deficiency, followed less often by deficiencies in vitamin B₁₂ and folate, although the prevalence of those deficiencies has decreased with the implementation of empiric vitamin supplementation. The challenge remains in the variability of vitamin supplementation advised by bariatric surgery programs. Despite a patient's adherence to vitamin supplementation regimens, vitamin and mineral deficiencies can still develop.

This patient reports early, mild symptoms of neuropathy. Simply reassuring the patient (Answer E) would be inappropriate. Although it is reasonable to consider recurrent hyperglycemia as a potential contributor to her symptoms, her history of Roux-en-Y gastric bypass should increase the concern for a nutritional etiology (thus, Answer B is incorrect). Thiamine deficiency has been described after bariatric surgery and should be suspected in patients with active upper gastrointestinal symptoms such as uncontrollable vomiting. Because these symptoms are not described in this clinical vignette, thiamine deficiency is unlikely (thus, Answer A is incorrect). Although her vitamin B₁₂ value is normal, values between 200 and 300 pg/mL (200-300 ng/L) are considered borderline and may still be associated with vitamin B₁₂ deficiency. In this situation, checking her methylmalonic acid level (Answer D) can clarify the situation. Methylmalonic acid and homocysteine are metabolic intermediaries of vitamin B₁₂ and are elevated in the setting of vitamin B₁₂ deficiency. Methylmalonic acid elevation is more specific for vitamin B₁₂ deficiency because folate is not involved in methylmalonic acid metabolism.

Copper deficiency is rare and has been reported in patients requiring longstanding nutritional support (parental or enteral) or zinc supplementation and in patients with myelodysplastic syndromes or those who have undergone Roux-en-Y gastric bypass. The usual presentation consists of hematologic abnormalities (anemia, leukopenia) and myeloneuropathy. Although an important consideration, more common nutritional deficiencies must be contemplated (thus, Answer C is incorrect).

##### Educational Objective:

Evaluate potential nutritional deficiencies after Roux-en-Y gastric bypass.

UpToDate Topic Review(s):

Late complications of bariatric surgical operations

## 100 ANSWER: B) Measurement of 25-hydroxyvitamin D

Obesity is associated with vitamin D deficiency due to reduced bioavailability from sequestration in fat. Patients who undergo bariatric surgery are at increased risk for malabsorption of vitamin D, leading to further nutritional deficits because of the loss of sufficient gastrointestinal absorptive surface. Deficiency in 25-hydroxyvitamin D results in decreased calcium and phosphorous absorption from the gut and increased PTH, which leads to increased bone resorption to maintain serum calcium in the normal range. Severe and prolonged vitamin D deficiency results in osteomalacia or a mineralization defect in bone where osteoid remains undermineralized, soft, and vulnerable to fracture. Therefore, measurement of 25-hydroxyvitamin D (Answer B) is the best diagnostic step now.

Osteomalacia is characterized by deficient bone mineralization, and in combination with hyperparathyroidism, it can lead to an increase in bone loss and fractures. Affected patients often present with bone pain and proximal muscle weakness, which is aggravated by physical activity. Levels of 1,25-dihydroxyvitamin D are often normal, and its measurement (Answer A) is therefore not helpful in making the diagnosis. Patients with osteomalacia may present with reduced bone density on radiographic studies, with thinning of the cortex, and possible cortical stress fractures, often called Looser zones or pseudofractures. Bone density scans (Answer C) may show generalized osteopenia; however, this finding is nonspecific and does not distinguish between osteomalacia and osteoporosis. An iliac crest biopsy (Answer E) is not needed either, as it would reveal osteomalacia as well, without identifying a cause that could be elucidated more rapidly, simply, and less expensively with standard laboratory studies. Obtaining a bone scan (Answer D) to further evaluate her bone pain might be indicated in certain settings, such as in metastatic cancer or Paget disease. In the setting of high bone turnover, as would be the case in this vignette, bone scan will reveal a diffusely high uptake throughout the skeleton, including the tibia. Thus, it would not shed light on the correct diagnosis.

#### Educational Objective:

Evaluate for osteomalacia in a patient with risk factors for vitamin D deficiency.

UpToDate Topic Review(s): Clinical manifestations, diagnosis, and treatment of osteomalacia

Service GJ, Thompson GB, Service FJ, Andrews JC, Collazo-Clavell ML, Lloyd RV. Hyperinsulinemic hypoglycemia with nesidioblastosis after gastric-bypass surgery. N Engl J Med. 2005;353(3):249-254.

## 101 ANSWER: D) Switch to micronized progesterone, 200 mg Progesterone is necessary to protect the

101 Progesterone is necessary to protect the uterus from endometrial hyperplasia and cancer when estrogen is taken for menopausal symptoms in a woman with an intact uterus. Regimens using medroxyprogesterone acetate and micronized progesterone have been demonstrated to prevent endometrial hyperplasia in large studies. Micronized progesterone may be better tolerated and have a less adverse effect on lipids than medroxyprogesterone. Therefore, switching to micronized progesterone, 200 mg for 12 days of the month (Answer D), or 100 mg daily, would be the most appropriate next step.

The medroxyprogesterone acetate regimens that have been demonstrated to prevent endometrial hyperplasia include medroxyprogesterone acetate, 5 or 10 mg for 12 days of the month, or medroxyprogesterone acetate, 2.5 mg daily. There are not enough data to suggest that medroxyprogesterone acetate, 2.5 mg for 12 days of the month (Answer C), adequately prevents endometrial hyperplasia.

If a patient cannot take any type of progesterone by mouth, a levonorgestrel-coated intrauterine device could be considered. In meta-analyses, it has been demonstrated that the levonorgestrel-coated intrauterine device causes the endometrial lining to become atrophic and prevents endometrial hyperplasia. Intravaginal progesterone (Answer E) has also been shown to decrease the incidence of endometrial hyperplasia, but only in small studies. Therefore, it would not be the best treatment choice. In women who use intravaginal progesterone and in women who cannot take progesterone in any form, baseline and yearly endometrial biopsies are required to detect endometrial hyperplasia, with additional endometrial biopsies if there is new-onset vaginal bleeding.

Short-term (<5 years) hormone therapy is safe in a woman with no contraindications, such as personal or family history of breast cancer, thrombotic events, coronary artery disease, or endometrial cancer. This patient has no contraindications to hormone therapy and has had remarkable relief of her symptoms. Therefore, stopping estradiol (Answer A) would not be appropriate. Further, although venlafaxine (Answer B) has been demonstrated to alleviate hot flashes in menopause, it is not as effective as estradiol, and switching to venlafaxine would not be the most appropriate next treatment step.

##### Educational Objective:

Select appropriate hormone therapy to prevent endometrial hyperplasia in a postmenopausal woman.

## 102 ANSWER: C) A statin Hypertriglyceridemia is

102 Hypertriglyceridemia is associated with increased cardiovascular risk, although causality remains unclear. Triglyceride levels above 1000 mg/dL (>11.3 mmol/L) increase the risk of pancreatitis and should be aggressively treated with dietary and lifestyle interventions and usually concomitant pharmacologic therapy with fibrates, niacin, or fish oil.

Patients with diabetes mellitus who have hypertriglyceridemia should be screened for potentially treatable secondary causes such as hypothyroidism; nephrotic syndrome; obesity; alcohol use; and medication use including estrogens, glucocorticoids, cyclosporine, cyclophosphamide, noncardioselective  $ \beta $-adrenergic blockers, tamoxifen, phenothiazines, isotretinoin, and antiretroviral agents.

In patients with modest hypertriglyceridemia, the treatment focus should be nonpharmacologic and include weight loss in obese patients; regular aerobic exercise; and restriction of carbohydrates, especially food high in sugars. Among patients with diabetes, improved glycemic control effectively lowers triglyceride levels. In general, antihyperglycemic agents lower triglyceride levels. Insulin promotes the synthesis of lipoprotein lipase, which hydrolyzes triglycerides to free fatty acids. Intravenous insulin can effectively lower markedly elevated triglycerides. However, lowering triglycerides with prandial insulin has not been shown to reduce cardiovascular risk (thus, Answer E is incorrect).

Several cholesterol-lowering agents effectively lower triglycerides, including brates (18%-45%), niacin (17%-26%), and statins (5%-30%). High-dosage atorvastatin and rosuvastatin have the greatest triglyceride-lowering effect in the statin class. Niacin also raises HDL-cholesterol levels and has a modest LDL-lowering effect, but it has never been shown in monotherapy trials to reduce cardiovascular events rates or mortality among patients with diabetes (thus, Answer D is incorrect). Pharmacologic intervention of isolated hypertriglyceridemia with agents such as fibrates has not been shown to reduce cardiovascular event rates (thus, Answer B is incorrect). The Veterans Affairs High-Density Lipoprotein Intervention Trial (VA-HIT) showed a significant reduction in cardiovascular events among patients treated with gemfibrozil whose primary lipid abnormality was a low HDL-cholesterol level. Treated patients had a 6% increase in HDL cholesterol and a 31% lowering of triglycerides. In the Fenofibrate Intervention and Event Lowering in Diabetes (FIELD) trial, patients older than 50 years who had diabetes mellitus and were not taking lipid-lowering agents were treated with fenofibrate or placebo. After 5 years, treated patients had nonsignificantly lower rates of coronary events, and a nonsignificantly higher rate of coronary death and all-cause mortality. Combination therapy with a statin and fibrate has not been shown to improve cardiovascular risk reduction when compared with treatment with a statin alone (thus, Answer A is incorrect).

In addition to hypertriglyceridemia, this patient's cardiovascular risk factors include hypertension and albuminuria. The American Heart Association and the American College of Cardiology cardiovascular risk calculators scores this patient's 10-year cardiovascular risk at 17.6%, well above the 7.5% risk cutoff recommended for initiation of statin therapy, even in individuals whose baseline LDL-cholesterol concentration is well below 100 mg/dL (2.59 mmol/L). Given the high cardiovascular risk in patients with diabetes, the 2013 American Heart Association and the American College of Cardiology guidelines recommend that a moderate- or high-intensity statin be started in patients with diabetes who are between 40 and 75 years old and have an LDL-cholesterol concentration of 70 to 189 mg/dL (1.81-4.90 mmol/L) without clinical atherosclerotic cardiovascular disease (thus, Answer C is correct).

##### Educational Objective:

Recommend initial therapy to reduce cardiovascular risk in a patient with diabetes mellitus and hypertriglyceridemia.

## 103 ANSWER: A) Refer to a urologist Althe

103 Although this patient's serum prostate-specific antigen (PSA) concentration remains within the reference range, based on the degree of change in his PSA level, he should be referred for urologic evaluation (Answer A). PSA levels have considerable test-retest variability and they also fluctuate in an individual. However, in a man on testosterone therapy, an increase in PSA concentration greater than 1.4 ng/mL (1.4 μg/L) (confirmed by a repeated test) over the course of a year is not attributable to random variation alone. The cutoff of 1.4 ng/mL (1.4 μg/L) has been adopted on the basis of the findings of a 12-month seminal study that evaluated the effectiveness of finasteride vs placebo on lower urinary tract symptoms and prostate volume in men with benign prostatic hyperplasia. In that study, the 90% confidence limit for the change in PSA levels in the placebo arm was 1.4 ng/mL (1.4 μg/L). Furthermore, a systematic review of prostate risk during testosterone therapy found that the average increase in PSA after initiation of testosterone therapy is 0.3 ng/mL (0.3 μg/L) and 0.44 ng/mL (0.44 μg/L) in younger men (<60 years) and older men (>60 years), respectively (Figure). Hence, on the basis of the findings of the finasteride study and the fact that the average increase in PSA levels on testosterone therapy is less than 0.5 ng/mL (0.5 μg/L) (regardless of patient age), the Endocrine Society's clinical practice guidelines recommend that patients with a PSA increase greater than 1.4 ng/mL (1.4 μg/L) on testosterone therapy should be referred for urologic consultation. Thus, simply providing reassurance and scheduling a follow-up appointment in 3 months (Answer C) is incorrect. It is important to appreciate that this increase of 1.4 ng/mL (1.4 μg/L) in PSA concentration does not indicate the presence of prostate cancer, but only serves as a trigger for further evaluation. Because this patient's PSA had increased by 1.7 ng/mL (1.7 μg/L), he was referred for urologic evaluation. His prostate ultrasonography was negative for any focal lesion and results from random prostate biopsies were normal. Testosterone therapy was continued.

Data derived from Bhasin S, Singh AB, Mac RP, Carter B, Lee MI, Cunningham. Managing the risks of prostate disease during testosterone therapy in older men: recommendations for a standardized monitoring plan. J Androl. 2003;24:299-311.

This patient's on-treatment testosterone concentration is within the therapeutic range and, more importantly, he has shown clinical improvement on this dosage. Therefore, a decrease in dosage (Answer D) is not indicated. Although he has benign prostatic hyperplasia on digital rectal examination, he does not have any lower urinary tract symptoms. Treatment with a 5α-reductase inhibitor such as dutasteride (Answer E) is not indicated.

Subclinical prostatitis (asymptomatic patients, nontender prostate on digital rectal examination) is believed to be responsible for PSA elevation in some patients. Although the diagnosis of subclinical prostatitis is made on the basis of abnormal urinalysis (>10 white blood cells per high-powered field), it is mostly a presumptive diagnosis. Empiric treatment with antibiotics (mainly fluoroquinolones) (Answer B) has been reported to decrease PSA levels by approximately 30%. However, no placebo-controlled trials have shown whether this decrease is a result of antibiotic therapy or inherent variation in PSA levels. Furthermore, this decrease in PSA on antibiotics does not distinguish prostatitis from prostate cancer.

104 ANSWER: C) CT of the chest and abdomen

The diagnosis and differential diagnosis of Cushing syndrome is a challenging problem in clinical medicine. The clinical presentations are protean and the typical classic phenotype overlaps with the ubiquitous metabolic syndrome. The clinical presentation of Cushing syndrome in young postpubertal patients is often heralded by acne and the development of stria. Surprisingly, children and teenagers with Cushing syndrome are very good students despite the same degree of cortisol-induced cerebral atrophy that is seen in adults. The development of facial rounding, progressive hypertension with edema, and proximal myopathy were enough to inaugurate a biochemical evaluation.

The biochemical confirmation of ACTH-dependent hypercortisolism was easy in this patient. He had marked elevations of basal cortisol secretion accompanying his clinically obvious Cushing syndrome. The elevation in plasma ACTH confirmed an ACTH-secreting neoplasm, resulting in the pituitary imaging study. Pituitary MRI showed a hypoenhancing lesion (really seen on only 1 slice image) in the left pituitary. Pituitary imaging abnormalities such as this are common in the normal population and must be interpreted with great caution in patients with ACTH-dependent hypercortisolism. Moreover, the dramatic increases in plasma ACTH and the prodigious hypercortisolism should alert clinicians to the very likely possibility of a nonpituitary (ectopic) source of ACTH hypersecretion. Although patients with atypical corticotroph macroadenomas may generate ACTH levels of the magnitude seen here, patients with ACTH-secreting pituitary microadenomas almost never have an ACTH concentration greater than 200 pg/mL (44 pmol/L). Therefore, a young man with an ACTH level greater than 500 pg/mL (>110 pmol/L) and hypokalemia after a failed (and unnecessary) pituitary surgery must have a nonpituitary ACTH-secreting tumor accounting for his severe hypercortisolism. Although inferior petrosal sinus sampling for ACTH (Answer A) is the often the only way to confirm the presence or absence of an ACTH-secreting pituitary tumor, noninvasive body imaging should definitely be considered before this procedure in this young man. The finding of Crooke hyaline changes reflects histologic changes in pituitary corticotroph cells resulting from any condition associated with a state of hypercortisolism (including iatrogenic Cushing syndrome). It is not specific for pituitary ACTH-dependent hypercortisolism.

The most likely location of an ectopic ACTH-secreting tumor in a young man is in the thorax. Most of these ectopic ACTH-secreting tumors are neuroendocrine tumors in the chest (and sometimes in the abdomen), so most authorities believe that a CT of the chest and abdomen (Answer C) is the most sensitive initial screening test. Small bronchial carcinoids can be confused with pulmonary vessels on CT or MRI imaging. Small thymic masses are seen on MRI in about one-third of patients with Cushing disease and these do not always represent neuroendocrine tumors. This finding usually represents residual normal thymic tissue, particularly in patients younger than 40 years. In fact, thymic hyperplasia may actually occur in young patients after correction of their hypercortisolism. This thymic enlargement can sometimes be mistaken for an occult ectopic source of ACTH hypersecretion after medical or surgical correction of the Cushing syndrome.

Other imaging modalities, such as 18-fluorodeoxyglucose positron emission tomography (PET), have not been shown to be better than CT or MRI. The sensitivity of octreotide acetate scintigraphy for detecting occult tumors that secrete ACTH ranges from 30% to 53%. This may be due to their small size or also to inadequate expression of somatostatin receptors that are down-regulated by the ambient hypercortisolism. Some patients with pituitary

ACTH-secreting tumors may have a late remission of their hypercortisolism and conservative management (Answer B) may be reasonable in some cases. However, this young man is desperately ill. Cushing syndrome of this magnitude should be considered a medical emergency. Patients must be promptly referred to regional medical centers that have expertise in the rapid diagnosis and management of patients with life-threatening cortisol excess. These patients are at extremely high risk for venous thromboembolism and serious opportunistic infections. A prompt differential diagnosis must be established followed by medical or surgical therapy to rapidly control the hypercortisolism.

High-dose dexamethasone suppression testing (Answer D) really provides no added value in the differential diagnosis of ACTH-dependent Cushing syndrome and it would frankly be dangerous in this young man.

Medullary thyroid carcinoma is a neuroendocrine tumor that has been reported.

<div style="text-align: center;"><img src="imgs/img_in_image_box_26_420_556_793.jpg" alt="Image" width="45%" /></div>


syndrome, but this is a late event in the progression of this cancer and has never been reported to be the cause of occult ectopic ACTH syndrome. Therefore, measuring serum calcitonin (Answer E) would not be useful.

Finally, if you have never missed the diagnosis of Cushing syndrome or been humbled by trying to establish its cause, you should refer all your patients with suspected hypercortisolism to someone who has. Cushing syndrome is hard to diagnose, and, often, unraveling a cause is even more difficult. Ask for help.

This young man underwent a CT scan of his chest and abdomen (see image). A large 7-cm anterior mediastinal mass (arrow) was found. He underwent a sternotomy and removal of a large thymic neuroendocrine carcinoma with 13/15 lymph nodes positive for metastatic disease.

##### Educational Objective:

Diagnose ectopic ACTH-dependent Cushing syndrome in patients with prodigious hypercortisolism and hypokalemia.

UpToDate Topic Review(s): Establishing's the diagnosis of Cushing's syndrome

##### Reference(s):

Javorsky BR, Carroll TB, Findling JW. Differential diagnosis of Cushing's syndrome. In: Swearingen B, Biller BMK, eds. Cushing's Disease. Springer, New York: 2011:85-1066.

Ilias I, Torpy DJ, Pacak K, Mullen N, Wesley RA, Nieman LK. Cushing's syndrome due to ectopic corticotropin secretion: twenty years' experience at the National Institutes of Health. J Clin Endocrinol Metab. 2005;90(8):4955-4962.

## 105 ANSWER: E) Reassure the patient; no further evaluation is necessary

This patient's MRI demonstrates an empty sella. Empty sella is a radiographic diagnosis in which the subarachnoid space extends within the sella, such that cerebrospinal fluid is seen within the sella and the pituitary tissue is often flattened. It is a radiologic description and not a clinical diagnosis. In many cases, the empty sella appearance develops after known pituitary disease has enlarged the sella and/or altered the size and position of the pituitary gland. This is termed secondary empty sella and in adults it has been seen in association with a pituitary adenoma (including spontaneous infarction or surgical, medical, or radiation therapy for the adenoma). In addition, other pathologic processes involving the sellar region, such as Sheehan syndrome, Langerhans cell histiocytosis, spontaneous enlargement and rupture of a cyst, or granulomatous diseases affecting the pituitary, have been associated with empty sella. Empty sella not associated with one of the listed etiologies is termed primary empty sella. In adults, primary empty sella has been associated with congenital deficiency of the sellar diaphragm and benign intracranial hypertension (pseudotumor cerebri). Other risk factors for the development of an empty sella are pregnancy, obesity, and a previous autoimmune process involving the pituitary gland. Some patients with what appears to be primary empty sella may have had a pituitary disease that went undiagnosed.

Finding an empty sella on MRI is common and occurs more often in female patients. Some have suggested a relationship to previous pregnancies, with the enlarging and then shrinking of the pituitary in the postpartum period predisposing to the herniation of the sellar diaphragm into the sella and cerebrospinal fluid.

Headaches and a variety of vision disturbances have been reported in about one-third of patients with empty sella. Although previously considered very unlikely, hypopituitarism has more recently been reported in up to 40% to 50% of patients with empty sella. Hyperprolactinemia seems to be the most commonly associated endocrine abnormality. Patients with an empty sella observed on imaging should have basic testing of pituitary function, including measurement of prolactin, thyroid hormone, and morning cortisol. The chance of an endocrine abnormality is less likely in patients without clinical signs or symptoms in whom the empty sella was noted incidentally on a scan done for another reason. The need for any additional testing for hypopituitarism should be directed on the basis of the history and examination findings. Therefore, the chance of hypopituitarism in this patient is low and she should be reassured; no further evaluation is indicated (Answer E). More testing might be necessary in a different clinical scenario if a patient with empty sella had a history of a pituitary tumor or other sella lesion that may have secondarily led to the empty sella.

Given that the patient in this vignette has no clinical evidence of hormone hypersecretion and no visible tumor on MRI, there is no indication to assess her for a hormone-secreting tumor. Because she is 54 years old, whether or not her gonadotropins are elevated is not clinically relevant (thus, Answer A is incorrect). There is no evidence that incidentally noted empty sella on MRI progresses over time. Therefore, following this radiographic abnormality with serial imaging studies (Answer B) is unnecessary. This patient has hyperprolactinemia, which could be associated with the empty sella, but also could be due to paroxetine therapy. There is no need to evaluate her further for a prolactinoma because no lesion is visible. It is unlikely that the hook effect is occurring, and measuring her prolactin concentration with serial dilutions (Answer C) is incorrect. Visual field testing (Answer D) is not clinically indicated because there is no mass in her sella.

##### Educational Objective:

Explain the clinical significance of an empty sella seen on MRI and recommend appropriate management.

UpToDate Topic Review(s):

Causes of hypopituitarism

##### Reference(s):

De Marinis L, Bonadonna S, Bianchi A, Maira G, Giustina A. Primary empty sella. J Clin Endocrinol Metab. 90(9):5471-5477.

Naing S, Frohman LA. The empty sella. Pediatr Endocrinol Rev. 2007;4(4):335-342.

## 106 ANSWER: B) 75-g oral glucose tolerance test Diseases

100 Diseases involving the pancreas affect insulin production from this gland and thereby cause diabetes mellitus. Cystic fibrosis-related diabetes (CFRD) is unique in that in addition to losing insulin-secreting  $ \beta $ cells, there is also loss of  $ \alpha $ cells, which secrete glucagon. Such patients are therefore at an increased risk for both spontaneous and treatment-induced hypoglycemia.

Cystic fibrosis occurs in approximately 1 in 2500 live births, and it is the most common autosomal recessive condition in white populations. Treatment advances have significantly improved patient survival, with a median survival age of 40 years for babies born in the 1990s. CFRD is a unique disease that has overlapping features with type 1 and type 2 diabetes. Affected patients typically develop this condition after the onset of exocrine pancreatic insufficiency. In the initial stages, patients are able to maintain normal glucose tolerance because of a combination of increased hepatic glucose production and increased peripheral glucose use. Subsequently, patients develop impaired glucose tolerance and diabetes associated with decreased hepatic insulin sensitivity and reduced insulin production. The occurrence of CFRD increases by 4% to 9% annually as a person with cystic fibrosis ages. The prevalence is approximately 9% at age 10 years and 43% at age 30 years and older. A decline in nutritional status and pulmonary function can occur in patients 2 to 4 years before CFRD is diagnosed, so timely diagnosis and initiation of insulin therapy are pivotal in maintaining health. Because the onset of CFRD can be insidious, annual screening with standard 75-g oral glucose tolerance testing (Answer B) is recommended in patients with cystic fibrosis beginning at age 10 years. Standard oral glucose tolerance testing thresholds are used to diagnose both impaired glucose tolerance

and diabetes in this population. Timely diagnosis and initiation of insulin therapy improves nutritional status and may help maintain pulmonary health in patients with this challenging type of diabetes.

Type 1 diabetes is associated with the (Answer E) and this diagnosis is very unlikely in this patient. Studies have shown that continuous glucose monitoring (Answer D) may be a useful tool for early detection of hyperglycemia in patients with cystic fibrosis. However, it has not been proven to be a diagnostic tool in this setting. Hemoglobin  $ A_{1c} $ measurement (Answer A) and fructosamine measurement (Answer C) provide data about average blood glucose values over the previous 3 months and the previous 3 weeks, respectively. Accuracy of these test results can be affected by anemia, decreased albumin levels, acute illness, and intermittent steroid use, which may all occur frequently in patients with cystic fibrosis. For these reasons, these tests cannot be used to diagnose CFRD.

#### Educational Objective:

Select the best test to diagnose cystic fibrosis-related diabetes and describe the pathophysiology of this condition.

UpToDate Topic Review(s):

Cystic fibrosis: overview of gastrointestinal disease

#### Reference(s):

Moran A, Dunitz J, Nathan B, Saeed A, Holme B, Thomas W. Cystic fibrosis-related diabetes: current trends in prevalence, incidence, and mortality. Diabetes Care. 2009;32(9):1626-1631.

Moran A, Brunzell C, Cohen RC, et al; CFRD Guidelines Committee. Clinical care guidelines for cystic fibrosis-related diabetes: a position statement of the American Diabetes Association and a clinical practice guideline of the Cystic Fibrosis Foundation, endorsed by the Pediatric Endocrine Society. Diabetes Care. 2010;33(12):2697-2708.

Jefferies C, Solomon M, Perlman K, Sweezey N, Daneman D. Continuous glucose monitoring in adolescents with cystic fibrosis. J Pediatr. 2005;147(3):396-398.

O'Riordan SM, Hindmarsh P, Hill NR, et al. Validation of continuous glucose monitoring in children and adolescents with cystic fibrosis: a prospective cohort study. Diabetes Care. 2009;32(6):1020-1022.

107 ANSWER: A) Referral to a clinical research study that performs type 1 diabetes screening. This young woman has a sibling who has recently received a diagnosis of type 1 diabetes; thus, she is understandably concerned regarding her own likelihood of developing diabetes. In general, type 1 diabetes is diagnosed when patients present with acute symptoms of hyperglycemia and possibly diabetic ketoacidosis. There is much interest in predicting which individuals will develop type 1 diabetes with the aim of more closely monitoring at-risk patients and perhaps intervening to prevent or delay disease. However, clear guidelines for the screening of at-risk but asymptomatic individuals in routine clinical practice are not yet established. Because this patient has a nontwin sibling with a diagnosis of type 1 diabetes, her personal risk of developing the same condition is estimated to be approximately 10%.

The American Diabetes Association recommends that individuals at higher risk, including relatives of patients with type 1 diabetes, be referred for antibody testing and risk assessment in the context of a clinical research study (Answer A). This coordinated screening may enhance understanding of individual risk, provide education regarding diabetes symptoms, and permit close follow-up with perhaps an earlier diabetes diagnosis in those who do progress. Given the potential benefits of trial participation and the absence of firm guidelines for screening in clinical practice, enrollment in a clinical trial would be the preferred option for this young woman. Additional information regarding participation in such trials is available at www.diabetestrialnet.org.

Measurement of hemoglobin  $ A_{1c} $ (Answer B), although a reasonable screening test for type 2 diabetes, is not appropriate method to screen for type 1 diabetes. Promisingly, studies of individuals at high risk do suggest that type 1 diabetes may be predicted on the basis of a variety of parameters. In the Diabetes Prevention Trial of type 1 Diabetes (DPT-1) (a study of islet-cell autoantibody-positive relatives of patients with type 1 diabetes), use a risk score (DPTRS) derived from parameters including BMI, age, fasting C-peptide level, and postchallenge glucose and C-peptide levels appeared to predict type 1 diabetes in that population. However, measurement of its patient's fasting glucose, insulin, and C-peptide levels in the clinical setting (Answer C) is unlikely to be similarly meaningful. In the TrialNet Natural History Study of antibody-positive relatives of individuals with type 1 diabetes, generation of a risk score incorporating levels of multiple islet antibodies has shown promise in predicting diabetes risk. However, there are not yet clear guidelines for the screening of asymptomatic individuals via

autoantibody testing in clinical practice. Thus, in-clinic screening for either the antibody combinations of islet-cell cytoplasmic autoantibodies and glutamic acid decarboxylase autoantibodies (Answer D) or insulinoma-2-associated autoantibodies and zinc transporter 8 autoantibodies (Answer E) would not be the preferred next steps. Continued engagement of high-risk individuals in clinical trials is the best means of refining clinical care in the future.

##### Educational Objective:

Provide guideline-based screening recommendations to patients at risk for type 1 diabetes mellitus.

UpToDate Topic Review(s):

Overview of medical care in adults with diabetes mellitus

##### Reference(s):

American Diabetes Association. Standards of medical care in diabetes--2014. Diabetes Care. 2014;37(Suppl 1):S14-S80.

Sosenko JM, Krischer JP, Palmer JP, et al; Diabetes Prevention Trial-Type 1 Study Group. A risk score for type 1 diabetes derived from autoantibody-positive participants in the diabetes prevention trial-type 1. Diabetes Care. 2008;31(3):528-533.

Sosenko JM, Skyler JS, Palmer JP, et al; Type 1 Diabetes TrialNet Study Group; Diabetes Prevention Trial-Type 1 Study Group. The prediction of type 1 diabetes by multiple autoantibody levels and their incorporation into an autoantibody risk score in relatives of type 1 diabetic patients. Diabetes Care. 2013;36(9):2615-2620.

From: compoundF@zonafasciculata.com

Subject: Re: interesting patient question

Dear Endo Doc,

Thanks for asking me about your patient. I appreciate your confidence. The presence of a very low serum cortisol and plasma ACTH suggest that the patient has secondary endogenous adrenal insufficiency. The elevated plasma renin activity and low aldosterone can be attributed to the use of an ACE inhibitor. In the absence of any evidence of primary adrenal failure, there is no need for adrenal CT (Answer B). Since glucocorticoids such as cortisol exert negative feedback on vasopressin, hyponatremia is common in patients with secondary adrenal insufficiency. Your patient has clinical features consistent with excessive glucocorticoid exposure with facial rounding, increased dorsocervical fat, accelerated hypertension, and cutaneous wasting. In the presence of these findings, the low ACTH and cortisol are probably caused by suppression of hypothalamic corticotropin-releasing hormone and pituitary ACTH by exogenously administered glucocorticoids. Even with periodic or intermittent Cushing disease, the serum cortisol levels and ACTH levels are not fully suppressed during the “nonCushing” part of the cycle. The normal brain MRI certainly excludes any clinically significant pituitary lesion that might cause pituitary hypoadrenalism, and a dedicated pituitary MRI (Answer C) would not be helpful in this patient.

Exogenous corticosteroid exposure is the only cause of both Cushing syndrome and secondary endogenous adrenal hypofunction. Unfortunately, many patients receiving steroid injections have no idea that they are receiving corticosteroids that may affect their metabolic milieu and physiology. For example, epidural steroid administration (especially when multiple injections are given over a few weeks) suppresses the hypothalamic-pituitary-adrenal axis and occasionally causes signs and symptoms of Cushing syndrome. Intra-articular steroids may have a similar fate and plasma levels of these exogenously administered steroids may be detectable in the plasma for many weeks after administration, as they are slowly absorbed from a closed space. Factualious Cushing syndrome is a rare disorder that implies surreptitious intake of a glucocorticoid. However, occasionally patients may purchase “vitamins” from outside the United States for arthritis and other ailments. Some of these preparations contain large amounts of corticosteroids—initially the patient feels much better, but after a few months, the patient develops Cushing syndrome.

A synthetic screen for glucocorticoids (Answer D) is available. Using liquid chromatography-tandem mass spectrometry, many commonly administered steroids can be quantified. I am certain this would provide you with the reason your patient has low cortisol and ACTH levels.

Unlike the precursor for aldosterone (11-deoxycorticosterone) that has agonist activity at the mineralocorticoid receptor, 11-deoxycortisol (precursor for cortisol) (Answer E) has no agonist activity at the glucocorticoid receptor. Although a mutation in the CYP11B1 gene (11β-hydroxylase) causes the second most common type of congenital adrenal hyperplasia, you would expect this disorder to present with ambiguous genitalia in female patients (penile enlargement in boys) with hypokalemia and hypertension developing later. However, a late-onset form of congenital adrenal hyperplasia is very rare. Of course, in any form of congenital adrenal hyperplasia, you would not expect a suppressed plasma ACTH and clinical Cushing syndrome. Carriers of a CYP11B1 mutation have no detectable clinical or biochemical abnormalities. Measurement of serum 11-deoxycortisol would not be useful; it will be undetectable in this woman.

The major binding protein for cortisol also referred to as serpin A6, belongs to the major binding protein for cortisol.

Corticosteroid-binding globulin (CBG, transcortin), also referred to as serpin A6, belongs to the serpin superfamily and it is the major binding protein for cortisol. CBG is a 52-kDa secreted  $ \alpha1 $-glycoprotein consisting of 405 amino acids. It is synthesized and secreted by hepatocytes in the liver and is present in glycocorticoid responsive cells. The concentration of CBG is regulated by estrogens. CBG is the main transport protein for progestins and glucocorticoids in the blood. CBG levels increase in states of increased estrogen secretion or administration and levels may be very low in critically ill patients with hypoproteinemia. Defects in the gene encoding CBG are the cause of CBG deficiency, a rare disorder characterized by reduced CBG production that results in hypotension or hypertension and muscle fatigue. Because you measured a very low salivary cortisol level in the morning (clever idea), this confirms true endogenous cortisol deficiency and excludes a binding protein abnormality. As you know, salivary cortisol is an easily accessible surrogate of “free” biologically active cortisol that represents approximately 5% total cortisol. The concentration of unbound or free cortisol can also be calculated from the concentration of total cortisol and that of CBG on the basis of mass action. Nonetheless, measuring CBG (Answer A) would not be helpful in this patient.

Finally, she does not need to be treated with hydrocortisone replacement since the exogenously administered steroid should provide adequate glucocorticoid support until the hypothalamic-pituitary-adrenal axis recovers. Nonetheless, it might be prudent to use stress steroid dosing for a serious illness or stressful event.

I hope this has been helpful. Please let me know what happens.

Dr. Compound F

109 ANSWER: E) Erythrocytosis

To achieve masculinization of a female-to-male transgender individual, testosterone is administered to target a normal male range of 300 to 1200 ng/dL (10.4 to 41.6 nmol/L), 1 week after testosterone administration if given intramuscularly, but levels less than 700 ng/dL (24.3 nmol/L) are recommended. Transgender individuals should be seen every 3 months during the first year of treatment, then every 6 to 12 months to monitor testosterone levels; the effectiveness of therapy; and potential adverse effects including erythrocytosis, weight gain, salt retention, lipid abnormalities, cystic acne, and psychological changes. The testosterone level in this individual is greater than 700 ng/dL (24.3 nmol/L) the day before administration of the next testosterone dose, suggesting that his testosterone levels are always higher than the recommended range. Testosterone replacement may cause erythrocytosis (Answer E). The transgender man in this vignette is also overweight and has acne, which might improve slightly with a lower testosterone dosage.

Few transgender men can afford to have a hysterectomy, salpingo-oophorectomy, and mastectomy. Therefore, clinical breast examinations, mammograms, and Papanicolaou testing should be performed at recommended intervals as indicated by the individual's risk group. Testosterone levels in the male range often result in amenorrhea. Female-to-male transgender patients are at risk for endometrial cancer based on aromatization of testosterone to estradiol (Answer D), and endometrial hyperplasia was described in 3 of 19 transgender patients treated with androgens. However, the endometrium is typically atrophic with high-dosage testosterone therapy when examined after hysterectomy and no cases of endometrial cancer have been reported. Therefore, the actual risk of endometrial cancer is unknown.

Liver function abnormalities (Answer A) should not increase with intramuscular testosterone administration, although liver function should be evaluated every 6 months to 1 year because transient elevation in liver enzymes is common.

Low bone mineral density (Answer C) is not a concern if testosterone is maintained in the male range.

Although male-to-female transgender individuals on estrogen therapy are at high risk for thromboembolic events (Answer B), female-to-male transgender individuals are not.

##### Educational Objective:

Monitor hormone levels and potential adverse effects of testosterone therapy in female-to-male transgender patients.

Futterweit W, Deligdisch L. Histopathological effects of exogenously administered testosterone in 19 female-to-male transsexuals. J Clin Endocrinol Metab. 1986;62(1):16-21.

## 110 ANSWER: E) Measurement of apolipoprotein B or LDL particle number

Although laboratory and clinical parameter-based assessments of cardiovascular risk have been validated in a wide range of populations, they are not perfect. Individuals who have low predicted risk might still experience a cardiovascular event; conversely, individuals who have high predicted risk may never have an event, even when risk-reducing therapies (such as lipid-lowering therapies) are not used. Thus, there is an ongoing interest in additional means to assess cardiovascular risk, especially in situations such as the one described in this vignette—this patient scores at “intermediate” risk using the Framingham risk calculator.

Apolipoprotein B (Answer E) is the main structural protein for atherogenic lipoproteins (VLDL, IDL, LDL), but it is not present in HDL. There is 1 apolipoprotein B molecule per lipoprotein particle, so apolipoprotein B indicates particle number, whereas the LDL cholesterol indicates the amount of cholesterol found in the LDL fraction. An LDL-cholesterol concentration of 100 mg/dL (2.59 mmol/L) could be due to many smaller particles (with less cholesterol per particle) or fewer larger particles (with proportionately more cholesterol per particle). Because the smaller, denser LDL particles are thought to be more atherogenic than larger, more buoyant particles.

there has been an emerging interest in measuring particle number, and several commercial labs provide this service. The INTERHEART study was a large international study of cardiovascular risk in which apolipoprotein B levels were found to be a significant predictor of myocardial infarction, even after adjusting for age and traditional lipid risk factors. However, other studies have refuted this finding, so it remains controversial. There is potential redundancy because measurement of apolipoprotein B or LDL particle number can be approximated by the use of the non-HDL-cholesterol concentration: if the non-HDL-cholesterol level is more than 30 mg/dL (0.78 mmol/L) above the LDL-cholesterol level, this indicates high particle number. Some guidelines have recommended measurement of apolipoprotein B or LDL particle number, particularly in patients at high risk, to enhance risk assessment and provide apolipoprotein B goals for therapy. Although routine measurement of apolipoprotein B or LDL particle number has not been uniformly adopted, there is some suggestion in the literature that determining these values may be appropriate in individuals at very high risk, in those with hypertriglyceridemia, or in those with genetic dyslipidemias. Although cost-effectiveness for mass screening has not been formally assessed for apolipoprotein B measurement, it is relatively low-cost assay and is well validated in several populations. In this patient with elevated triglycerides, it may more accurately assess risk than measurement of LDL cholesterol or non-HDL cholesterol, and it is the best choice of the options presented here. However, it is important to note that measurement of apolipoprotein B or LDL particle number is not presently part of most guidelines. In fact, recent studies suggest that up to 75% of cholesterol laboratory testing could be avoided in a cost-effective strategy of cardiovascular risk assessment via a multistage screening approach using non-laboratory risk factors such as age, smoking status, blood pressure, and BMI.

The coronary artery calcium (CAC) score (Answer A) is determined by a CT-based scan that detects calcium, which indicates atherosclerosis in asymptomatic individuals. It has been proposed as a tool to detect disease (and thus risk for cardiovascular events) in persons who may be incorrectly assessed as being at low risk by traditional risk factors such as the Framingham score. Meta-analyses of CAC studies have found correlations between CAC scores and cardiac outcomes, with a relative risk of a cardiac event of 4.4 if CAC is present vs absent. As the CAC score increases, so does the risk of cardiac events. Furthermore, the CAC score varies between individuals even if they have the same or similar Framingham risk scores. However, the CAC score itself is not perfect because it only detects calcified plaque, and many dangerous (at risk of rupturing) atherosclerotic plaque lesions are not yet calcified; thus, the CAC score can underestimate disease. Conversely, CAC scores can overestimate risk in calcified low-risk-for-rupturing lesions. Cost-effectiveness analyses of CAC scoring have not supported its use, and there are health concerns with the procedure itself because of radiation exposure. Therefore, CAC scoring is not routinely recommended, nor is it cost-effective. There is no evidence that knowledge of CAC score improves adherence to lifestyle measures.

HDL has been long regarded as the protective lipid particle, and epidemiologic studies demonstrate that a low HDL-cholesterol concentration is associated with increased cardiovascular events. Conversely, higher HDL-cholesterol levels are thought to be protective. However, recent studies attempting to raise HDL cholesterol using fibrates (ACCORD [Action to Control Cardiovascular Risk in Diabetes]) or niacin (AIM-HIGH [Atherosclerosis Intervention in Metabolic Syndrome with Low HDL/High Triglyceride and Impact on Global Health Outcomes]) in statin-treated patients have not shown any additional benefit in cardiovascular outcomes, thus casting doubt on the utility of HDL-cholesterol measurement. There is an emerging interest in assessment of HDL function (Answer B), which includes antioxidant, anti-inflammatory, and reverse cholesterol transport activities. However, to date these assessments are available in research laboratories only, not in clinical practice.

Coronary CT angiography (CCTA) (Answer C) is a newer tool using CT imaging to assess the presence of coronary artery disease. A recent study evaluated outcomes in more than 24,000 patients without known cardiovascular disease and determined that the absence of coronary disease was associated with a favorable prognosis. The study also documented a correlation between the number of coronary vessels with obstructive coronary artery disease and mortality. Thus, CCTA is emerging as a new tool to diagnose coronary artery disease and stratify risk in persons with detected coronary artery disease. Findings from additional studies comparing CCTA with invasive coronary angiography in patients with coronary symptoms have suggested that CCTA may have better utility. At this time, there are no cost-effectiveness data to support CCTA screening in primary prevention.

Lipoprotein(a) (Answer D) is an atherogenic lipoprotein not measured in most routine lipid panels.

to be an independent risk factor for cardiovascular disease, although the relative risk ratio for an elevated

lipoprotein(a) level is modest. The association between lipoprotein(a) and cardiovascular events appears to be

more related to certain subtypes of lipoprotein(a) rather than simply the lipoprotein(a) levels, and to date no evidence has documented that lowering lipoprotein(a) is beneficial (niacin is the only lipid-lowering agent that reduces lipoprotein[a]). Thus, lipoprotein(a) measurement is not recommended for routine screening. Most experts recommend measurement of lipoprotein(a) only if there is a strong family history of premature cardiovascular disease, particularly if there is no obvious dyslipidemia to explain such a family history.

Assessing the costs of various tests is difficult because costs range widely among providers, laboratories, and countries. As rough estimates, a coronary artery calcium scan costs between $150 and $500 (US dollars); contrast-enhanced coronary CT angiography costs between $500 and $1500; measurement of lipoprotein(a) costs between $50 and $250; and measurement of apolipoprotein B or LDL particle number costs between $50 and $200 (the costs for lipoprotein[a] and/or apolipoprotein B or LDL particle number often include the cost of the standard lipid panel).

##### Educational Objective:

Evaluate the utility of tools available to estimate cardiovascular risk beyond life factor— and lipid panel-based risk assessment.

UpToDate Topic Review(s):

Overview of the risk equivalents and established risk factors for cardiovascular disease

##### Reference(s):

Pandya A, Weinstein MC, Salomon JA, Cutler D, Gaziano TA. Who needs laboratories and who needs statins?: comparative and cost-effectiveness analyses of non-laboratory-based, laboratory-based, and staged primary cardiovascular disease screening guidelines. Circ Cardiovasc Qual Outcomes. 2014;7(1):25-32.

Waugh N, Black C, Walker S, McIntyre L, Cummins E, Hillis G. The effectiveness and cost-effectiveness of computed tomography screening for coronary artery disease disease: systematic review. Health Technol Assess. 2006;10(39):iii-iv, ix-x, 1-41.

Min JK, Dunning A, Lin FY, et al; CONFIRM Investigators. Age- and sex-related differences in all-cause mortality risk based on coronary computed tomography angiography findings results from the International Multicenter CONFIRM (Coronary CT Angiography Evaluation for Clinical Outcomes: An International Multicenter Registry) of 23,854 patients without known coronary artery disease. J Am Coll Cardiol. 2011;58(8):849-860.

Mangalmuri SS, Davidson MH. The incremental value of lipids and inflammatory biomarkers in determining residual cardiovascular risk. Curr Atheroscler Rep. 2011;13(5):373-380.

Sniderman AD, Williams K, Contois JH, Monroe HM, McQueen MJ, de Graaf J, Furberg CD. A meta-analysis of low-density lipoprotein cholesterol, non-density lipoprotein cholesterol, and apolipoprotein B as markers of cardiovascular risk. Circ Cardiovasc Qual Outcomes. 2011;4(3):337-45.

## 111 ANSWER: E) Discontinue risedronate and start denosumab

Bisphosphonates are poorly absorbed orally, with less than 1% absorbed in the gastrointestinal tract. In addition, gastrointestinal adverse effects, including gastritis and esophagitis are relatively common. Often, these symptoms resolve within a few weeks or within a few doses. However, an alternative medication should be prescribed when gastrointestinal symptoms persist. Therefore, continuing the current medication (Answer A) is incorrect. Prescribing another oral bisphosphonate (Answer C) with a similar adverse effect profile when the patient has developed gastrointestinal adverse effects is not a tenable long-term solution. Moreover, ibandronate does not offer fracture protection at the hip. In this patient, intravenous zoledronic acid (Answer D) is desirable because in a trial of zoledronic acid for secondary hip fracture prevention, a mortality benefit was documented and the intravenous administration of the agent solves the issue of gastrointestinal intolerance; however, this patient's reduced renal function is a contraindication to use of zoledronic acid.

Given his history of hip fracture and low bone density in the osteoporotic range, he should receive treatment. Alternative therapies include calcitonin, teriparatide, or denosumab. In this patient, replacing risedronate with calcitonin (Answer B), which does not offer fracture protection at the hip, is not a good choice. Teriparatide (not an option offered in this vignette) is indicated to increase bone mass in men with primary or hypogonadal osteoporosis at high risk for fracture. Thus, it would be an option for this man. Denosumab (Answer E) given by injection once every 6 months, reduces the risk of fracture at both the hip and spine, as demonstrated in the FREEDOM trial. Because it is given by subcutaneous injection, it avoids the gastrointestinal adverse effects common to bisphosphonates and it can be used in the setting of chronic kidney disease.

### Educational Objective:

Educational Objectives: Prioritize the choice of osteoporosis therapy on the basis of patient characteristics and contraindications.

ToDate Topic Review(s):

Treatment of osteoporosis in men

#### Reference(s):

Neer RM, Arnaud CD, Zanchetta JR, et al. Effect of parathyroid hormone (1-34) on fractures and bone mineral density in postmenopausal women with osteoporosis. N Engl J Med. 2001;344(19):1434-1441.

Cummings SR, San Martin J, McClung MR, et al; FREEDOM Trial. Denosumab for prevention of fractures in postmenopausal women with osteoporosis published correction in N Engl J Med. 2009;361(19):1914]. N Engl J Med. 2009;361(8):756-765.
